# What the River Returns

# Chapter 1

By 7:18 a.m., the tin siding of Voss Cotton Gin had started to ring with heat. The sound came in thin pops, like knuckles cracking in a quiet room. Lena Waller ducked under the yellow tape at the office door and the air changed from raw sun to bleach, metal, and blood already turning sweet.

Hank Voss lay on his back between the desk and the filing cabinets, shirt gone dark from sternum to waistband. A deep slash opened his throat, cartilage flashing white through the red, but the heaviest soak spread lower at the center of his chest. His right hand was skinned at the knuckles, and one fingernail had torn loose down to meat. He had fought for something, then lost to it. Which injury had actually killed him was an autopsy question, not a scene-floor guess.

Beau Broussard stood by the door with his scene log in one hand and a roll of antacid tablets in the other, shaking two into his palm without looking. He had the weathered, wide face of a man who had spent too many years squinting off boat ramps, and this morning he had a crescent of zinc sunscreen across his nose where he had missed a spot with his thumb.

"Perimeter's holding on the office side," he said. "Loading bay's ugly. We had six workers try to come back in for their lunch pails before I got a second deputy over there."

Lena knelt by Voss's left shoulder. Her right knee scar, old surgery from a blown ligament and a younger version of herself that thought she healed fast, gave a quick sting against the concrete. She ignored it and leaned in close enough to smell copper and old coffee on the dead man.

"Any shell casings?" she asked.

"None. No obvious weapon in the room. Front safe's closed. Cash drawer's open, but there are twenties still in it."

"Signs of forced entry?"

"Nope. Back office door was unlocked when first shift came in."

She took that in, then stood and looked where Beau was not looking: the second body, laid three feet from Hank as if someone had measured the gap.

The second man was Black, lean, and half-rigid with an unnatural waxy sheen over skin that should not still have held to the face this long after death. The neck showed an old deep ligature groove under new staining. Mud clung to one heel. The shirt was a plain white button-down, clean except for two rust-colored handprints at the chest, like the person who placed him there had paused to adjust him.

Lena felt, stupidly, the urge to apologize to him.

She moved closer and saw the chipped left incisor. The break was not fresh. It had healed years before whoever killed him. She had seen that tooth in a photograph that had lived in church binders and memorial programs since she was a teenager. She straightened, pulse jumping once, hard.

"Who called it in?" she asked.

"Darlene McKee. Payroll clerk." Beau tipped his chin toward the doorway. "She's outside with EMS. Couldn't keep her from puking."

"Bring her in when she's steady."

He gave her one look. "Len. You thinking what I'm thinking?"

She did not answer him yet.

Outside, cicadas drilled from the tree line past the gravel lot. A drone of voices had formed behind the tape, workers in sweat-dark shirts and steel-toe boots trying to see around deputies. Beyond them, two local TV vans were already wedging into the shoulder. Someone had called the media before the blood cooled.

Sheriff Clay Reddick arrived in a white Tahoe that still carried campaign magnets from spring primaries. He came through the tape with his tie straight, his heavy shoulders set, and a pair of reading glasses tucked into the front pocket of his pressed shirt. The glasses had a cracked temple wrapped in clear tape. He kept using them anyway.

"Detective Waller," he said, voice warm enough for church foyer conversation. "Tell me what I can stand in front of."

"You can stand in front of a locked scene and say we're processing," Lena said. "Anything beyond that is guessing."

Clay glanced at Voss, then at the second body, and the warmth in his face thinned. "Hank Voss is our victim. The other one is a prop until I hear different from the lab. We do not reward whoever staged this with a circus."

"He's not a prop." Lena kept her tone flat. "He's a body."

"You know what I mean. We can call this a homicide with postmortem contamination and keep public order intact."

The phrase public order landed in her ear the way other men said grace.

Lena crouched again near the second body and forced herself to track facts instead of fear. The fingertips were stained dark, not in a lividity pattern. The shirt cuffs were buttoned wrong, left through right hole. The soil under the nails was fine and pale, cemetery clay, not the black grit from the gin lot. Whoever moved him had done it recently and with refrigeration or chemicals, because the Delta heat should have bloated and broken this tissue already.

She stood and pulled her phone. "Dispatch, this is Waller. I need county archive clerk on line now."

Clay watched her with a small patient smile that meant irritation. "Lena, don't start naming history in front of my deputies."

"I'm not naming anything. I'm verifying identity."

He inhaled slowly. "We're not turning this into 1963 before breakfast."

"Then don't." She kept her eyes on her phone. "Let me do my job."

The archive clerk, Nita Fellows, answered on the third ring, breathless and chewing something crunchy. "Records."

"Nita, I need the 1962-63 Mercer Colored School yearbook page for Isaiah Bell. High-res scan. Text it to me and Beau right now."

There was a silence, then the crackle of a desk chair. "That's from the memorial litigation packet, right? I can pull it. Give me three minutes."

"Two if you love me."

"I don't love nobody before coffee, Detective. Hang tight."

Lena ended the call and went back inside. Dr. Naveen Rao was at the doorway now, tying on gloves, his voice already moving ahead of him.

"I can do field observations," he said. "I can't certify much in this heat before transfer. Whoever set your second decedent here changed decomposition trajectory somehow. Could be cold storage. Could be surface preservative. Could be both."

Rao wore clean scrubs under an old denim overshirt and a wristwatch with a busted strap held on by black thread. He refused to replace it because it had been his father's. Lena knew that because he had once told her at two in the morning while they waited for tox screens and stale vending machine coffee.

"What can you tell me now?" she asked.

He crouched at the second body, careful and close. "There are older injuries consistent with hanging trauma at the neck and hyoid region, at least from external exam. There are newer transfer artifacts on the shirt and forearms. This body did not lie here all night."

Clay snorted behind them, a short sound with no humor in it. "You can tell all that in five minutes?"

Rao did not look up. "I can tell enough to stop you from making statements you can't retract."

Beau's phone buzzed. Then Lena's.

Nita's text came through with a scanned yearbook portrait: Isaiah Bell, senior class, 1963. The face was narrow. The left incisor was chipped. The ear was bent slightly at the top like it had healed from a hard hit in childhood. Beneath the photo, the quote line from school editors read clean and banal and unbearable.

Lena held the phone beside the dead man's face. The same tooth. The same ear.

The world did not tilt. It narrowed.

She turned the screen to Clay. "It's Isaiah Bell."

He looked, jaw locking once, then handed the phone back like it was wet. "It's a resemblance."

"It's enough for provisional ID pending prints and dental."

"Prints from 1963?"

"We have church burial records and a county death certificate from June 1963. We have family to notify if this confirms. We treat him as identified until disproved."

Clay's voice softened again, that false courtesy she had learned to hear as pressure. "Lena, listen to me carefully. If that name gets out before we control narrative, this county catches fire. You know that."

She did know. She could already picture New Hope Missionary's bulletin board by noon, Ms. Ida reading a screenshot that said, PUT THAT BELL BOY BACK IN THE DITCH. She could picture Hollis's friends piling on underneath: String the next one who says lynching and film it. She could picture donors calling Clay, and Clay calling everyone else.

She also knew what happened when scenes got politically expensive: bags opened, logs rewritten, timestamps smoothed until a body turned into paperwork.

"Then we lock it down now," she said. "Both decedents, full trace collection, independent transport witness, and immediate state-lab intake."

"State lab is twenty-four hours out."

"Not if you mark emergency historical-custody risk."

Clay stared at her for a beat too long. "You are jumping levels."

"I'm preserving evidence."

He pulled a folded handkerchief from his pocket and wiped the back of his neck. The sun was climbing hard, and his face had gone shiny at the temples. "Fine. You get your transfer request. But this stays internal until I brief city hall."

"I need that in writing."

"You have my word."

"I need it in writing."

Beau made a small sound in his throat that might have been a cough and might have been a warning.

Clay folded the handkerchief into a neat square on Voss's desk and nudged it flush with the edge before he spoke. "Draft it and bring it to my vehicle."

Lena nodded, because nodding cost less than speaking right then.

Darlene McKee came in fifteen minutes later with a deputy escort. She was in lemon-print house slippers, the kind with foam soles, because she had come to run payroll and not to stand in blood. Mascara had dried in streaks under both eyes. She kept touching the gold cross at her throat until her knuckles whitened.

"I opened at six-ten," she said, voice shaking. "Mr. Voss liked me early on Mondays because the truckers came with invoices. I walked in and saw his shoe first. Then I saw the rest and I screamed till my chest hurt."

"Did you touch anything?" Lena asked.

"No, ma'am. I dropped my keys. I still left them right there by the copier."

"Anybody else had access overnight?"

Darlene swallowed. "Everybody's got a key who matters. That's what Mr. Voss used to say."

"Name them."

"Him. Me. Floor manager. His nephew Trey. Two maintenance men. Sheriff's office, one copy for alarms."

Lena looked up. "Sheriff's office?"

Clay answered for Darlene. "Standard alarm protocol for commercial properties. Not unusual."

It might have been standard protocol, and it might not have been. Lena wrote it down.

By 10:05 the first evidence coolers were sealed. Lena watched each strip of tamper tape go down with date and initials. She signed chain-of-custody twice, then made Beau countersign. She put Rao's transfer notes in a separate pouch and stapled them herself.

When the county van rolled out toward Jackson with deputy escort, camera crews were already at the gate filming through heat shimmer. A man in a union shirt shouted at the lens that Voss had stolen years from every worker on the line. From behind the barricade, a white man in a seed-company cap jabbed a finger at Terrence Cole, one of Voss's Black forklift hands, and yelled, "Say Isaiah Bell on TV and we'll hang you beside him." Terrence flinched so hard he dropped his water bottle.

Lena stood in the grit and sweat and diesel smell and watched the van until it took the curve toward the highway and disappeared.

Then she turned to canvass the people who were still alive.

At noon, the break room fans were pushing hot air in circles and losing. Lena set up at a scarred laminate table with Beau beside her and a digital recorder between them. Every worker she called in had the same line ready: they hadn't heard anything, hadn't seen anything, had arrived at six, had found chaos, had waited outside.

The answers were too clean and too matching.

The fourth interview was Alonzo Pierce, union steward, forearms roped with old burns from machine jams. He set his cap on the table and lined it up with the grain in the laminate before he sat.

"You want truth?" he said. "Truth is folks are scared of losing jobs and scared of getting blamed for this. Those are two different fears, but they feel the same in your gut."

"Who told them what to say?" Lena asked.

Alonzo rubbed his jaw. "Nobody had to. We all know how this goes. First headline says worker rage, second headline says lock everybody up, third headline says management was a saint and we are all animals."

Beau leaned forward, elbows on knees. "You got anything concrete, Lonzo? I can fight rumor all day, but it won't hold up in court."

"Concrete?" Alonzo bared his teeth without warmth. "Concrete is your office had an alarm key in this building. Concrete is cameras in dock three were dead since Friday and nobody fixed them. Concrete is Mr. Voss told us payroll would be late again while he ordered a new bass boat."

"That last one isn't homicide evidence," Lena said.

"No, ma'am. That's character evidence."

She wrote it anyway.

The ninth interview was the one that shifted the floor.

Calvin Rusk, senior mechanic, came in with his hearing protectors hanging around his neck and a paper sack of pecans he cracked one-handed when he got nervous. He wore a stitched patch on his shirt that read RUSK but the R had been repaired twice with pale thread. He sat down, took a pecan from the sack, and did not eat it.

"Mr. Rusk," Lena said, "you were on overnight maintenance last night?"

"I was here till four-thirty. Dryer line three kept spiking temp, and if that line catches, we all cook."

"Did you leave straight from here?"

"I did."

"Did you see anybody at the office before you left?"

He stared at the recorder. "I already told Deputy Kane I didn't see nothing useful."

"Tell me again."

He rolled the pecan between thumb and forefinger until shell dust coated his nail beds. "I got a daughter at Delta State," he said quietly. "She starts sophomore year next week. I can't lose this job because I said the wrong name in the wrong room."

Lena nodded once. "I'm not asking you for gossip. I'm asking what your eyes saw."

"My eyes saw a county refrigerated van by dock two at about four-twelve."

Beau straightened. "County how?"

"Gray stripe down the side, faded seal on the door. Same van your office sent last winter when that inmate died in lockup and y'all needed extra cooling because morgue compressors were down. I know that squeal in the belt. I replaced that belt for Fleet in January."

Lena felt the back of her neck go cold despite the heat. "Who was driving?"

"I couldn't see his full face. White man, deputy-style haircut, baseball cap, long sleeves even in this weather." Calvin cracked the pecan in his fist by accident and winced. "He came out the office side with two others. They loaded something long under a tarp. Then they left by the east gate, no lights."

"Did you report this to anybody last night?"

"No, ma'am. I figured maybe Mr. Voss had legal disposal, maybe spoiled lint bales, maybe something above my pay grade. Then I came in this morning and saw tape everywhere and I thought I might puke."

"Why tell us now?"

Calvin looked up for the first time. His eyes were wet and angry. "Because if this turns into y'all saying one of us workers butchered a boss in his office and dragged some dead Black man in there for fun, I'm not carrying that lie for the rest of my life."

Silence held for a second. The fan rattled overhead.

Beau clicked his pen and wrote the statement header. "We'll take this signed and witnessed."

Calvin scrubbed shell dust off his palm and let out a thin breath. "Can you keep my name out of the six o'clock?"

Lena answered before Beau could. "I can flag you as confidential source in initial report and move your full statement under protected annex until probable-cause filing. I can't promise nobody ever says your name. I can promise we fight for it."

"That's the best offer I've heard all day," Calvin said.

Word spread through the break room in under ten minutes even though no one had heard the actual statement. Fear moved faster than facts. Terrence Cole stayed by the vending machines with his hard hat clamped to his ribs, eyes fixed on the door.

Lena caught him before he could leave. "Terrence, I need a formal statement about dock three and the gate."

He shook his head hard. "No, ma'am. Not while that bastard outside is calling me by name and talking about hanging folks."

"Point him out."

"Seed-cap by the Channel Seven truck. Deputy Hollis heard him and kept walking." Terrence swallowed. "I'll talk in Jackson before I talk in this building."

Lena turned to Beau. "Shut down open-floor interviews. Move the rest to courthouse annex under escort. No county deputy rides alone with witnesses."

Beau was already on his radio. "Copy. Kane, lock this hall. I need two state troopers for witness transport now."

By three-thirty, Lena had nineteen interviews, three walkouts, and an emergency transfer roster that had blown her schedule apart but kept Terrence and two others talking. Her shirt collar had dried stiff with sweat and bleach and rasped her neck every time she turned. She also had three missed calls from Clay and a text from county communications asking for "approved language" before afternoon press.

She left both unanswered until the annex intake sheet was signed. She could fix witness routing and custody gaps tonight; she couldn't fix anybody's spin.

At 8:47 p.m., the sheriff's briefing room smelled of old paper, stale coffee, and the sharp lemon cleaner the night janitor used when she was angry at people who left sugar rings on furniture. Fluorescent lights flickered just enough to tighten everybody's shoulders.

Clay sat at the head of the table with a legal pad and no jacket, tie loosened but still knotted. County PIO Cora Denning sat to his right with perfect hair that had survived twelve hours of humidity and a notebook full of highlighted talking points. Beau was at Lena's left, rubbing a thumb over the dent in his wedding band.

Clay opened with a practiced, level cadence.

"Tomorrow we're in one lane. Voss is confirmed. Second body stays under forensic confirmation until command signs off. No freelancing, no side briefings, no history sermons on camera."

The cadence dragged up an old press conference from her patrol years, when "contained incident" meant three families were still waiting for body IDs.

Cora kept writing. "I can feed press three clean lines: active investigation, no confirmed motive, tip line open. If they push Bell on camera--"

"They'll push it," Lena said.

"--I'll say dental and prints are pending."

Lena waited until Clay looked at her. "No."

The room went still.

Pressure pinched behind Lena's eyes, the same warning throb she got before a witness recanted on tape.

Clay folded his hands. "Then give me better language, Detective."

"Call him provisionally identified. We've got archival match, morphology, and burial-record overlap. If we bury that tonight and it leaks at sunrise, defense will argue we started scrubbing before day two."

Cora looked up. "That one word puts crowds on courthouse steps before dawn. I've got twelve deputies for patrol and two are already on witness transport. One bad chyron and we're pulling patrol off intersections to guard judges' chambers and the Bell cemetery road."

"Crowds come either way," Lena said. "If we dodge the name, they'll show up convinced we buried it again."

Clay kept his voice mild. "This isn't a seminar. It's keeping deputies on corners, keeping courthouse doors from getting kicked in, and keeping this file admissible."

Beau exhaled like a man watching a bridge cable fray. "We'll split hairs without lying. Say historical identity's under active forensic confirmation till dental lands."

She let him carry that sentence, weighing whether Clay wanted compromise or a clean insubordination write-up.

"Fine," Lena said. "Then state lab gets all trace tonight. Not just tissue. Fibers, latent lift sheets, soil, transfer prints, everything in the chain."

She counted what she still had leverage on: the transfer request already logged, Beau in the room, and a witness queue too public to bury clean.

Clay tapped his pen once. "County lab can photograph latent lifts, seal the intake fridge, and run first pass before sunrise."

"County lab shares a hallway with the records room where movement sheets get corrected in pencil." Her voice dropped, harder now. "I'm not accusing techs. I'm saying the opening for contamination is obvious. Keep it local and defense guts us."

Cora capped her pen. "If we call state this fast--"

"We already did," Lena said.

"--every camera says we've lost our own house."

Lena pictured tomorrow's steps anyway: church mothers with folding fans, seed hats at the curb, both sides sure the sheriff's office was lying.

"For this case, we did," Lena said. "People were filming our tape line by nine and threatening witnesses by noon."

Clay's jaw worked. He leaned back and gave her that falsely gentle tone again.

"Lena, you've had a long day. I respect the work. I also have to keep this county from tearing itself open on live TV."

Her knee scar throbbed under the table, hot and precise. She thought of the handprints on Isaiah's shirt, the way they had looked like a person arranging furniture for company.

"Then manage this," she said. "I'm requesting in writing: state-lab priority, dual-chain witness on every transfer, and immediate lock audit on all county refrigerated vehicles with movement logs from midnight to six."

Beau let out a low whistle that he turned into a cough.

Clay stared at her for three long seconds. Then he smiled without mirth.

"You are very comfortable issuing requests in this room."

"I'm comfortable documenting risk."

"Then let's--" Beau started.

"No." Clay held up a hand. "You're going to listen now. I'm not giving campaign season to every pastor with a bullhorn and every donor with a checkbook. You keep this inside lanes, or I shut your lane down."

Every syllable was control dressed up as calm.

Lena answered in short declaratives. "A man is dead. Another man was taken from a grave and staged. A county vehicle was seen at that scene before dawn. If we narrow language to protect campaigns, we're part of the crime."

Cora set down her pen.

Clay looked at Beau. "Do you endorse this position?"

Beau rubbed the bridge of his nose. "I don't want our case laughed out of court. State-lab help gives us cleaner chain."

It was not support. It was enough.

Clay stood, palms on the table. "Fine. State lab gets priority intake. Cora, draft a statement that says historical identity is under forensic confirmation and that outside lab support has been requested in the interest of transparency." He signed the yellow routing sheet in front of him and slid it across. "Effective now: Detective Waller is read-only on case communications. Every outbound call, memo, and media answer routes through my desk."

Cora tore off the carbon copy and handed it to Lena. The header read ADMIN DIRECTIVE 14-B.

He did not raise his voice. He did not need to.

"Understood," Lena said.

It tasted like swallowing chalk.

Meeting broke at 9:36. Lena stopped at her desk, opened the case file, and tried to upload Terrence Cole's protected annex. The screen flashed READ-ONLY ACCESS - CONTACT SHERIFF ADMIN. The attach button was gone.

She printed the statement and handed it to Beau for upload under his credentials. Then she walked with him to the stairwell.

"You poked him in both eyes at once," he said.

"He had both eyes on the wrong thing."

"Yeah." Beau looked down at the vending machine, considered it, then kept moving. "You did right. Just know he don't forget this kind of right."

Outside, the night had not cooled. The air felt wet enough to drink. Cicadas sawed from the courthouse trees. Across the street, three satellite trucks idled with mast lights up, and a small crowd had already gathered at the barricades with handmade signs and church fans. Someone had printed Isaiah Bell's senior portrait and taped it to a poster board in the time it took to hold one meeting.

News moved faster than command. So did grief.

Lena stood on the courthouse steps and watched the evidence courier pull out for Jackson under state escort, red taillights shrinking down Front Street. She felt the familiar split open inside her: one part procedure, one part rage, both of them awake.

She took out her notebook and wrote the next day's first line in block letters so there would be no argument later.

GET THE VAN LOGS OFF SHERIFF SERVER BEFORE THEY LOCK ME OUT FOR GOOD.

Then, beneath it, she added: NEW HOPE LOT, 5:30 P.M. Half her witnesses would be there with grease on their hands and nowhere clean to hide.


# Chapter 2

By five-thirty the church lot looked like a county fair run by people who knew one another's debts. Pickup beds stood open with coolers sweating into the dust. Aluminum trays steamed on folding tables under blue pop-up tents. A hand-lettered sign leaned against a bucket of catfish fillets: YOUTH CHOIR TRIP FUNDRAISER, CASH ONLY, GOD BLESS. Heat pressed down hard enough to flatten voices. The oil hissed anyway.

She had written NEW HOPE LOT, 5:30 P.M. in block letters the night before, like ink could keep the day from splintering.

Lena parked under the only pecan tree left with shade and sat a moment with both hands on the wheel. The wheel felt sticky where last summer's vinyl had started to crack. Her shirt still carried morgue bleach in the seams. She could smell it each time she breathed. If she shut her eyes, she saw the cotton gin office floor and the shape under the tarp that had been impossible and still real. She opened her eyes before the memory finished its loop.

At the fellowship-hall door, Deacon Avery Stokes waved her in with the hand that still wore a white golf glove in August. He had lost two fingertips to a baler in 1989 and said the glove kept people from staring. He always smelled like peppermint oil and diesel.

"Detective Waller," he said, pitch-perfect formal because the teenagers were listening. "You here to eat or here to inspect our grease compliance?"

"If this oil passes chain of custody, I'll take two plates," Lena said.

Avery barked a laugh and slapped a ticket strip into her palm. "You sound tired enough to tell the truth. You want hushpuppies before they go hard, you ask Bernice at the fry station. She hoards the good batch."

The line curled from fryers to cake table to drinks cooler, then doubled back near the wall fans. Cicadas shrilled above the lot, and somebody's little cousin ran between adults with a gallon tea pitcher too heavy for him, sloshing sweet tea onto white sneakers. Men in Sunday belts and weekday boots argued softly over whether the county fair had ever paid prize money on time. Two girls in choir polos practiced harmony under their breath while they wrapped cutlery in napkins. The noise was ordinary. It sat on her shoulders like a wet towel and, for a minute, did not feel like threat.

Ida stood behind the dessert table in her slate-blue church dress, wrists braced against arthritis tape that matched nothing she owned. Her smile made room for everyone and gave nothing away. Lemon cakes on one side, peach cobbler on the other, and index cards with names of whoever donated what because Ida believed accounting was a moral discipline.

"You made it," Ida said, not looking up from her clipboard. "There are people here who think your badge buys you a line pass. It does not."

"I'm off the clock."

"You're never off anything. Move that tray before it slides."

Lena shifted a pan of cornbread and kissed Ida's cheek. Her mother smelled like Pond's cold cream and fryer smoke. She wore pearl studs with one scratched surface that caught the light wrong, same pair she'd worn to Lena's graduation and to funerals where nobody spoke above a murmur.

"Jalen with you?" Lena asked.

"He said he was coming if we had battery packs for the phone army."

"That means no."

Ida gave a short look over her glasses. "That means he's sixteen. He'll arrive hungry and annoyed in equal portions."

Lena took her plate and moved along the line. Bernice Talbot worked the fish station with her reading glasses chained to her neck and a sequined visor that said BLESSED in silver letters. Every time she dropped fillets in the basket, she muttered, "Go on now," like fish could be coached.

"Baby," Bernice said to Lena, "you need more than two pieces. Don't make me testify you under-ate in this heat."

"If I eat four, I'll sleep on my feet."

"Then eat three and sin less."

Bernice slid an extra fillet onto Lena's tray and winked.

Lena ate at a folding table near the side steps where breeze moved in brief, stingy pulls. Fried crust cracked under her teeth; hot cornmeal burned the roof of her mouth before she tasted salt and pepper. It was better food than anything she'd made in a week. For nine bites she thought about nothing but chewing.

Jalen came in from the lot with a basketball tucked under one arm and his black hoodie tied at his waist because he refused to wear shorts unless he was on a court. His hair was half twisted, half undone, like he'd started and quit when somebody texted him. He did not look at the fryers first; he looked at the room, mapping where phones were pointed.

"You got me a plate?" he asked Lena.

"I can get one."

"I already got one." He set down a paper tray heaped with fries and fish and two lemonades sweating through the paper. "These people fry like they're under federal order."

"Say hello to your grandmother."

"I said hey when I walked in. She was auditing the cobbler table."

He sat, dropped his basketball between his shoes, and nudged his phone toward Lena. The screen showed a clipped video of yesterday's perimeter tape at the gin, with captions stacked over her face: DEAD MAN FROM 1963 BACK IN COUNTY CUSTODY?? and #MERCERGHOSTCASE. Someone had added laughter tracks between shaky zooms.

"This got like eighty thousand views already," Jalen said. "People are making merch. There's a shirt with your squad car and a skeleton in the backseat."

Lena kept her fork still. "Take that down at this table."

"I'm not posting it. I'm saying everybody's talking about it like a show."

"It's not a show."

"I know that. I'm saying that's how they're doing it."

He said it with more curiosity than cruelty. That was the part that got under her skin. He had grown up with his mother leaving mid-meal for callouts and returning smelling like rain, blood, or bleach. He understood danger as schedule disruption, not as lineage. That was not his fault. It still felt like a cut.

"A man is dead," she said. "Two men are dead. One of them got lynched before your grandparents had indoor plumbing. Keep your voice down."

Jalen shrugged and stabbed a fry into tartar sauce. "I'm keeping it down. Everybody else is talking louder than me."

At the next table, Calvin Reed leaned in over a paper cup, his old Mercer Transit ID still clipped to his belt like retirement had never fully convinced him. "They say that Bell body keeps showing because somebody cursed the river," he told his wife.

Then he caught Lena listening, shook his head, and corrected himself. "It ain't no curse. That's logistics and evil."

He lifted his cup in apology. She nodded back.

Ida passed carrying a tray of tea cups and did not break stride. "You can discuss curses after cleanup," she told the men. "Right now you can discuss mopping."

The men saluted with mock obedience and laughed. Ida kept moving, practical as a metronome.

Lena waited until Jalen had half his plate gone before she tried again. "You remember your granddaddy's old storage key? Small brass one with blue paint on the bow?"

Jalen frowned. "Why would I remember that?"

"Because you used to steal it and pretend you had your own office."

"That was elementary school."

"Do you know where it ended up?"

"No. Probably in one of Grandma's tins with all the church money and random screws. Why?"

"Just asking."

"It's not just asking if you're doing that voice."

Lena looked toward Ida. Her mother was taking cash with one hand and straightening the donation ledger with the other, face set in polite attention. If Lena walked over there with detective questions, she would make a scene at a church fundraiser. Ida would call it disrespect, and Ida would be right.

Jalen read her silence too easily. "This is about old stuff, right? About Granddaddy and that Bell name."

"Eat your food."

"You always say that when the answer is yes."

He pushed back from the table, balancing his basketball with his knee. "I'm gonna go shoot at Marcus's cousin's rim before dark. You can text me later."

"Stay where I can reach you."

"You can always reach me." He held up his phone. "Unless I'm in a game."

She wanted to tell him that was not true and getting less true every year. She said, "Eight-thirty check-in. No later."

"Fine."

He jogged out to the lot, shoes slapping concrete, already typing with his free hand.

Lena found Ida behind the kitchen doors rinsing metal tongs in a sink too shallow for the work. The kitchen fan rattled overhead and moved hot air from one corner to another. Grease had slicked the tile near the fryer, and Ida planted her feet wide to keep her balance. She looked up once, saw Lena's face, and set the tongs down.

"Not here," Ida said.

"Then where?"

"Where nobody is stacking chairs with half an ear on us."

They went out the side door behind the fellowship hall to the narrow strip of grass by the dumpster enclosure. The air smelled like wet cardboard and tartar sauce. Choir kids shouted in the lot. A thunderhead stacked itself purple over the levee, holding rain hostage.

Lena spoke first. "I need to ask about 1963."

Ida's mouth flattened. "No."

"I'm not asking for gossip. I'm asking for records."

"And I said no."

"Mama. Yesterday that body showed up beside a fresh homicide. People are moving remains like props. If Daddy touched paperwork then, if he filed anything, I need to know what exists before somebody burns it now."

Ida turned, braced one hand on the brick wall, then looked back. Her eyes had gone bright in the way that meant pain arriving as anger.

"You want me to stand in this church yard and open that year like a pantry can?" she said. "You want details? Fine. Men laughed with blood on their cuffs and still made Sunday service. Women scrubbed truck beds before daylight because if they didn't, the smell stayed all week. Your father came home with river mud in his boot tread and wouldn't let me wash those socks with your baby clothes. You think I forgot what month that was?"

Lena did not move.

Ida stepped closer, voice low and hard. "Don't ask me to defend him, and don't ask me to betray him for free at a fish fry. I carried that house on my back while he put on a badge every morning and told himself he was keeping order. Order for who? Tell me that."

The question landed like a slap. Lena's throat went dry.

"I'm not trying to put you on trial," she said.

"You already did."

"I'm trying to find out what is true."

Ida laughed once, bitter and brief. "Truth. Truth is he kept papers he should have burned, because men like him always believed paperwork could save them from memory."

Lena felt her pulse jump. "What papers?"

Ida looked away immediately, angry at herself now. "Nothing you need tonight."

"You just said he kept them. Where?"

"I said too much."

"Mama."

"Don't call me that in your work voice." Ida pinched the bridge of her nose and breathed through her mouth. "Green sugar tin in my pantry. Key is inside. Unit fourteen at Delta Lock and Store, old Highway Nine lot. That's all I'm giving you. You hear me? That's all."

"Did you read what's in there?"

"I read enough to lose sleep for thirty years."

"Does it mention Bell?"

"I'm done."

Ida straightened her shoulders, wiped her palms on a dish towel, and stepped back toward the kitchen door. Before she went inside, she looked at Lena with exhausted fury.

"If you crack that box open," she said, "you don't get to come back here pretending you were only curious."

Then she returned to the fundraiser, smile in place before the door finished swinging.

Lena stayed in the narrow strip of grass until cicadas drowned out the kitchen fan. Her palms had gone damp. She could feel the old ache in her right knee where the surgery scar pulled in humidity. She told herself to finish one plate, stack some chairs, be a daughter for another hour. Instead she went through the fellowship hall, through the line of children carrying dessert plates, and into Ida's pantry where canned tomatoes sat alphabetized by date.

The green Imperial sugar tin sat on the top shelf behind dry beans. Lena reached up, cursed under her breath when her knee bit, then dragged a step stool from beside the mop sink. One stool leg caught on the tile and screeched loud enough to carry into the hall.

Bernice Talbot leaned into the doorway with a tray of lemon slices. "Ida sent you for something?"

"Tea stir sticks," Lena said, too fast.

Bernice's visor flashed in the fluorescent light. "Uh-huh. We got stir sticks right here by my hand, baby."

Lena gave her a tight smile and climbed the stool anyway. Bernice watched one extra second, then moved on.

Inside the tin lay safety pins, two church envelope clips, one house key with yellow tape, and a brass storage key marked 14 with chipped blue polish. Lena closed her fist around the key and stepped down.

Ida stood in the doorway before Lena could pocket it.

"You couldn't wait ten minutes?" Ida asked. Her voice stayed level, which was worse than yelling. "You had to do this in my pantry where everybody can see your back and guess the rest."

"Mr. Pritchard locks at eight. If those boxes are getting cleaned out, ten minutes matters."

"And I don't matter."

"That's not what I said."

"It's what you did."

Lena slid the key into her pocket anyway. Ida looked at the movement like Lena had reached for her throat.

"Go then," Ida said, stepping aside. "But don't come to my house tonight and ask me to make this feel righteous. You chose your side in my kitchen."

Lena went past her with her pulse hammering in her ears and the taste of old fryer grease rising in her throat.

Delta Lock and Store sat at the edge of a drainage ditch off Highway Nine, behind a shuttered bait shop with a Pepsi sign bleached almost white. The office window held a handwritten CLOSED AT 8:00 PM sign and a string of plastic beads that clicked each time the door opened. A sodium light buzzed above the rows of corrugated metal doors. Rain smell moved in before the rain itself.

Mr. Pritchard ran the office with a hearing aid that whistled when his collar pressed the wrong way. He wore an Ole Miss polo tucked into work pants and a belt holster for a can of wasp spray.

"Waller," he said when she stepped in. "You're Deputy Waller's girl. Been years."

"Detective now," she said.

"Good for you. Bad for me if you're here for trouble."

"I need unit fourteen. Family property."

He checked a ledger with yellowed tabs. "Paid through September, so nobody can accuse me of theft. You have thirty-two minutes before lockup. If lightning hits the gate again, I'm not climbing out there."

"I won't keep you."

"Everybody says that."

He handed her a flashlight with a cracked lens. "Back left row. Watch your step. That flood in spring floated half these units. Smells like a wet dog graveyard if you breathe too deep."

Unit 14's padlock resisted once, then gave with a dry snap. When Lena rolled the door up, humid air dumped out dense with mildew, motor oil, and paper rot. The beam from the office flashlight cut across stacked boxes bowed in the middle like tired backs. A metal shelf had rusted through one leg and leaned into a bundle of fishing rods. Waterline marks striped the cardboard at mid-calf height.

She stood in the doorway and listened: rain starting in soft drops on the tin roofs, distant interstate hum, one loose gutter tapping time. There was nobody else in the row.

The first box held Deputy uniforms folded by someone who cared about creases long after retirement. Patches from Mercer County Sheriff, old badge pins in a tobacco tin, a cracked citation stamp pad. Second box: tax receipts, bait invoices, church bulletins Ida had probably meant to throw out and never did. Third box: photographs fused at the corners, edges furred white with mold. Her father at a catfish tournament, younger than Lena had ever known him, one arm around men she now recognized as names in city minutes and sheriff reports.

She pulled another box forward and the bottom gave way in her hands, dumping damp files onto concrete. A dead roach skittered from the stack. She knelt, knee protesting, and spread pages in the flashlight beam: handwritten shift logs, map photocopies, an envelope labeled FEED BILL 1984, then nothing, then more nothing. The gaps made more noise than the papers. There should have been more boxes. Shelf dust showed clean rectangles where something had sat for years and then disappeared recently.

Her mouth tasted metallic. Had Ida moved them? Had somebody else with access done a cleanup after chapter-one headlines hit? She checked the lock hasp from inside. There were no fresh pry marks. That did not mean it was safe. It meant someone had been careful.

"Fifteen minutes," Mr. Pritchard called from two rows over. His voice bounced off sheet metal, already irritated.

"I heard you."

She opened a low plastic tote under the shelf and found a black composition notebook sealed in a gallon freezer bag. The bag had yellowed, but the zipper still held. On the notebook cover, in her father's square block letters: INDEX / DO NOT REMOVE PAGES.

Lena sat back on her heels and opened it.

The first pages were columns of initials and numbers, each line tagged with short codes she did not recognize yet: CH-7, LV-2, RIV-A, MC-DKT. Dates ran down the margins from 1979 to 1986. It was not a diary. It was a cross-reference key, built by someone who expected retrieval under pressure.

Her father had taught her as a kid to label fishing tackle by depth and current, never by lure color, because color changed and depth did not. This notebook used that same brain: route first, then cover story, then person. The codes tightened her chest.

She flipped to the back where an alphabetical index was penciled faintly enough to evade a casual glance. Bell, Isaiah had one entry and a page number torn out. Bell family cemetery had another with a star beside it. Then:

Mercer docket - MC-DKT-11 - "land cert chain / deputy intake / Pike chambers copy"

The words sat in the beam like a live wire.

She read the line twice, then once aloud to make sure she was not forcing pattern from fatigue. "Mercer docket."

Rain hardened overhead, drumming the metal roof in fast rolls. Her phone vibrated in her back pocket. She ignored it, turned another page, found a notation linking MC-DKT-11 to box marker RED TWINE LEDGER. She scanned the unit. No red twine, no ledger, only broken banker boxes and a coil of trotline rope.

Phone vibration again, then FaceTime ringtone thin through the rain. Jalen's name flashed across the screen with the picture he hated, him at thirteen holding a fish longer than his forearm and grinning like he had invented the river. She watched the call ring to the final pulse while her thumb hovered above accept.

If she took the call, he would ask where she was, why she had dipped from church, whether this had to be tonight. He would be right in all the ordinary ways and wrong in the one that mattered. Evidence moved when people felt watched. Missing boxes proved that already.

She let it go to voicemail.

The screen showed 7:58 PM, then a text arrived before she could lock it: `nvm. at dad's in 20.`

Guilt came hot, then flattened into focus so quickly it scared her. She set the phone facedown on a dry patch of concrete and kept reading.

Mr. Pritchard's keys jangled nearer. "Eight o'clock, detective. Gate timer's meaner than me."

"Two minutes."

She photographed every page she could, hands steady from habit. Then she slid the notebook back into the freezer bag and tucked it under her arm. She took one old map tube and a packet of loose index cards because they looked like they belonged with the coding system. Everything else stayed. Carrying too much would announce value to anyone watching cameras or logs.

When she rolled the unit door down, rain blew sideways through the row and soaked her shirt in seconds. Mr. Pritchard stood under a golf umbrella patched with black duct tape.

"Find what you needed?" he asked.

"I found enough."

"That's never good news in this county."

He locked the row gate and waited while she signed out on the clipboard. The hearing aid whistled once when he ducked his head. "Your daddy used to come in late," he said, almost to himself. "Always said he was organizing receipts. Nobody organizes receipts at midnight."

Lena met his eyes. "Did he ever come with anyone?"

"Sometimes a courthouse boy in a tie too expensive for humidity. Sometimes alone. I didn't ask. My job was locks."

She nodded and stepped into the rain.

In the car she sat with the notebook in her lap and the engine off, listening to water hammer the windshield until the glass looked milked over. The cab smelled like wet cotton and old coffee. Her pulse had finally slowed, leaving a hollow ache behind her ribs.

Ida had not denied anything. Ida had fought like someone defending a wound from salt.

Lena opened the notebook one more time at the Mercer line. `land cert chain / deputy intake / Pike chambers copy.` Her father's block letters did not tremble. The man who wrote this had believed paper could outlast shame.

Her phone lit again with a voicemail icon from Jalen. This time she hit play.

His voice came in thin under wind noise and traffic. "Ma, Grandma told me you took Granddaddy's key and dipped. She said she's fine, but she was crying, and then she hung up on me. I'm at Dad's now. If this case is about us, stop leaving me blind."

The recording clicked off. Lena stared at the rain-smeared windshield. MC-DKT-11 had felt like code on paper five minutes ago. Now it had her mother's voice breaking and her son doing triage between houses.

She called Jalen back. He answered on the third ring.

"Where you at?" he asked.

"Still at the storage lot. I'm on my way to your dad's now. Stay inside until I get there."

A beat. "You coming for real?"

"Yes. You're hearing this from me tonight."

She started the car and turned east toward Jalen's father's place instead of home, the notebook pinned under her forearm so it could not slide when she braked, promise first and sleep nowhere in the plan.


# Chapter 3

By five-thirty the morgue loading bay already smelled like hot pennies and disinfectant, as if the building had spent the night chewing on blood and bleach and had not decided which taste it preferred. Lena parked beside the biohazard dumpster, killed the engine, and sat for one extra breath with both palms on the steering wheel. Her right knee complained in its old surgical seam when she turned too fast. The humidity got inside scar tissue before it touched skin.

She had made it to Jalen's father's place before midnight and back out before dawn, promise kept and no sleep to show for it.

Beau came around the hood with two coffees balanced in one hand and a manila folder under his arm. His shirt was wet at the spine before sunrise.

"You sure we need to do this before shift change?" he asked. "Night people are meaner than day people and worse at paperwork."

"That's why we're here now," Lena said. "If this was a one-off stunt at the gin, the log should be clean this morning. If it isn't, we start with custody breach, not folklore."

He handed her coffee. "I brought the kind you hate because it stays hot longer."

"That isn't coffee. That's roofing tar."

"Tar keeps you upright."

Inside, the hallway lights flickered with a faint insect buzz. The tile floor held a film of damp that made every shoe squeak. Rochelle Pritchard was at the intake counter in a turtle-print scrub cap, balancing a crossword on the clip tray while she signed transfer receipts. She had one purple nitrile glove on and one bare hand with chipped coral polish. A nicotine patch curled off the back of her neck. She greeted Lena without looking up, filling three letters in the crossword with quick, angry jabs.

"You people are early," Rochelle said. "I get off in thirty-two minutes and my girl has all-state band tryouts at seven. If y'all are here to ask me broad questions and nod at me, do it fast."

"We're not here to nod," Lena said. "We need drawer verification on Bell remains and full access history from midnight to now. Printed and digital."

Rochelle finally looked up. Her eyes were red in the corners in the way of somebody who had cried or stared too long at fluorescent light. "You need Dr. Rao for release-level access."

"Call him," Lena said.

"Already did when your car turned in. I might work night shift, but I do own ears."

Rochelle led them down the cold corridor. The compressor thrum sat in Lena's teeth. On the steel door to Cold Storage B, the swipe panel showed green, then amber, then green again before it unlocked. Rochelle noticed the flicker and swore softly.

"It did that twice at two in the morning," she said. "I logged it. Maintenance said 'reboot the panel.' Maintenance says that about everything except their marriages."

She opened the paper ledger clipped beside the door. Lena pulled her phone and photographed each page with timestamp overlay. Entry lines for the last six hours looked wrong before she did any math. The handwriting changed mid-line. One row listed Bell remains transferred from Drawer B-7 to B-7, same location, same minute, different initials. Another row had an impossible clock sequence: 02:14 release stamp followed by 02:09 return seal.

Lena slid the folder from Beau's hand and opened a fresh discrepancy form. "We're not doing memory today," she said. "We're doing record. Beau, witness line. Rochelle, you'll sign originator and correction note."

Rochelle set her jaw. "You think I forged this?"

"I think the log's compromised," Lena said. "That can happen to your pen, your software, or your card reader. We document it so nobody gets to rewrite it later."

Beau signed first, muttering, "Never seen a dead man check himself in and out like a motel guest."

Rochelle gave him a look that should have peeled paint. "You got jokes before sunrise, Broussard. Must be nice."

Dr. Naveen Rao arrived in a wrinkled button-down under a disposable gown, hair still damp from a sink wash. He carried a stainless tiffin clipped shut with two rubber bands and smelled faintly of cardamom instead of breakroom coffee. Lena had learned that his wife packed him tea concentrate because he forgot to eat on overnight call and then shook through noon. He set the tiffin on a counter, snapped on gloves, and scanned the ledger without touching it.

"This is not random sloppiness," he said. "Two inks, one pressure profile. Somebody traced over original times."

"Can you certify that?" Lena asked.

"I can certify that this page has overwriting and non-sequential entry logic. I can certify that either software or person manipulated chronology. I cannot certify motive while standing in a refrigerator."

"Good. Say exactly that again when I record your statement."

Rao glanced at her. "You slept?"

"No."

"Then your temper is ahead of you. Keep it on a leash until after chain review."

He opened Drawer B-7.

The rail came out halfway and stopped. The tray was empty. A rectangle of meltwater sat in the center, clean-edged, with one curled fiber stuck to the metal like wet thread. The toe-tag wire was gone. There was no body bag, no wrapped remains, and no trace of the county evidence seal Lena had applied two nights earlier.

Beau's coffee cup hit the floor and rolled under another drawer.

Rochelle made a sound that was not a word. She put both hands on her head, gloves squeaking against scrub cap. "No. No, no, no. I saw him at one-fifty. I checked tags when the panel glitched. I checked because I don't trust this damn building."

Lena made herself breathe through her nose. She had expected tampering. She had not expected absence. The cold bit through her shirt and raised heat on her skin. She heard her own voice go procedural because if she let it go personal now, she would kick something and lose the room.

"Nobody leaves this hallway," she said. "Beau, seal both ends, log everyone present by name and time. Dr. Rao, we need stills of tray surface before contact. Rochelle, I need access logs from door panel and workstation M-2. Now."

"You think we still have M-2 data?" Rochelle said, already moving toward the terminal. "That box crashes when it rains sideways."

"Then print whatever it has before it crashes again."

They worked inside compressor noise and bad light. Lena's fingers numbed through two layers of gloves while she bagged the fiber and swabbed condensation rings. Rao dictated temperature differentials in clipped syllables. Beau radioed dispatch for internal scene status only and shut down anyone asking for details over open channel.

By 06:07 they had hard copies of the card-reader events.

01:48 - Rochelle Pritchard, authorized entry.

02:14 - Admin Override, credential masked.

02:16 - Terminal M-2 offline.

02:33 - Admin Override close.

02:41 - Rochelle Pritchard, authorized entry.

Rochelle stared at the printout. "Masked means what?"

Rao answered before Lena did. "Masked means somebody with high-level privileges suppressed the visible credential string. That setting requires IT or command permissions."

"Command like sheriff command?"

"Command like somebody who knows where to click and who can get away with it," Rao said.

Lena took photos of the screen, the printouts, and the overwritten ledger page, then initialed every page with date and time. The discrepancy was on paper, not rumor. She had learned from too many dead files that truth without paper was just grief.

Her phone buzzed. Sheriff Clay Reddick.

She put him on speaker because she wanted witnesses.

"Morning, Sheriff," she said.

Clay's voice came warm and polished through static. "I heard you've got a custody concern."

"It is more than a concern," Lena said. "Bell remains are missing from cold storage. We have overwritten log entries and an admin-level masked access event between 02:14 and 02:33. I am opening an internal breach file now."

A pause. Paper rustled on his end, as if he were choosing which page to inhabit.

"Contain the language," he said. "Use 'irregularity' until we verify. We are not feeding panic with speculative wording."

"Sheriff, the drawer is empty."

"Then secure the facility and wait for me. Nobody briefs press. Nobody briefs freelancers."

"We still need to notify state lab and medical examiner chain."

"You'll do what I authorize, Detective."

The line clicked dead.

Beau let out a breath through his teeth. "He woke up in campaign voice. That's never a good sign."

Before Lena answered, dispatch cracked through his shoulder radio, too loud for the corridor.

"Units near levee road, respond. Possible DOA at Cormorant River Ramp. Caller reports one fresh male and one... old body, repeat old body, wrapped and staged. Crowd forming."

Nobody in the corridor moved for half a second. Even the compressor seemed to pause.

Rochelle whispered, "Jesus."

Lena was already pulling off one pair of gloves and reaching for another. "Beau, internal seal stays. Rao, you hold this room and don't surrender originals to anybody without my signature. Rochelle, you call your daughter and tell her you'll be late. Then you sit in that office and write exactly what you saw at 01:50 and 02:41. No improvements, no theories."

Rochelle swallowed hard and nodded. "She's playing clarinet," she said, as if reminding herself there was still a world where scales mattered.

"Good," Lena said. "Then write fast."

Outside, the heat hit like an open oven.

By the time they reached the river ramp, half the county had already found the live stream.

The levee road narrowed into a choke point of patrol SUVs, pickup trucks, and civilians on foot holding phones over each other’s shoulders. Cicadas screamed from the willow line. The river carried a skin of fuel sheen and sun glare. Lena stepped out and heard three versions of the same accusation before her boots hit gravel.

"They lost him again."

"They moved him."

"This is your circus, Waller."

Each shout landed in the same sore place behind Lena's ribs. Half the phones were aimed at her badge before she even reached the tape, hungry for a sentence they could post before she had a body temperature.

Two deputies were arguing at the tape line, one trying to keep bystanders back and the other waving in a city councilman with his camera team. Lena shoved between them.

"Nobody crosses that tape without scene assignment," she said. "I don't care who he votes with. Lock this down to one hundred feet and start a name log on every person already inside."

The younger deputy opened his mouth to protest and then shut it. He had acne scars and trembling hands and a wedding ring still bright from new metal. "Yes, ma'am."

She moved down the ramp.

The ramp seemed to narrow with every step, crowd noise hitting her in hot bursts: chant, laugh, camera beep, thunder mutter upriver. She kept her eyes on concrete so she would not start reading faces instead of evidence.

Lyle Fenn lay on the concrete near the waterline, one arm bent under him like he had tried to get up and failed on the second effort. A narrow ligature groove rode high under his jaw, and below it the front of his neck had been opened in a ragged slash that showed flashes of white cartilage when the flies lifted. Blood had dried in layered runs as if the body had been handled after the first injury. His left cheekbone was crushed flat, likely from a blunt strike during the struggle. He wore marine-supply work pants with reflective tape at the cuffs and one steel-toe boot half unlaced. His belt buckle was shaped like a catfish. His right pocket held a receipt from Delta Bait & Fuel for dog food, brake fluid, and cinnamon rolls, timestamped 10:42 p.m.

Three feet away, on a cheap plastic tarp pinned by cinder blocks, Isaiah Bell's remains had been laid out with deliberate care. Whoever moved him had wrapped and unwrapped him like ritual: county sheet, then burial cloth, then county sheet again, each tied with twine knots that looked clean and practiced. The evidence tag hanging from the wrist wire carried Lena's initials in black marker, but the sequence number was wrong by one digit.

The empty drawer from dawn and this staged river tableau slammed together in her head. Somebody had moved a body, copied her hand, and counted on noise and cameras to do the rest.

Her stomach lurched. The wrongness of the tag was worse than an empty drawer. It meant somebody had copied her handwriting and made a mistake only someone inside process would notice.

Beau crouched by Fenn's boots and looked up. "Wallet says Lyle Aaron Fenn. Driver's license confirms. You remember him from last summer? Barges and scrap permits, always in the tax office hollering."

Lena remembered a man who smelled like diesel and peppermint and wore his sunglasses indoors because he thought it looked like power. She remembered him laughing too loudly outside a hearing where families had lost land he called "unproductive acreage." She had forgotten the dimple in his chin until she saw it split open now.

"Call next of kin through dispatch," she said. "And run all his contacts from last forty-eight hours."

"Already started."

Mara Quinn ducked under the tape before the deputy saw her, phone rig strapped to her chest and a spiral notebook tucked in her back pocket. She wore yellow rubber rain boots on a cloudless morning because, once, a drainage ditch had swallowed one of her shoes on camera and she had refused to repeat the embarrassment. Her close-cropped hair shone with sweat and river mist.

"Lena," Mara said, voice even but fast. "I need one attributable fact before rumor writes it for you. Was Bell in county storage at midnight, yes or no?"

"This is an active scene," Lena said.

"No, that's your perimeter. I'm asking chain of custody."

"No public statement yet."

Mara held her gaze. "Then on background. One clean sentence. If I can't quote a fact from you, I'll end up quoting somebody else's lie and your office will bless it by lunch."

Lena hated that Mara was right. She hated it more that Clay would use that truth against both of them.

"On background," Lena said quietly, "morgue access logs were altered before this call came in. Write it as a records breach."

Mara's pen moved. "Clerical slop or override credential?"

"One sentence was the deal."

"You're not wrong, and you know it."

A shout rose from behind the tape as a woman pushed through two deputies, still wearing a red fast-food apron over jeans and house slippers. A grease stain marked the apron pocket, her hands were shaking, and a cigarette was tucked behind one ear like she'd forgotten it was there.

"Where is he?" she yelled. "Where's Lyle?"

The younger deputy grabbed her arm. She swung at him, missed, and nearly fell.

Lena stepped in before the deputy forced her down. "Ma'am, stop. Stop and breathe. What's your name?"

"Tina Fenn. I'm his sister."

She stared at the body and made a noise that scraped Lena's spine. It was not a scream. It sounded like a person trying to swallow a scream and failing. Tina pointed with both hands because one hand would not stay steady.

"That's him. That's Lyle. He can't swim. You hear me? He don't come down here before daylight because he can't swim. He said somebody from your office called him last night, said they had questions about permits. He went because he always thinks he's smarter than trouble."

"Who called him?" Lena asked.

"Unknown number. He put it on speaker for a second while he was looking for his truck keys. Man voice. Said 'deputy's office.' That's all I heard."

Mara had stopped writing and was watching Tina's face. Then she lowered the phone, not recording for a full ten seconds, and offered Tina a bottle of water from her bag without a word. Tina took it with numb fingers and did not drink.

Lena signaled a victim services volunteer and turned back to the bodies. The crowd had swollen again. Someone had started chanting "Whose chain? Your chain." Someone else yelled that cops were staging corpses for votes. People weren't whispering anymore. They were broadcasting.

Clay Reddick arrived with two command SUVs and a pressed white shirt that somehow stayed crisp in ninety-degree air. He walked the tape line greeting cameras by first name, hand on shoulder here, solemn nod there, campaign-ready grief. By the time he reached Lena he had already found the angle where the river sat behind him like a backdrop.

"Detective," he said, voice pitched for nearby microphones, "what do we have?"

"Confirmed homicide victim Lyle Aaron Fenn, male, local. Bell remains are present again with altered tag sequence and rewrapped staging. Morgue records show overwritten times and a masked admin access window from 02:14 to 02:33."

Boom mics dipped closer. Lena could feel the tug between what a court needed and what a crowd could weaponize before lunch.

Clay's smile did not move, but his eyes cooled. "You're putting internal process in front of open mics."

"This is operational language."

"This ramp is full of phones. Use preliminary terms until command clears the rest."

He leaned closer and dropped volume. "You're not briefing anybody else until I say so. That includes your reporter friend in the rain boots."

"Too late. She already has one line."

"Then that line stops here."

"No. It's in witness statements and access logs now."

Clay straightened, back to public voice. "We'll handle this inside. Finish scene work."

He moved away before she answered, stepping directly into a camera scrum where he said, "We are addressing irregularities with full professionalism and no rush to judgment," as if he had not just tried to scrub her morning statement.

Rain hit twenty minutes later, sudden and hard, one of those Delta bursts that turned dust to slick clay in three breaths. Deputies wrestled tarps over evidence markers while the crowd retreated to truck cabs and kept filming through cracked windows. Thunder rolled across the levee and made everyone flinch at once.

Kneeling in runoff, Lena photographed each twine knot on Isaiah's wrappings with a scale card in frame, then cut the falsified tag free and sealed it in a fresh evidence sleeve. Rain blurred her first initials on the custody tape, so she signed the strip again while Beau read times back over thunder.

Under the fire department awning, Tina Fenn shook so hard the unopened water bottle knocked against her teeth. Victim services asked who could reach Lyle's daughter and mother before the videos did. Tina said, "Not from Facebook. Not from TV." Lena called dispatch herself, logged family notification in progress, and put a hold on public name release until Tina made those calls.

By noon the preliminary packet had thirty-one pages, three signed witness statements, and a chain-of-custody addendum still wet at the edges. Lena's shirt clung cold against her back, her right knee throbbed from crouching on concrete, and Tina's swallowed scream kept replaying under every clap of thunder.

The command meeting started at one-thirty in Conference B, where the ceiling stain looked like a map of a country nobody wanted to visit. The air conditioner rattled without cooling. Someone had set out stale cheese cubes and sweating bottled water as if this were a budget hearing.

Clay sat at the head of the table with a legal pad and three color-coded folders. Beau took a chair near the door, shoulders hunched, knee bouncing. Lena sat opposite Clay and laid down two packets: morgue discrepancy report and river ramp scene log. She noticed Clay did not touch either packet.

Captain Darnell from internal compliance joined by speakerphone, voice tinny and over-bright. Public information officer Janice Bellamy sat along the wall, tapping notes into a tablet with one acrylic nail missing from her right thumb.

Lena's pulse still had river-ramp speed. The air conditioner rattled like loose metal in floodwater, and she flexed her right hand under the table so nobody saw it shake.

Clay drew a long breath, donor-banquet calm dropped into a room that smelled like wet uniforms.

"We're in a destabilized window," he said. "People are scared, cameras are starving, and one sloppy sentence turns into Exhibit A. We're holding one lane from here forward. Closed task force, effective immediately."

He slid one folder toward Lena and one toward Beau.

"Designated personnel only: Detective Waller, Detective Broussard, me, Captain Darnell for oversight, and a lab liaison assigned through my office. Evidence traffic runs through this table. Nothing freelances."

Lena opened the folder. The first page was TASK FORCE CONFIDENTIALITY ORDER. The second page listed contact restrictions. The third page listed disciplinary penalties.

The wording looked tidy enough for policy binders, but she could feel the trap in the sequencing: centralize first, explain later, punish anyone who asked why.

"This order blocks normal interagency channels," she said. "I need direct line to Rao and state lab if we want clean chain and same-day tox."

"You'll submit requests through command," Clay said.

"That adds delay at the exact choke point where the chain already failed."

"What it creates is accountability," Clay said. "No more side channels."

The confidentiality order sat in Lena's hands like quarantine tape with letterhead. She could feel Clay tightening process around facts, one signature block at a time.

Beau leaned back and rubbed his forehead. "Sheriff, if my outboard's taking on water, I don't run every bucket through city hall. We got a masked admin override in the morgue system. If command credentials are in the blast radius, routing everything through command is us tying our own hands."

Clay folded his hands, voice still polite. "Detective Broussard, boat metaphors don't help me, and implications help me even less."

"Wasn't implying," Beau said. "I'm saying it plain."

The room went quiet enough that Lena could hear the hum from Janice's tablet charger.

Silence pushed against Lena's ears harder than the morgue compressor had. Clay was not contesting facts; he was redrawing who got to name them in public.

Clay turned to Lena instead. He always did when he wanted to isolate resistance.

"Let's talk about media contamination," he said. "Ms. Quinn has been receiving privileged texture from this office. That stops now. Effective today, Mara Quinn is removed from all official briefings, hallway updates, and informal access. She gets the same press statements as everyone else, through PIO only."

Janice looked up. "I can draft that language and send by three."

Lena kept her voice level. "Mara is one of the only reporters in this county who corrects errors publicly when we give her documentation. Cutting her off won't stop leaks. It will move leaks to people who don't care about accuracy."

Clay gave her the courtesy smile he used when donors asked naive questions.

"Your role is evidence handling," he said. "Not media strategy."

"Tracking leaks is evidence handling."

"Tracking leaks is command prerogative."

He tapped the confidentiality order.

"Read paragraph four out loud, Detective."

Lena read: "'Any unauthorized disclosure of investigative information to external persons, including members of media, advocacy groups, and non-assigned personnel, constitutes insubordination and may result in immediate suspension pending review.'"

Clay nodded. "Clear enough?"

"Clear," she said.

It was clear in the way a locked door was clear.

Darnell's voice crackled through speaker. "For record completeness, note that the morgue discrepancy packet is received and logged under internal file IC-24-19. I recommend temporary credential freeze for all nonessential staff at morgue and periodic audit snapshots every four hours."

"Approved," Clay said. "Command office will control credential reactivation."

Lena made a note in the margin: *Control where breach occurred.* She did not show the note to anyone.

Meeting ended with signatures. Clay stood first, collecting folders before anyone else rose. He paused behind Lena's chair.

"You are good at scenes," he said softly, private voice now, no cameras. "Be good at structure too. Structure keeps people alive."

She looked up at him. "Structure also buries people when the wrong hands hold it."

His jaw flexed once. "Don't confuse defiance with integrity."

"Don't call this structure and expect me not to notice who's holding the keys."

He left without answering.

In the hallway outside Conference B, Mara was waiting by the vending machines with her notebook tucked under one arm and an empty crackers sleeve sticking out of her pocket. She had changed into dry shoes but kept the rain boots clipped to her backpack with a carabiner. She did not wave.

"How bad is it?" she asked.

Lena could have lied. Clay had just handed her the script for lying and called it order.

"You're cut out," she said. "Officially. No briefings, no hallway, no side calls. Janice will send you canned statements and smile while she does it."

Mara's face went still in that way Lena had learned to fear. Silence first, then decision.

"Who made the call?" Mara asked.

"Clay. Closed task force."

"So this isn't about me. It's containment."

"It's about who gets to touch evidence and who gets to describe it."

Mara looked down the hallway, where deputies moved in and out of offices carrying paper cups and rumors. "I can still work documents," she said. "Court pulls, procurement logs, vendor bids. I don't need their podium."

"They'll dress you down in public before they admit you were right."

"That hasn't changed since middle school," Mara said. "Question is whether you can still get me what can be proved. Not what can be said."

Lena rubbed her eyes with thumb and forefinger until sparks flared in the dark behind her lids. "I can't promise a channel."

"I didn't ask for promise. I asked for answer."

"Then here's the answer. The morgue logs were altered. We have printouts and signatures. Bell left county storage between two and two-thirty under masked credentials. If they deny that on camera, the paperwork contradicts them."

Mara nodded once. "Good. Copies outlive statements."

She started to turn away, then stopped. "Tina Fenn called me. She's sitting in her car in the Burger Barn lot because she can't stand the smell on her clothes and doesn't want her kids to see her like this. She said Lyle had a habit of leaving voice mails to himself when he was drunk so he wouldn't forget what he promised people. If that's true, his phone might have more than call logs."

Lena felt the day tilt. "Did Tina give you passcode?"

"No. She said he used his daughter's birthday, but she wouldn't say it out loud in a parking lot full of strangers. She said she'd tell you in person if you treat him like a person and not a lesson."

"Where is she now?"

"Still in the lot. Blue Corolla with busted taillight and a Saints sticker that's half peeled off."

Lena looked back toward Conference B, where Clay was probably already drafting tomorrow's narrative, and then toward the exit where heat pressed against the glass like another wall.

"Thank you," she said.

Mara gave a dry half-smile. "Don't thank me. Just don't waste the crack in the door."

She walked away fast, boots clacking from her backpack against her thigh.

Lena went to her desk, shut the door, and spread out copies of the morgue report, the ramp log, and the confidentiality order. The fluorescent light above her flickered twice and settled. She wrote three columns on a legal pad: *Who knew at 06:00. Who knew at 06:30. Who spoke by 07:00.* She filled names and times until the ink smeared under her hand sweat.

By three o'clock, Janice's office sent a countywide press email: *A specialized task force has been assembled to streamline communication and protect case integrity. All inquiries should be directed through official channels.*

It said nothing about missing remains, which meant half the county would call the river scene a hoax by supper.

Altered logs got flattened into "internal review," the kind of phrasing that let command choke every question at the hallway door.

Lyle Fenn appeared only as "adult male decedent pending family notification," as if Tina had not already screamed his name at the river and still had to carry that bureaucratic lie home to her children.

Lena printed the email and stapled it to the discrepancy packet. Then she opened her bottom drawer, moved the spare holster and tire gauge, and slid one copy of the morgue access printout under the false panel she kept for cash and emergency meds. If command wanted every route narrowed, she would make one route that did not depend on command.

Outside her office, thunder rolled again. The building lights dipped and recovered. Somewhere down the hall, somebody laughed too loudly at nothing.

Lena snapped a photo of the three-column leak map, tore the page free, and folded it into her back pocket. She called Tina once and got voicemail, called again and hit a full mailbox, then texted anyway: *Do not hand Lyle's phone to anybody. I'm coming now.*

From the bullpen she heard Janice tell dispatch, "Sheriff wants all Fenn family contact routed through command before sundown." Delay was how evidence got laundered in this building.

Lena grabbed a digital-consent form, her recorder, and her keys, then limped fast for the lot before the next storm cell flooded levee road and put Tina behind another ring of uniforms.

Four days later, the storm still had not broken when Rao sent word to meet him at the morgue without using email.


# Chapter 4

Four days later, the morgue sat behind the hospital loading dock like a forgotten appliance, humming harder than it should in the late-August heat. Lena parked under a light that buzzed with moths and got out into air that already felt wet enough to drink. The storm had not broken. The sky pressed down in one solid color, and the concrete gave the heat back through the soles of her shoes.

Inside, bleach and old coffee fought a losing war against sweet rot. The fluorescent tubes shivered overhead. Dr. Naveen Rao stood at the stainless table in a disposable gown, goggles pushed up in his thick hair, reading from a legal pad dense with numbers. He always wrote in pencil, narrow block letters, and the eraser end was wrapped in blue tape because he chewed pencils down to wood by noon. A child had stuck a glitter star sticker to the edge of his county ID badge, and he had not peeled it off.

"You got my message," he said without looking up.

"You said not to use email." Lena set her case file on the counter and kept her voice flat because she was tired enough to sound angry by accident. "I figured that meant it mattered."

"It matters." He finally looked at her. His eyes were red-rimmed and careful. "Beau with you?"

"On his way."

On the back bench, Trina Polk from histology lined up reagent bottles by height, then by label color, then back by height again. She wore two latex gloves on each hand because formalin cracked her knuckles until they bled. Under her breath she counted pipette drops in the same cadence every time, like she was saying grace over each sample.

Lena pulled out a chair and sat before her knee started complaining. Since the river ramp she still had grit from that levee in the hem of her slacks. She had washed the clothes twice. The grit stayed.

Rao flipped a page and pushed a printed chain-of-custody packet toward her. "Start there. I need you to see the gaps before I explain the data."

The packet looked complete until it did not. Item numbers jumped. Time stamps repeated. One page ended mid-line where a signature should have been and resumed on a different date, different pen, same initials. She knew those initials. She had seen them three times this week on forms that had no business matching.

"Who touched this after intake?" she asked.

"Officially? Three people." Rao gave a dry laugh without humor. "Actually? I can't prove that."

He tapped a second folder, older cardboard, brittle at the corners. "This is what we have from the 1963 burial transfer and county pathology addendum. Pages four through seven are gone. Torn, not misplaced. The edge fiber says hand removal, not decay."

Lena stared at the ragged inner seam. Somebody had pulled those pages with fingers, not floodwater. Her mother once kept church bulletins for forty years in acid-free sleeves, and this county could not keep one dead man's paperwork whole for sixty-three.

The door banged. Beau Broussard came in carrying two sweating fountain sodas and a paper bag gone translucent with grease. He was broad enough to make the room smaller and sunburned along the collar where his vest never covered. He set one cup near Lena, then peeled open the bag.

"Boudin kolaches," he said. "If this turns into one more six-hour science sermon, we're eating first."

Rao did not look up. "I heard that."
