"You were meant to." Beau leaned on the counter and nodded at the trays. "Tell me we aren't chasing ghosts before the sheriff walks in and says we're."

Rao laid out three specimen cassettes and a set of photographs under the bench lamp. The trays held bone, tissue, and a hair fragment, all tagged from two scenes that should not have shared a corpse. He started in his usual precise cadence, then sped up like he could outrun interruption.

"I ran standard morphology, then collagen integrity. It didn't fit a single-body profile."

"Contamination?" Lena asked.

"That's what I thought, so I reran extraction twice. Same match." Rao tapped the tray with a gloved knuckle. "I requested isotope panel approval, and command kicked it to review."

Beau frowned. "So they stalled you."

"They denied it pending command review."

Lena felt Beau turn his head toward her. Denied by command meant Clay.

"So what did you do?" she asked.

Rao reached for his goggles, then put them down. "I ran it anyway under method-validation code. That's outside routine protocol. I documented every step in my own log, and if they fire me, they fire me."

Trina stopped counting for one second and looked up. "They better fire all of us," she said, then went back to her pipette, voice steady again. "Because I signed the witness line."

Rao slid a printed chromatogram and three isotope charts across the table, then turned two autopsy photos faceup.

"Simple version," he said. "Your first scene and the river ramp were not one intact corpse moving through space."

Beau stared at the photos. "Then what are we looking at?"

"An assembly. At least three biological sources. One source reads long-buried mid-century." Rao moved one image closer to Lena.

Beau pointed to the second chart. "And the rest?"

"One reads modern dietary isotope profile. The third is partial and degraded, and I can't age it cleanly yet."

"Show me the cut points," Lena said.

"Here." He traced the image with a capped pen. "This femur has parallel machine kerfs, and this torso seam mixes black surgical nylon with white funeral thread. The assembler packed restorative wax into the seam to hide the color break."

Beau swore softly. "Assembled like what?"

"Like someone with access and time, not someone in a panic. Long bone cuts are clean. Soft tissue was rehydrated before placement."

Lena looked up. "With what?"

"They wired cartilage to old bone, then brushed cavity dye over it to even the tone. Chemistry says handling happened within days, not decades."

Lena looked down at the photo until the edges blurred. The wax in the seam had trapped lint from a blue transport blanket. She swallowed acid, gripped the steel table lip, and read the chemical list twice. The list included ethanol, methanol, quaternary ammonium disinfectant, lanolin-based restorative wax, and the dye carrier used in cavity fluid.

"These are funeral chemicals," she said.

"Some of them," Rao said. "Not all. The blend is odd. It is not textbook embalming and not hospital preservation. It is hybrid work, improvised with whatever was available."

He pointed to one peak on the chromatogram. "This marker should not be in remains buried in 1963 and left alone. It degrades fast in soil. I can say with high confidence it was introduced recently."

The room was so cold her skin pebbled, but sweat still gathered at her back under the vest. Outside the steel door someone rolled a gurney past, wheels squealing on one bad bearing, and then it went quiet again.

Lena said, "Say it the way I'd have to say it on record."

Rao met her eyes. "On record: Evidence supports deliberate postmortem manipulation and mixed-source staging."

Beau cut in. "And the ghost part?"

"No evidence supports supernatural recurrence."

Beau let out a breath he had been storing. "Thank Christ."

"Don't thank anybody yet," Rao said. "If this gets in front of the wrong camera before we map source routes, somebody will spin this as desecration by whoever they already planned to blame."

As if cued, Lena's phone buzzed on the table with a command-channel text: _SHERIFF NEEDS YES/NO FOR NOON PRESS. PRELIM PACKET BY 10:00. KEEP MESSAGE DISCIPLINED._

Rao held still. Trina kept counting drops in a whisper.

Lena stared at the screen until it dimmed, then locked it without replying.

Beau rubbed a thumb over the edge of his soda lid until it cracked. "He's going to bury this if he can pin it on chain noise."

"He might try," Lena said. "He can't un-run the chemistry."

Rao pulled another tray forward. "There is more. I went into the old freezer box where they held Isaiah's documented remains after the first-scene intake."

He corrected himself with a grimace. "After intake last week. There were fiber transfers in the drawer track. Cotton-poly blend with crematory ash and diesel particulate. That drawer has not left this building. The fabric did."

"From what?" Lena asked.

"Transport blanket, likely. Cheap fleet issue. I sampled it."

Beau said, "County van blankets are that blend. So are half the funeral homes."

"Exactly," Rao said. "The science gives direction, not identity. I can tell you this took handling, knowledge, and a route. I can't tell you the hands yet."

Lena pulled the old cardboard file toward her and held it by the edges. The pages were missing and the seam was torn. Whoever had access to this building knew which years mattered and which forms would have made this easier to prove.

Her mother had looked straight through her at the fish fry when 1963 came up, hands deep in batter, talking about catfish oil temperature like it was scripture. _Some papers should have burned_, Ida had said. Lena had heard warning; maybe it had been grief.

"Can you tie the modern chemistry to supplier lots?" Lena asked.

Rao nodded once. "Maybe. I need purchase records from county crematory inventory and private funeral transfer logs for at least the last six months. And I need them before somebody edits the spreadsheet history."

Beau gave a short laugh. "You're asking for a unicorn and a warrant."

"Ask anyway," Rao said. "Also, keep this off email."

He pushed a folded page toward Lena with handwritten columns in pencil. The page listed brand abbreviations and partial lot codes, and two chemical families were circled. At the bottom he had written one sentence in all caps: _IF YOU GO PUBLIC EARLY, THEY WILL PICK THE WRONG MONSTER ON PURPOSE._

Lena put the page in her notebook. The paper was warm from his hand.

Trina sealed the last cassette and finally looked up fully. Her voice was soft but carried. "My cousin works intake at Bell and Sons on weekends," she said. "She says county overflow has been coming through odd hours this month. It was not illegal, just odd. Like nobody wants daylight signatures."

Lena held her gaze. "What is your cousin's name?"

"Keisha Polk." Trina peeled off her outer gloves and flexed her cracked fingers. "She's saving for dental school and she hates all of us on principle. But she writes everything down."

"Would she talk?"

"If you ask like a person and not like command." Trina's mouth twitched. "And if you don't let this county hang it on the first Black family with a funeral sign."

No one answered for a beat. The compressor kicked on in the wall with a shudder.

Lena capped her pen. "Then that's the rule. Noon brief gets no family name and no funeral-home identifier until Rao corroborates lot codes from unopened bottles at both sites."

Beau's jaw flexed. "Clay's going to call that insubordination."

"He can," Lena said. "He can bench both of us by supper if he wants. I'm not handing him a Black scapegoat off half-proof."

She stood, knee stiff, and looked once more at the charts spread across steel. The rumors had given her one kind of dread: that the world was ripping at its own seams, that law and evidence were toys. Rao's data replaced that dread with a different one. This horror had been built with tools, invoices, access cards, and enough patience to lay body parts beside fresh murder victims and watch a county tear itself apart.

In one close-up, two skin tones met under a ridge of wax the color of candle fat. In another, fresh suture crossed an old burial fissure like boot laces cinched through old leather. Her stomach clenched so hard she had to lean into the table.

A ghost would have been easier.

She gathered the packet and turned to Beau. "We start with inventory and transport. Quietly. If Clay asks, we're still verifying sample integrity."

Beau nodded. "I can pull fuel-card pings if dispatch has not scrubbed them yet."

"Pull first," she said. "Then tell me what we're allowed to know."

---

By noon the thunderheads had climbed up and gone nowhere, the sun trapped behind them and the air hotter for it. Lena and Beau crossed the courthouse annex lot with paper folders under plastic and sweat at their collars. In the far corner, two stray dogs slept under a county maintenance truck because the truck still dripped cool water from its A/C line.

Purchasing sat on the second floor above tax appeals, behind glass that made everyone look underwater. Darlene Fitch worked the counter in a navy cardigan despite the heat and wore metal finger splints on both index fingers from a quilting injury she talked about before anyone asked. She kept a row of cat photos clipped to her monitor and corrected their names when people called them strays.

Lena passed over her badge and request sheet. "I need chemical lot purchases tied to county crematory operations and overflow transfers, six-month window."

Darlene read the request, then looked at Lena with real sympathy that did not help. "I can release aggregate totals today. Named vendor lines and lot-level detail are restricted under litigation hold as of this morning."

"By whose order?"

"Sheriff's legal office." Darlene lowered her voice. "Hold notice came in at 8:12. If I open that folder without clearance, I lose this desk and my benefits."

Beau leaned in, elbows on the counter. "Darlin', we don't need a sermon. We need the raw file before it gets polished."

"Then you needed to file before hold time." She slid a printed summary through the slot. "This is what policy lets me release without a termination letter."

The sheet was thin and bland by design: monthly totals, no names, no lot numbers. Clay's command text flashed in her head: KEEP MESSAGE DISCIPLINED. She forced her eyes to the only line that could still move the case. Preservative and restoration compounds had spiked threefold in August, while official death intake stayed flat.

Lena tapped the column. "What changed in August?"

Darlene gave a tight shrug. "Compressor failure at county crematory in July, then temporary overflow routing. That's what the memo said."

"Where is the memo?"

"In the hold folder."

Beau muttered, "Convenient as hell."

Darlene glanced toward the back office and lowered her voice again. "If you go to Logistics, ask for paper. Portal history got 'cleaned' after breakfast."

Lena nodded once. "Thank you."

"I didn't help you," Darlene said, but she tapped twice on the cat photo labeled _Biscuit_, a nervous habit she seemed not to notice. "I told you which office still keeps paper."

Logistics was three blocks away in a converted feed warehouse that still smelled like grain under the bleach. The clerk at dispatch refused them at first, then sent them to the loading coordinator, then to records, then back to dispatch again. Each desk said policy. Each desk said come back with formal approval. By the third loop Beau's jaw had set hard enough to show a pulse below his ear. Lena felt the trap in her teeth: stall, exhaust, refuse. She kept her face blank and signed the next routing slip anyway.

They finally got a portal printout after forty minutes and two signatures. It showed spotless transfers straight to county crematory, no detours, every timestamp rounded to quarter hours. At the bottom, a system footer read LAST MODIFIED 08:17.

"They're running us in circles until somebody at command calls," he said.

The loop felt engineered to burn daylight and hand them clean lies. Her father had left her one useful courthouse rule: trust what still stains your fingers. "Then we stop asking on their path." Lena handed the printout back. "This is scrubbed. We go older than the portal." She scanned the wall above dispatch and found what she wanted: old metal cabinets, color tabs, years hand-labeled in marker. The county had digitized everything except what was too annoying to scan.

The oldest clerk in the room had watched every loop without once looking surprised. Lena trusted that kind of stillness more than the portal printout, so she walked to him. He wore hearing aids and a Saints lanyard and had been silently sharpening pencils with a pocketknife. His tag read B. Whitaker.

"Mr. Whitaker," Lena said, "where are the carbon manifests for overflow transfer runs? The blue copy, not the white."

He looked at her badge, then at Beau, then back at her. "Who told you to ask that?"

"Nobody official."

"Good answer." He stood with a wince and led them to a cabinet jammed half shut by humidity-swollen paper. "Blue copies stick because the ink is waxy. Folks hate pulling them. That means they're usually still here."

Whitaker wore one knitted glove though it was ninety-eight degrees. He caught Lena looking and said, matter-of-fact, "Neuropathy. One hand burns, one hand goes numb. July and August are in drawer three. You didn't get this from me."

The manifests were smudged and old-school, route by route, driver initials, odometer start and stop, vehicle number, destination code. Lena and Beau took photos as fast as the office printer coughed toner dust nearby.

Three things surfaced inside ten minutes, and one of them looked too neat to trust.

First: Vehicle 14B, the county refrigerated van a gin mechanic had flagged on day one, had made unauthorized after-hours stops on both nights before the two murder scenes. Dispatch logged it as "overflow transfer," not scene transport.

Second: Destination codes that should have read only county crematory included repeated hand-written detours to B&S-2, Bell and Sons back receiving bay, 01:12 a.m., 02:07 a.m., times that kept the truck off main roads and out of daylight.

"Could be planted," Beau said. "Fresh ink, clean story."

"Don't trust it yet," Lena said. She pulled the duplicate sheet underneath. The B&S-2 detour had bitten through all three carbon layers in the same slant and pressure. It had not been added later. She nodded once. "Now cross-check."

Beau opened the fleet-card audit feed on his phone, scrolled through raw vendor pings, and held the screen out. Two diesel swipes from 14B hit within five minutes of those detour times.

Third: The signatures on those detour lines were initialed H.R. once, then scratched over, then replaced with generic shift initials no one in dispatch claimed.

Beau stared at the page long enough to leave sweat spots at the margin. "H.R. can be half this damn building."

"Not on that route," Whitaker said quietly from behind them. "Route 14B requires deputy escort authorization after midnight. That changed last month."

Lena turned. "Changed by who?"

Whitaker slid his pocketknife closed and tucked it away. "Bulletin came from sheriff ops with Judge Pike's emergency-facilities attachment. Stuck in my head because it used three signatures where one would've done."

Beau cursed under his breath. "Judge Pike does not write transport policy."

Whitaker gave him a look that was almost pity. "Son, out here policy follows money. Always has."

A phone rang at dispatch. No one moved to answer it.

The unanswered ring told Lena exactly where the next order would come from. She copied the memo reference number from the cabinet index and felt her pulse shift into that cold, narrow band she got when the shape of a thing emerged. It still was not solved, but now it had edges.

She called Rao from the hallway. He picked up on the second ring.

"Tell me you got lot codes," he said.

"Not yet. We got routes, detours, and a spike in preservative purchases. Overflow was routed through Bell and Sons back bay at night."

Silence for a beat. "That would explain your hybrid chemistry. County stock plus private stock in one handling chain."

"Can you match your compounds to county-only versus funeral-home-only formulations?"

"Partially. If I can get one unopened reference bottle from each site, yes." Rao exhaled hard. "Lena, be careful with how you write this. Bell and Sons employs half that family. If command wants a fast villain, this is tidy enough to weaponize."

"I know." She watched rain begin in huge drops that hit hot asphalt and vanished before they pooled. "Noon packet gets a corroboration hold and no Bell surname. Clay can write me up."

"Good," Rao said. "Because the data says network. It is not one person. It is not one bloodline. It is a network of access."

She hung up and found Beau leaning against the cinderblock wall under a broken fan. He had taken his tie off and wrapped it around one hand, twisting it tight and loose, tight and loose.

"You're thinking about Ruth Bell," he said.

Lena had not said her name out loud. "I'm thinking about what this county does when it gets a chance to recycle blame."

"Yeah." Beau looked out at the rain starting to stick now, darkening the lot in coin-sized spots. "And I'm thinking if H.R. matches who I think, this isn't just a body job. It's command cover."

"It has been command cover since day one," Lena said. "Now we can prove pieces of it."

They went back inside and pulled every blue-copy manifest for six weeks. The stack grew thick enough to bend the folder clips. A pattern repeated: late-night reroutes, unsigned corrections, missing digital mirrors for paper runs. The more ordinary the paperwork looked, the uglier it became.

At four-thirty, with thunder finally cracking over the levee, Lena drove the stack to Rao in a banker box that smelled like wet cardboard. He met them at the morgue side door with his sleeves rolled and a surgical cap still on his head, as if he had forgotten it there.

"I can work from this," he said.

"Can you get me language by morning that survives cross-exam?" Lena asked.

"If nobody burns the lab down tonight, yes."

Beau handed him a photocopy of the route sheet with the scratched initials. "You didn't get this from me."

Rao took it and looked from one of them to the other. "You both look like hell."

"That means we're on schedule," Beau said.

Lena did not laugh. She watched Rao carry the box inside and thought about the torn pages again, how neat the removal had been. The removal read as planning, not rage. Whoever ran this scheme treated history like inventory, pulling what they needed and discarding what complicated the sale.

By the time she got back to her car, rain hammered the roof hard enough to drown the cicadas. Her phone lit with three missed calls from command and one from her mother that lasted nine seconds and left no voicemail.

She sat with her hand on the wheel and let the rain drum through the metal.

The county had wanted a ghost story because ghost stories did not need subpoenas. Ghost stories did not have fuel cards, shift codes, and procurement spikes. Ghost stories did not implicate offices with letterhead.

What Rao gave her carried two truths at once: people had done this, and people left receipts.

She looked at the missed calls again and knew morning would start on courthouse steps, not in a lab.


# Chapter 5

By ten the next morning, the courthouse steps sounded like a boiler with a cracked valve. Chant rose against chant, whistles against bullhorns, and somewhere in the middle somebody had put a snare drum on a folding chair and kept beating the same roll until it turned into a headache for half the block.

Lena stood inside the metal barricade corridor with sweat running down her spine under her vest and counted bodies the way she counted shell casings: fast, again, then one more time because the first count was never enough. Fifty-two on the west side by the veterans memorial. Forty-eight on the east side under the dead magnolia. Five deputies between them and two city uniforms who looked nineteen and scared of their own batons.

The sun sat straight overhead, white and blunt. Heat came off the asphalt in visible waves, and the paint on the bike-lane stripes smelled sweet and chemical, like new toys left in a hot trunk. Cicadas screamed from the oaks beyond the jail annex and never once took a breath.

"We're about one shove from a pileup," Beau said beside her. He had taken off his tie and stuffed it in his back pocket, where it hung like a surrender flag. "I hate to be old and prophetic, but this is how every bad shrimp boil starts. Too many cousins, not enough shade."

"Keep west side six feet off the rails," Lena said. She kept her voice flat and procedural because it helped her think. "No chemical spray unless I call it. If they surge, we split at the fountain, not at the stairs."

"Copy." He scratched the edge of his jaw and glanced at the crowd. "You ate anything?"

"Coffee."

"That ain't food, partner."

She did not answer. She had not slept more than two hours, and coffee sat in her stomach like a fist.

A woman in a denim vest leaned over the west barricade and shouted, "You all protect killers!" She held a hand-painted sign that said SAY ISAIAH'S NAME and had a cartoon river painted in blue nail polish. The polish had cracked in the heat, and she kept rubbing the edge of the poster with her thumb like she could smooth it back to perfect.

Across from her, a man in a camo cap yelled, "Do your damn jobs and lock somebody up!" His sign said LAW AND ORDER NOW in machine-cut vinyl letters that had not been made in his garage.

They were not shouting at each other anymore. They were shouting at Lena.

Her name moved through both groups in different tones and landed the same way, sharp and personal. Waller. Waller. Detective Waller is hiding something. Detective Waller is the only honest cop left. Waller's dirty. Waller's brave. Waller's bought. Waller's one of us. Waller's not.

A plastic water bottle flew from the east side and hit the barricade hard enough to ring. A deputy flinched and grabbed for his belt spray.

"Hands off," Lena snapped.

He froze. "Yes, ma'am."

On the courthouse landing, Sergeant Aisha Lane adjusted the elastic on her riot helmet and spat sunflower seed shell into a paper cup she carried for that purpose. She had quit smoking nine months ago and told everybody who asked that seeds were cheaper than nicotine patches and less likely to make her punch a lieutenant. She caught Lena's eye and tapped two fingers toward the north corner.

Lena moved that way, shoulders turned sideways to slip through the corridor. The crowd smell changed every three steps: sunscreen, beer sweat, incense, stale cigarettes, somebody's vanilla body spray, somebody else's fear.

A college kid in a press vest held up his phone and said, "Detective Waller, are you suppressing suspect names?"

"I'm not discussing active witness interviews," she said.

"That's not what I asked."

"Then ask a better question."

He blinked, half offended and half thrilled to have something quotable.

At the corner, Lane handed Lena a folded sheet torn from a copier ream. "They're passing these out on both sides," Lane said. "Not from one group. Both."

Lena unfolded it.

The paper showed her house in a satellite screenshot, roofline outlined in red. HER ADDRESS in bold black font. Side yard circled. Front door circled. It listed Marcus's full name and Jalen's school with the dismissal time in parentheses.

Under it: IF SHE WON'T PROTECT THE COUNTY, PROTECT YOURSELVES.

For one second Lena did not hear the crowd at all. Sound dropped out like somebody had packed her ears with mud. She only saw the front step of her house, the chipped brick where Jalen had fallen at seven and split his lip, the cheap motion light she kept meaning to replace.

Then noise came back all at once.

"Where'd you pull this?" she asked.

"North lot," Lane said. "Little blond guy in an American flag fishing shirt. I got his photo. Couldn't keep him. Crowd swallowed him."

"Send it to my secure folder and to Beau." Lena folded the flyer into quarters so tight her knuckles hurt. "And get a unit to my house now. Marked car out front, one in back alley. I want body-cam rolling before they get there."

"Already done. I sent Ortega and Finch." Lane paused. "You want me to call your people?"

"I got it."

Her hands were shaking. She pressed the flyer flat against her thigh until they stopped enough to tap Marcus's number.

He picked up on the second ring. "I was about to call you."

"Tell me where Jalen is."

"At home. Why?"

"Get him out. Right now."

A beat. "Lena, what happened?"

"My address is on flyers in a crowd of five hundred angry people. They're using his school schedule as a threat. Put him in the car and go to your sister's place in Greenwood or my mother's if Ida's home. Don't stay there waiting on me."

Marcus breathed once through his nose, the way he did when he was trying not to swing from calm to rage in one jump. "Is there a car there?"

"Two units en route."

"That helps exactly none if somebody's already at the porch."

"Marcus. Move."

"We're moving." His voice hardened. "You understand this is done. This is not normal ugly. This is over-the-line ugly."

She knew what he meant and hated him for saying it out loud because he was not wrong.

"Go," she said.

When she hung up, she found Beau watching her face.

"Flyer?" he asked.

She showed him.

His jaw tightened. "Jesus Christ." He looked toward the steps where media cameras crowded the tape line. "That's personnel file data. School schedule too. That didn't come from Facebook gossip."

"I know." 

"You want me to pull comms and request cyber?"

"Clay'll slow-roll it if he thinks it touches his office." She looked over Beau's shoulder at the crowd where three different hands now waved the same sheet of paper. "Get Lane's photo to Mara Quinn. She can run facial match faster than our unit if they decide this is politically inconvenient."

He gave her a long look. "You want to say that part again into a recorder?"

"Not yet."

A horn blast ripped through the corridor. Somebody on the east side shoved hard enough to topple a barricade section. Metal crashed. Three people screamed, not from injury, from surprise and the sudden animal certainty that everyone else might run.

Lena was moving before thought finished. She grabbed one end of the barrier, shoulder burning, and shoved it upright while Beau and two deputies locked the feet back in place. The camo-cap man lunged forward with his sign pole like a spear. On the west side, the denim-vest woman swung her poster board and caught him across the mouth. Teeth clicked. Blood appeared quick and bright on his lower lip.

"Back up!" Lena yelled. She put her body between them, palms out. "Both of you, now. You move again and you're in cuffs."

"He came at me!" the woman yelled.

"She hit me!" the man shouted through blood.

"I don't care who started it. I care who stops. Back up."

The woman stepped back first, chest heaving. The man hesitated, then spat red on the pavement and retreated two steps. It was enough. The wave that had started to curl broke and flattened into shouting again.

Lane arrived with two more officers and took over the line, seeds cracking between her teeth as if she had all day.

A voice called Lena's name from behind the press rope.

Mara Quinn stood on the courthouse lawn with a camera sling across her chest and a notebook clipped to the back pocket of her jeans. She was wearing old running shoes with orange laces that never matched anything else she owned, because she said she could outrun cops in them when she had to. She held up one of the doxxing flyers in a plastic evidence sleeve.

"You want this on record?" Mara asked.

"Not from me," Lena said.

"Then off record: I already got two names from print-shop watermarks. One of them's a county campaign vendor."

"Send it encrypted."

"You're still allowed to receive email?"

Lena looked at the courthouse doors where Clay's aide waited with her phone pressed to her ear, eyes locked on Lena like she was counting down a bomb.

"For now," Lena said.

Mara glanced toward the aide. "You getting hauled upstairs?"

"Emergency command hearing."

"About crowd control or about you refusing to give them a sacrificial suspect by dinner?"

"Both."

Mara's mouth tightened. "You need anything from me before they shut your badge in a drawer?"

The sentence hit harder than it should have because it sounded practical, not dramatic.

"Track who paid for these flyers," Lena said. "And if my house gets touched, I want faces before I want sympathy."

"You'll get both anyway," Mara said. "Faces first."

The aide cut through the rope and stopped three feet from Lena, breathless and composed at the same time. Assistant City Manager Renata Bell wore a yellow cardigan despite the heat because office air-conditioning gave her migraines. A dark damp line ran down her spine, and she ignored it.

"Detective Waller," Bell said, "the mayor and sheriff require you in conference room two immediately."

"I require ten more minutes out here," Lena said.

Bell shook her head. "The hearing started without you."

Beau muttered, "That ain't a hearing. That's a sentence with furniture."

Lena handed Lane the radio channel and climbed the courthouse steps with the doxxing flyer still folded in her fist so tight the paper edge cut her palm.

---

Conference room two had the same fluorescent buzz as every county room where bad decisions got laundered into policy. A long table ran nearly the length of the room, and water pitchers sweated onto legal pads nobody read. A state flag stood in one corner while a television on mute showed live footage from outside: signs, crowd, Lena's own face in a tight shot she had never consented to.

Mayor Evelyn Tate sat at the head of the table in a navy suit that did not wrinkle even in August. She kept pinching the edge of a nicotine patch under her sleeve, a little half-inch adjustment every time someone said the word liability. Sheriff Clay Reddick sat two seats down, jacket buttoned, tie centered, expression patient enough to be insulting.

City attorney Glenn Weems had three yellow highlighters lined up by color and had already capped one with his teeth hard enough to crack plastic.

"Detective Waller," Tate said, "thank you for joining us."

Clay gave a courtly nod. "We're all on the same team here."

Lena sat and put the folded flyer on the table in front of her.
The vinyl chair grabbed the backs of her thighs. She could still hear the crowd chanting her name in dueling tones, praise and accusation striking the same spot under her ribs.

"Are we?" she asked.

Tate glanced at the paper and then away. "We'll handle personal security, but first I need this county steady by the noon cycle."

Clay folded his hands. "People are scared. They need to see movement and hear one disciplined message."
Disciplined, she thought, the way men in pressed shirts described panic they wanted pointed somewhere else.

"Disciplined isn't the same as true," Lena said.

Weems set down the cracked highlighter. "Truth is trial language. Noon is liability language."
"If command looks split, every plaintiff's lawyer in the state starts drafting."

"Good," Lena said. "Operationally, we don't have probable cause for an arrest that survives first appearance. We still have a chain-of-custody breach at the morgue corridor and a likely internal leak of protected personnel data. If you push a name today, defense shreds it before lab reports finish."

"With respect, Detective, nobody is asking for a bad arrest."

"Then quit calling it a narrowed suspect field at four." Lena tapped the flyer. "That's pressure in nicer wrapping. By noon my kid's school schedule was in a crowd chant."

Tate's jaw flexed. "Your family protection is moving now."
"By noon I need this county looking governable."

"Then quit feeding a narrative that says I'm hiding suspects."

Clay smiled without warmth. "You're personalizing institutional management again, Detective. That is part of the concern."

She looked at him. "It's personal when my address comes out of your personnel files."

The mayor held up a hand. "Enough. Here's the sequence. Sheriff Reddick holds four p.m. press availability: active leads, narrowed field, no speculation. Detective Waller, you stand with him and reinforce message discipline."

"No," Lena said.
The word left her before she could dress it in policy language, blunt as a slammed door.

Nobody moved for a full second.

Weems cleared his throat. "Counsel needs clarity. Are you refusing a lawful supervisory directive?"

"I won't stand in front of cameras and imply we have a suspect field when we don't." She kept her voice level and heard herself doing it, heard the old academy habit of building a staircase of clauses before jumping. "I'll brief what's true: two current homicides linked by staged historical remains, breach points in county custody, and active witness interviews that aren't ready for public release. I won't sell closure I can't prove."

Clay's fingers tapped once on the table, then stilled. "You're not being asked to sell closure. You're being directed to support command decisions in a volatile environment."

"Command decisions that manufacture consent for a bad arrest are still bad arrests."

Tate looked at Clay, then at Lena. "Detective, choose your next sentence carefully."

Lena thought of the flyer. She thought of Jalen's backpack by the front door and the chipped brick step and the way fear had tunneled through her chest so fast it felt electrical.

"Then put this in your notes," she said. "If you need optics more than evidence, suspend me now and own that on camera."

Weems made a small disbelieving noise. Clay did not smile anymore.

"You're out of line," Clay said.

"I'm in the line where perjury starts."

The mayor spoke as if reading from an internal memo she had drafted on the drive over. "By authority of the city public safety emergency order and in consultation with elected county command, Detective Lena Waller is suspended from investigative duty pending immediate internal review for insubordination and unauthorized public contradiction of command messaging. Effective now."

The room went very still. Fluorescent buzz got louder.

Clay slid a form across the table. "Sign acknowledgment."

Lena read enough to see the words REMOVAL OF ACCESS PRIVILEGES and CONTACT RESTRICTIONS WITH ACTIVE CASE WITNESSES without counsel present.

"You're restricting witness contact?" she asked.

"Standard integrity protection," Weems said.

"No," Lena said. "Standard integrity protection would investigate whoever leaked my home address from personnel files."

Clay's voice turned almost gentle, which was how he sounded when he wanted his threat to pass as concern. "You need to cool off, Detective. You're too close to this one."

"I'm close because you put me on it and then kneecapped every clean lead."

"Sign the form," Tate said.

Lena signed.

Outside the room, cameras waited at the end of the hall because county buildings leaked faster than roofs in hurricane season. She could have walked past in silence. She could have preserved the thin fiction that this was temporary and procedural.

She stopped.

A reporter shouted, "Detective Waller, are you off the case?"

"I was suspended for refusing to announce an arrest without probable cause," Lena said.

Clay's voice came from behind her, calm and amplified for microphones. "Detective Waller is on administrative pause while we streamline operations for public safety."

She turned toward the cameras. "If your kid's name is in these flyers, that's not streamlined. That's reckless."

Gasps, scribbling, phones rising.

Clay stepped up beside her and put one hand lightly on her elbow as if he were guiding a relative to a chair. His smile for cameras was almost fatherly.

"No further comment," he said.

By the time she reached the detective bullpen to clear out her desk, her key card was dead and flashed red with a single beep.

Officer Chaney from IT stood there with a cardboard archive box and red eyes from allergies or shame. He collected novelty spoons from every county fair in the state and kept one from Tupelo in his shirt pocket as a lucky charm. It flashed when he shifted his weight.

"I hate this part," he said quietly.

"Then don't perform it like theater," Lena said, then softened because he looked nineteen under the fluorescent flicker though he was thirty if a day. "Sorry. Not at you."

"I know." He swallowed. "I've gotta witness badge turn-in and device surrender."

She placed her badge, department phone, and access fob in the box. The absence of weight on her belt felt like missing a tooth. She kept touching the empty spot with the side of her hand.

Beau appeared at the end of the hall, stopped when he saw the box, and did not come closer because cameras were still in the stairwell.

"I'll call you tonight," he said.

Clay's voice carried from the briefing room: "Detective Broussard, inside, now."

Beau shook his head once, tiny, like a man checking if a bridge cable might hold.

"Keep your phone loud," he said to Lena, then turned away.

She walked out of the building without a badge, without air-conditioning, without the protection of being a problem someone still had to include.

---

At dusk her street looked normal from a distance, which made it worse.

Kids rode scooters two blocks over. A dog barked behind a chain-link fence. Somebody grilled chicken with too much lighter fluid and sent a sweet petroleum smell down the block. Storm clouds stacked purple over the levee, but no rain came.

Closer to her house, two patrol cars sat where she had asked, one at the curb and one in the alley. Both officers were alert and embarrassed, which Lena recognized because she had worn that expression herself outside other people's disasters.

The front porch light was on. Marcus's car was gone.

She let herself inside with the house key she still had even if the county had stripped everything else.

Marcus sat at the kitchen table with an open duffel bag between his feet. He wore scrub pants and an old Delta Regional hoodie with bleach freckles on the sleeves. He worked nights as a respiratory therapist and still smelled faintly of hospital sanitizer and coffee from vending machines that never got cleaned. When he was scared, he lined bottle caps by color along the table edge without noticing he was doing it. Six blue, four green, one red.

Jalen stood by the sink in sock feet, phone in hand, jaw tight in the way teenagers pretend is indifference. He had grown two inches this summer and moved like his body was still surprising him.
Looking at him in that kitchen light, she saw both the child who used to sleep in the laundry basket and the almost-man strangers could read as a threat.

"You made it," Marcus said.

"Where were you going?"

"My sister's. He didn't want to go." Marcus jerked his chin toward Jalen. "Said if somebody wants to come here, they can come while he's here too."

"I'm not hiding at Aunt Trina's because idiots printed paper," Jalen said.

"Watch your mouth," Marcus said automatically.

"No, let him swear," Lena said. "He's earned it today."

Jalen looked at her then, properly looked, and his shoulders dropped half an inch. "Are we actually in danger or is this cop danger where everybody acts dramatic for TV?"

Lena pulled the flyer from her pocket and set it on the table.
The paper was damp from her palm, soft at the folds where she'd crushed it all afternoon.

Jalen read silently. His face changed in stages. First annoyance. Then disbelief. Then the flat stare of someone trying not to give adults the fear they were obviously asking for.
Watching fear lock into his jaw hurt worse than losing her badge in front of cameras.

"They got my school," he said.

"Yeah."

"Did your people do this?"

She should have lied. She should have said no, impossible, outside agitators, anything clean enough for a son.

"It used county data," she said.

Jalen laughed once without humor. "Cool. Great employer."

Marcus rubbed his eyes with thumb and forefinger. "I called Felicia Kemp."

"The labor attorney?"

"The one you said knew internal discipline cases and juvenile protection orders. She'll call in fifteen."

Lena set her hands on the counter because if she sat she might not stand back up tonight.

"I got suspended," she said.

Marcus stared. "Today?"

"Effective immediately."

"For what?"

"I told them I wouldn't stand in front of cameras and fake an arrest timeline."

Jalen let out a low whistle. "So you got fired for not lying."

"Suspended," Lena said.

"Same shape, different word."

Marcus pushed the bottle caps into a pile hard enough to scatter them again. "Lena, listen to me. You're done with this until they clean their mess. You hear me? No side calls. No hero bullshit. You already gave them your house, your kid's school, and now your paycheck."

His voice cracked on paycheck. That was the honest part.

She thought of the credit-union balance she never opened on days like this. Twelve thousand four hundred dollars hanging over everything, monthly auto-withdrawal waiting like a clock.

"You think I gave them this?" she asked.

"I think they will burn you and sleep fine." Marcus stood, palms flat on the table. "I don't care about your oath right now. I care that my son does not get grabbed outside algebra because somebody wants to scare a detective straight."

Jalen spoke without looking up from the flyer. "Can I stay with Grandma tonight? She'll make me shell peas until midnight and I won't doom-scroll."

The request relieved her enough that it hurt.

"Yeah," Lena said. "We'll take you."

She moved through the house with Marcus at her shoulder and checked every lock in order: front deadbolt, back deadbolt, window latches, alley gate chain. Marcus packed Jalen's inhaler, charger, two shirts, and a pair of jeans into the duffel and texted Ida their route with a pickup code word.
By the time Lena tested the back-door lock a second time, her hands shook hard enough that the key scraped paint off the plate. She bit the inside of her cheek until she tasted copper and forced her breathing to slow.

Her phone buzzed with an unknown number. She answered on speaker.

"Ms. Waller, this is Felicia Kemp." The voice was low and clipped, precise as a stapler. "I am in my office with my shoes off and a charley horse in my left calf, so if I sound irritable, that is because pain is not an abstract concept."

Marcus mouthed told you.

"I appreciate the call," Lena said.

"I reviewed your suspension memo. Don't contact active witnesses, crime-scene personnel, or assigned detectives without counsel present. The sheriff's office drafted broad language on purpose. If you violate it, they can recommend an obstruction inquiry."

"Can they enforce that off duty?"

"They can make your life expensive while arguing they can." A paper rustle. "If you're seen directing investigative steps while suspended, defense counsel will argue witness contamination and retaliatory motive."

"What about family protection?"

"I filed an emergency request for directed patrol and records audit. It may move quickly if your press clip keeps trending. It may stall if command decides silence helps optics. I don't bill for pessimism, detective, but I include it free."

Jalen snorted despite himself.

Marcus zipped Jalen's duffel and set it by the door.

Lena asked, "If a journalist sends me documents independently obtained through public records, am I barred from reading them?"

Kemp paused. "Reading isn't directing. But if your fingerprints are on acquisition strategy, opposing counsel will say you used media as a proxy investigator."

"Noted."

"You need sleep and a written log of every non-family contact starting now," Kemp said. "Time stamps. Method. Topic. Keep it boring. Boring keeps you out of jail."

"Understood."

"I'm not your mother, but I'm about to sound like her. Eat something with protein before your blood sugar murders your judgment."

The line clicked dead.

Marcus stared at Lena. "You heard her."

"I did."

"Say it back."

"No witness contact. No directing. Log everything." She rubbed the cut in her palm where the flyer had bitten. "I heard her."

A knock sounded at the back door. Three quick taps, pause, two taps.

Mara's code from the year before when deputies had tried to seize her notebook in a parking lot.

Marcus muttered, "You have got to be kidding me," but stepped aside.

Mara came in carrying a grocery bag and her phone rig. Rain had not started, but humidity had curled sweat along her hairline. She set the bag on the table and pulled out a carton of eggs, a loaf of bread, and a brown envelope thick with printouts.

"I brought groceries so this doesn't look like conspiracy if somebody's filming through your hedge," she said. "Also, your hedge is dead on one side."

"Helpful," Lena said.

"I try." Mara nodded at Marcus and Jalen. "Hey."

Jalen gave a small wave and slipped down the hall to pack, because teenagers understood when adults were about to say things they would regret in front of a child.

Marcus crossed his arms. "You're press."

"Yes," Mara said. "And I'm not here for a quote."

He looked at Lena. "You just got warned by counsel."

"We're discussing public documents," Lena said.

"You're going to split that hair until it bleeds."

"Probably."

Mara opened the envelope. "Print-shop watermark confirms campaign vendor: Delta Meridian Strategies. Same vendor that handled reelection mailers for three county seats, including Clay's PAC. I also pulled bulk text logs from a protest organizer chat. Somebody pushed your address as a community alert at 9:12 this morning. Number routes through a prepaid account bought cash at a gas station on Highway 61."

As Mara talked, Lena crossed to the front window, counted both patrol units still posted, and texted Lane for a rolling pass every twenty minutes until midnight and a tail unit for Marcus's drive to Ida's. She had to wipe her palms on her jeans before her thumb stopped slipping on the screen.

Marcus said, "And this helps us how?"

"It helps because it gives us a path that doesn't wait on people who already decided Lena's expendable," Mara said.

Lena looked at the pages: names, time stamps, and vendor invoice fragments with blurred totals. Nothing was admissible yet, but the pattern had shape.

"I can't tell you where to go next," Lena said carefully.

"Good," Mara said. "Don't. But you can answer one thing as background. If I file open-records on sheriff campaign vendor payments and county emergency communications between seven and noon, am I fishing or am I on a dock?"

Lena met her eyes. "You're on a dock. Deep water."

Mara nodded once, decision made. Her silence lasted three seconds exactly, and Lena recognized it from the style of her thinking: risk calculation, ethics pass, then motion.

"Then I file tonight," Mara said. "And I call my editor before Clay calls his."

Marcus gave a bitter laugh. "Great. Everybody gets a strategy but my kid."

Lena turned to him. "Jalen goes to Ida's for three nights. Patrol stays here. You take day shifts if you can swap and keep pickup routine off social media. I won't post, tag, or answer unknowns."

"And you?"

"I stay visible enough that they don't think they can scare me into a scripted confession, and quiet enough that Felicia doesn't have to represent me in an obstruction hearing."

"That's not a plan."

"It's what we have."

Thunder rolled far off, long and low. Still no rain.

Mara repacked the envelope and slid one sheet back toward Lena: a public campaign-finance report with Clay's committee line item highlighted in green.

"I can leave this here," Mara said. "It's public. If anyone asks, I dropped off eggs and paperwork I pulled off the internet."

Lena pulled her suspension memo from the counter and laid it beside the doxxing flyer. "Take these too. Run both tonight with my name on them."

Marcus's head snapped toward her. "Lena, no."

She did not look away from Mara. "If this stays private, Clay buries it as an internal misunderstanding and drags me back only if I play nice. If this runs tonight, there's no quiet way back for me."

Mara watched her for a long second. "You sure?"

"Run it," Lena said.

Marcus did not smile. "I'm taking him now."

Lena nodded.

When Jalen came back with his duffel, he hugged her hard and fast, already turned away before she could decide what to say. He smelled like laundry soap and the peppermint gum he chewed when he was trying to act older than sixteen.

"Text me when you get there," she said.

"I'll lock both deadbolts, okay?" He looked toward Mara. "If you put my face on your site, I'll sue you myself."

"Noted," Mara said.

The house emptied in under six minutes; the front door shut, the car engine started, and tires scratched over gravel until the sound was gone.

Silence settled wrong, too large for the rooms.

Lena stood at the kitchen sink and watched the patrol car lights wash blue across her dead hedge. Her knee scar throbbed with the weather shift, old surgery metal warning storm before the sky did.

Mara rinsed two coffee mugs and set them upside down on a towel like she had lived there for years.

"You okay?" Mara asked.

"No." Lena touched the empty space on her belt again, then dropped her hand. "But I can still count."

"Count what?"

"Who benefits if I disappear from this case." Lena looked at the highlighted campaign line item and then at the doxxing flyer on the table, edges curled from sweat. "Today they took the badge. Tomorrow they try to take credibility. After that they try to take memory."

Mara leaned against the counter, arms folded. "Then we build memory where they can't seize it. Time stamps, documents, recordings, multiple copies."

Lena nodded.

She pulled a legal pad from the junk drawer, wrote the date and time at the top, and started a log the way Felicia told her: plain, boring, admissible. She logged her contact with Marcus Waller about safety relocation, her contact with Felicia Kemp about legal restrictions, her contact with Mara Quinn about public-records background discussion, and the authorization she had given for publication of the suspension memo and doxxing flyer.
Before midnight she pulled her father's notebook from the hall cabinet and stacked county request forms beside the pad, ready for office phones in the morning.

Outside, the crowd downtown was still audible in thin waves when the wind shifted, a far-off roar like river water moving through a narrowed channel.

Lena listened to it and kept writing, suspended and sweating in her own kitchen, case hottest it had ever been and now out of official reach, already building the only reach she had left.


# Chapter 6

By noon the next day the house had gone damp as a lung. The swamp cooler in Lena's window rattled and spat warm air that smelled faintly of bleach from the rag she had tied over the filter. She sat at the table with her father's notebook open to the index, legal pads spread under her elbows, and a stack of county request forms curling at the corners from sweat. Suspension left her with time and no badge, which turned out to be worse than no time at all.

She had called the chancery office at 8:04, 8:37, and 9:11. At 9:12 a clerk named Bernice answered with a voice like dry paper and told Lena she needed a signed owner-release form to view parcel transfer books older than twenty years.

"They're public land records," Lena had said. "I'm requesting books, not juvenile files."

"Ma'am, that's the policy now."

"Policy since when?"

"Since this morning," Bernice said, and hung up before Lena could ask who wrote it.

Lena drove to the courthouse annex anyway. The parking lot shimmered. A protest sign from the weekend had blown against the chain-link fence and bleached to a pink ghost of letters. Inside, the hallway lights buzzed over old tile and somebody had mopped with lemon disinfectant strong enough to sting her eyes.

At Window Three, a woman with silver cat-eye glasses and a hummingbird pin looked at Lena's forms, then at Lena, then at the line behind her. The woman's nameplate said T. RANSOM, and she kept one thumbnail wrapped in a strip of blue tape to keep herself from biting it.

"System migration," Ransom said. "No historical pull today."

"I called first. Nobody said migration."

"We're all learning the same time, Detective."

"I'm suspended."

Ransom blinked once. "Then we're both having a week."

She slid the forms back through the slot with a carbon-copy packet stapled on top and lowered her voice. "Try next Tuesday. Maybe the server's done pretending to die by then."

When Lena stepped out into the heat, her shirt stuck to her back at the scar line over her right knee. She sat in her car until the steering wheel cooled enough to touch and counted what she had left that the county could not lock: her father's notebook, a map from memory, and people who still answered when she called them as Lena instead of Detective Waller.

Ida had texted at ten-thirty: `Dinner at six. Real food. Bring your stubborn self and don't bring papers.`

Lena brought papers anyway, tucked in a grocery sack under the passenger seat.

Ida's house stood two streets over from the old elementary school, white siding gone cream with years and humidity. She had every porch light on before sunset, trying to push darkness back with electricity. Through the kitchen screen Lena heard gospel radio, oil popping in a skillet, and Ida singing one line ahead of the station in a low scratchy alto.

The kitchen smelled of onion, flour, and smoked turkey necks. Ida wore a green church dress she only pretended was casual and a pair of yellow dish gloves even though she was not washing anything. The gloves let her grip bowls without her arthritic fingers freezing halfway closed. She took one look at Lena and said, "You lost weight in your jaw. That's where worry sits on your side of the family."

"Hello to you too."

"Wash your hands and stop making jokes you don't mean."

Jalen was at the table with his geometry notebook open, one sneaker off, heel bouncing against a chair rung in a steady wooden thud. He had drawn a basketball court in the margin and kept darkening the free-throw line until the paper started to tear. A pair of cheap earbuds hung around his neck, one wire repaired with black electrical tape that he had wound too carefully to be temporary.

"Hey," Lena said.

"Hey." He did not look up right away. Teenagers could turn a single syllable into a whole weather system.

Marcus came in from the back porch carrying ice in a red cooler and an envelope tucked under his elbow. He still wore his work boots unlaced and the faded gray polo from Delta Fleet Services, collar sweat-dark and smelling faintly of diesel and peppermint gum. He had always chewed peppermint when he was angry because his father had quit cigarettes that way and never let anybody forget it.

"I grabbed your mom's bag of ice before the machine quit," he said. "Whole county's running on prayers and extension cords."

"Sit down," Ida said. "Nobody's fixing infrastructure in my kitchen."

For fifteen minutes they managed ordinary life. Ida fussed over whether the rice was too soft. Marcus told a story about a customer who tried to pay for brake pads with frozen venison. Jalen snorted tea through his nose laughing and swore he did not. Lena almost forgot where her phone was.

Then Marcus set the envelope beside Lena's plate.

"I need you to read this when we eat," he said. "It's from chancery or tax office or whichever office wakes up wanting money."

Ida gave him a look. "Can he get through one meal without paperwork?"

"I'm asking her to translate, not to chase anybody."

Lena turned the envelope over without opening it. The return stamp read MERCER COUNTY CHANCERY CLERK and the window showed block numbers she knew too well: GT-12-44, SUBDIVISION C. Her stomach tightened before she could tell herself not to jump.

"After dinner," she said.

"After dinner," Marcus agreed, but his jaw stayed set.

They bowed for grace. Ida thanked God for food, for breath, for children and grandchildren, and for the strength to tell the truth even when the truth did not make anybody safer tonight. She did not look at Lena while she said it.

Halfway through the meal Jalen pushed his peas into a green crescent and asked, "Are you still a cop if they took your badge?"

"I'm still me," Lena said. "Badge or not."

"That's not what I asked."

Marcus put his fork down quietly. Ida reached for hot sauce, missed by an inch, and tried again.

"I'm suspended," Lena said. "That means I can't work scenes right now."

"Because you did something wrong?"

"Because I didn't do what was convenient."

Jalen looked up then, eyes dry and bright the way they got when he had decided he was done being handled. "Can police lie to people?"

The fan over the stove clicked as it turned. Outside, cicadas started up in the pecan tree all at once, like somebody dragging metal teeth over a comb.

"In interviews," Lena said slowly, "they can bluff. They can say they have evidence they don't have yet. In reports and under oath, they can't. If they lie there, cases break and people get hurt."

"So they can lie if it's useful."

"It's supposed to be legal strategy, not anything they want."

"Did they lie about your address getting out?" Jalen asked. "Coach said everybody at school already knew where I sleep before I did."

Lena felt the back of her neck go hot. "Who told you that?"

"Everybody told me that."

Marcus leaned forward. "This is what I've been saying. He's twelve, Lena. He shouldn't hear adults arguing about chain of custody while he's trying to shoot free throws."

"I know his age."

"Then act like it. Step back until this blows over."

"It won't blow over."

"Then let somebody else hold it."

"Who? Clay? Hollis?"

"I don't care if it's a scarecrow with a badge," Marcus said, voice cracking at the edges for the first time. "I care that my son got walked to his car by a teacher because some man in a truck sat outside practice with his phone up. I care that he checks our mailbox with a baseball bat. I care that every time your name trends, his stomach goes bad."

Jalen stared at his plate and said, barely audible, "I don't check it with a bat anymore."

Nobody corrected him.

Ida folded her napkin once, precise, like she was grading papers again. "The county loves a Black woman in uniform right up to the minute she stops making things easier," she said. "After that they put her child on the bill with everybody else."

Lena looked at her mother. "You think I should quit too?"

"I think quitting and stopping are not the same thing," Ida said. "And I think men in this county have buried harm in file cabinets for sixty years and called it order. You being right won't stop a brick through your window."

Marcus turned to Ida. "So tell her. Tell her to walk away for now."

Ida shook her head. "I did not raise her to walk away. I raised her to count the cost before she steps. That's different."

Jalen finally met Lena's eyes. "If you keep doing it, can you at least stop saying we're fine? Just say we're not."

Lena had talked down armed men with less effort than it took to answer her son in that chair.

"We're not fine," she said. "We're not safe enough yet. I hate that sentence, but it's true."

He gave one short nod, which was not forgiveness and not rejection, just data accepted.

Jalen pushed his chair back so hard it barked across the linoleum. "Then I'm not sleeping at your place this week," he said, looking at Lena and nobody else. "I don't want to watch headlights slow down outside your house all night."

"Jalen-" Lena started.

"No, let him finish," Marcus said.

Jalen swallowed and kept going. "Don't come to practice either. Coach already has to walk me out. If you show up, they'll film that too."

The room went still except for the fan clicking over the stove. Ida set her fork down, stood, and started stacking plates with both gloved hands, one plate at a time because her fingers would lock if she rushed.

"I'll take him tonight and the rest of the week," Marcus said. "We can revisit Sunday."

Lena nodded once because anything else would have been a fight she could only lose in front of him.

When the dishes were stacked and the sink ran hot, Marcus caught Lena by the back door.

"I'm filing if this keeps climbing," he said. He did not raise his voice. That made it worse. "Temporary custody, full school transport, no press contact around him. Denise already drafted it."

Lena leaned against the doorframe, feeling old paint catch at the skin above her wrist. "You talked to a lawyer before talking to me."

"I talked to a lawyer because talking to you has not changed anything this week."

"You think I don't know the risk?"

"I think you know it like a detective. I need you to know it like his mother." He looked toward the kitchen where Jalen was laughing once, unexpectedly, at something Ida said. Marcus's voice dropped. "I still know you. That's why this is hard."

He walked out before she answered.

Ida wrapped leftovers in old butter tubs and pushed two into Lena's hands. "Eat tomorrow before coffee," she said. "Your stomach has gone mean on you."

"Mama-"

"No speeches tonight." Ida wiped the counter in small circles, fighting stiffness in her knuckles. "Your daddy used to bring home those chancery books in his head, all those numbers, and he'd sit right there and mutter GT this, GT that, like numbers were scripture. I asked him once what GT stood for and he said, 'Depends who's asking.'"

Lena froze with the container warm in her palm. "When did he say that?"

"Nineteen years ago? Twenty? I had my good knees and bad patience. Why?"

"Because I keep seeing GT in his notebook."

Ida's face did not change much, but her shoulders settled a fraction lower, a load shifting and landing where it belonged. "Then stop asking clerks who owe favors to men in office," she said. "Read what he left you."

The drive home took seven minutes. Lena sat in her parked car for ten more, watching a moth batter itself against her porch light. Her phone lit with a text from Marcus before she had shut the engine off.

`I'm serious about court if this turns uglier. I'm not bluffing.`

She set the phone face down and carried the grocery sack inside.

Her father's notebook had swollen from years in damp storage. The cardboard cover smelled of mildew and machine oil. Inside, his handwriting marched in narrow capitals with the same pressure on every letter; he had trained his pen the way he trained his face: give nobody extra information.

Lena cleared a space on the table and laid out three things: the notebook index she found in the flooded storage box, Marcus's chancery envelope, and a county plat atlas from 1998 that she had bought at a library discard sale for one dollar and a quarter. The atlas still had a blue sticker on the spine that read REFERENCE COPY.

She flipped to the old township map of the southern parcels. Section lines crossed like stitching. A thick rectangle was labeled GIDEON TRACT in faded block print, with subparcels marked C-1 through C-56. She went back to the notebook.

GT/12/44 - FENN
GT/09/31 - VOSS
GT/18/02 - HOLLER PET.
GT/04/07 - BELL, I. (OLD)

She had read those lines twenty times and treated GT like initials for a person or task force. Tonight the envelope window and Ida's memory moved the pieces in her head one notch to the right.

The code marked Gideon Tract, parcel 12-44 and parcel 09-31.

She pulled the atlas close enough that her breath dampened the paper and found C-44 near a drainage ditch south of levee road, then C-31 beside a collapsed grain spur. Voss and Fenn, both men dead this month, both tied in her father's private shorthand to specific Gideon parcels.

Lena got up, paced to the sink, came back, and opened her laptop even though she knew county portals would choke her out. She was right. The assessor site spun until it timed out. The clerk e-filing page returned a red banner: ACCESS RESTRICTED - CONTACT ADMINISTRATOR.

She laughed once, sharp and joyless, and reached for analog work.

In the bottom drawer she kept copies of public notices she clipped for no reason she could explain at the time. Tax sale ads, trustee substitutions, probate announcements in six-point font that most people used to line birdcages. She spread them across the table under a magnet lamp and ran her finger down columns until names started echoing.

The notice for parcel GT-09-31 showed three delinquent years, then a redemption by a holding company listed only as CROWN ACQUISITIONS LLC, with nothing but P.O. Box 447 and a registered-agent stamp blurred into ink fog. She pulled her stack of Pike Foundation vendor printouts and found two different entities that used that same box at different times. No officer names. No signature she could tie to a body in this case.

The notice for parcel GT-12-44 showed a quitclaim transfer through an estate dispute, but the witness line was a black smear and the book citation ended in a hand-marked correction: Chancery Book 92, page 611, see corrected page.

She checked the microfilm index she had photocopied last spring. Page 611 existed. Page 612 was logged as corrected. Next to both entries someone had stamped WITHDRAWN FOR AUDIT in purple block letters.

It was Chancery Book 92 again, and this time the trail stopped where names should have been.

Her father's notes had the same book number beside both entries, just written as B92 in tiny script at the margin like he did not want it noticed even by himself.

Lena felt the old investigative lift in her chest, the dangerous relief of pattern after days of static. She hated how good it felt. Pattern meant she was useful again. Pattern also meant Marcus was right about things getting uglier.

She called the chancery office one more time and got voicemail. Her message came out too calm.

"This is Lena Waller. I submitted records requests this morning and in person. I need Chancery Book 92, land transfer entries for Gideon Tract parcels zero-nine-thirty-one and twelve-forty-four. These are public records. I'm documenting access denial. Call me back tonight."

She hung up and stared at her own reflection in the black phone screen. Suspended detectives still sounded like detectives, even in voicemail they knew nobody would return.

At 9:43 p.m. Bernice from the morning call texted from an unknown number.

`Ms Waller stop leaving messages. Book room is closed until audit. don't make this harder.`

The text had no signature and no explanation of what audit or whose order. It was a soft threat in lowercase.

Lena typed back: `Who ordered the audit?`

Three dots appeared, disappeared, appeared again.

`You know who.`

She took a screenshot and printed it. Old habits made paper even now.

Then she tried one more route, calling the number of a retired title attorney who owed her a favor from a custody case three years back. Voicemail. She left details and got no call back.

Then she went back to the notebook and looked at the one line she had avoided because it felt like superstition when read too fast: GT/04/07 - BELL, I. (OLD).

It was not superstition. It was inventory.

Her father had mapped the tract because he expected somebody to ask someday how a dead man stayed useful to living thieves.

From the bedroom came the buzz of her bank app reminder: auto-draft for her credit-union payment in forty-eight hours. Twelve thousand four hundred dollars still hanging off her name, and suspension meant payroll could stall if internal affairs wanted to play games. She wrote the due date on the corner of the legal pad and hated the superstition that facts got lighter once ink touched paper.

Her phone lit again with Marcus's name.

`Did you hear me tonight?`

She stared at the text long enough for the screen to dim.

`Yeah,` she wrote. `I heard you.`

Three minutes passed before another text arrived.

`Then choose us.`

Lena set the phone beside the notebook and pressed her thumb into the old knee scar through her jeans, a pressure trick the physical therapist taught her years ago when panic tried to run her body faster than sense. She could still smell Ida's kitchen on her shirt, fried onion and pepper and dish soap, the smell of being held inside a family whether you deserved it that day or not.

She opened a new message to Mara.

`I hit a wall. Book 92 pages for GT-09-31 and GT-12-44 are pulled for "audit," and the only company trail is a shared PO box with two Pike vendors. I need your archive access and your chancery source tonight or first light. County is burying the owner names.`

Mara answered in under a minute.

`You finally sound certain. Call me.`

Lena did not call right away. She looked once more at Marcus's last text, then at Jalen's geometry notebook she had brought home by reflex when she helped Ida clear the table, pages full of clean angles and one margin full of tiny drawn basketballs. He was a child trying to make a court behave like lines on paper.

She picked up the phone and called Mara.

They burned seven minutes arguing source terms and risk before Mara said she'd text coordinates when she had eyes on a safe door and a blind camera.


# Chapter 7

By nine-thirty the courthouse square had gone hollow except for the soda machine outside tax assessors and the cicadas sawing in the oak trees hard enough to feel in her teeth. Lena sat in her car with the air off, because she wanted to hear if anybody crossed the lot, and the inside of the windshield held a cloudy print of her own breath. Early fall in Mercer County only meant the heat stopped punching by daylight and started leaning at night. Her shirt clung between her shoulder blades. The old knee scar under the right kneecap ached every time she moved from brake to gas, though she was parked.

She watched the chancery annex lights through a slit between two sycamores. Half the windows were dark. One row on the second floor still burned the thin blue white that made every room look like a hospital waiting area. She told herself those lights meant a janitor, maybe a clerk finishing payroll, maybe nobody. She told herself five things like that and believed none of them.

Her phone buzzed once and lit Mara's name.

`Meet me in the back alley in two minutes. Don't park by the camera.`

They had already burned seven minutes on the phone arguing source terms and risk; this text was just coordinates.

Lena cut the screen and rested the phone on the passenger seat beside her father's notebook. The cover had gone soft from humidity and from her own hands. The penciled code she'd broken at the dinner table last night sat on a torn yellow sticky: `C-11 / Box 62 / CH-Probate / Cor Packet.` Her father had written it twenty years before she became a cop. Maybe he had written it on a lunch break. Maybe after he came home and told Ida he had worked late and had never said late doing what.

When Mara tapped on the glass, Lena flinched so hard her knee hit the steering column.

Mara slid in and shut the door without a hello. Her close-cropped hair was damp at the temples, and she kept her backpack in her lap with her notebook tucked under one arm and a phone rig clipped to her belt like a holstered tool. She smelled like rain and printer toner.

"Before we move," Mara said, "we settle source terms. Right now."

Lena kept her eyes on the annex. "You got us in?"

"That's not an answer."

"It's the first problem in front of us."

"No. The first problem is trust. If we find your father in those files, nothing disappears. If it touches Bell land, Bell counsel gets it tonight."

Lena turned toward her. "You think I'd drag you into a felony and then pocket evidence?"

"I think blood scrambles people. I think two in the morning scrambles people worse."

The words stung because they were true enough to land. Lena swallowed and felt the dry scrape in her throat. "You post raw scans tonight, Clay says we forged everything, calls me a suspended cop breaking into county records to settle a family grudge. He'll get a judge before sunup."

"I didn't say post. I said share. There's a difference and you know it."

Mara pulled a folded legal pad sheet from her bag and held it in the dark between them. The page held names and numbers in block print.

"Fallback," Mara said. "If we're stopped inside, we call Deirdre Stokes first, then Bell family counsel in Greenville. If your old command shows before counsel does, I start recording and state we're documenting imminent records transfer that affects active homicide inquiry and civil heirs. If one of us gets detained, the other leaves with copies. No hero shit."

Lena took the paper and read it twice. Deirdre Stokes she knew by reputation: civil-rights attorney, mean cross, no patience for sheriff-office theater. The list made this feel less like improvisation and more like an autopsy report waiting for a body.

"You already typed the statement?"

"Three versions. One for sheriff deputies, one for county security, one for press if it goes bad."

Lena gave a short laugh without humor. "You always this cheerful before a misdemeanor?"

"Burglary and tampering can stack to a felony if Clay wants to perform." Mara met her eyes. "You still in?"

The honest answer sat ugly in Lena's chest: she wanted to go home, lock the door, and pretend she had not decoded those notes, not opened this lane where her father's name could become evidence bag material. She pictured Jalen's face at dinner when he had asked if police lied. She had said yes. She had watched him absorb it like a burn.

"I'm in," she said. "You get Bell counsel everything we touch that ties to tract ownership. You hold publication till we verify provenance. If you think I'm stalling, you don't ask permission. You go."

Mara folded the legal pad sheet and tucked it back into her bag. "Done."

"And I need one thing from you," Lena said. "Who gave you access?"

Mara's jaw tightened. "Not happening."

"If this is a setup, I need to know who can bury us with one statement."

"If I burn a source, I don't have a job, and no one ever talks to me again. You want that, find another door."

The old argument was stripped to bone. It was cop logic against reporter survival. Lena let it sit. She hated it and she needed it.

"Fine," she said. "You keep the name. You answer one question: are they doing this for money, fear, or conscience?"

Mara looked toward the annex and took longer than she liked. "Mostly fear," she said. "A little rent." 

That answer was enough to make Lena's stomach drop another inch.

They moved the car two blocks, cut through the alley behind chancery, and crossed under a security light that buzzed like an insect trapped in glass. Mara produced a clipped badge sleeve with a county logo and a magnetic stripe inside.

"Temporary records contractor," she said when Lena stared.

"You forged ID now?"

"No. I borrowed a life." 

"Jesus." 

"Save your theology. Swipe this, hold that door three seconds, then we're in."

Lena hated how cleanly Mara had rehearsed the timing. She hated more that she followed it.

The reader at the back entrance blinked red, then green. The latch clacked loose. They slipped into a stairwell that smelled of mopped bleach and damp concrete. Lena counted the steps without meaning to: fourteen to the first landing, seventeen to the second, twelve to the corridor. Her pulse stayed in her throat the whole climb.

Second floor chancery records looked less like a vault than a tired school library. The room held metal shelving and banker boxes and cartons with county seals split and re-taped so many times the paper looked furred. Fluorescent bulbs flickered at irregular beats, giving every aisle a stutter. A dehumidifier droned from the far wall, its collection tank burping every few minutes.

Mara checked her watch. "Source said sensors in this wing cycle at ten-fifty and eleven-thirty when humidity system resets. We have twenty-five, maybe thirty minutes before the next clean sweep logs motion."

"You keep saying source like it's a weather front," Lena muttered.

"You keep asking like you're still on payroll."

They split the aisle by shelf marker: C-11 through C-14. Lena carried the notebook and an LED penlight; Mara handled phone camera and a portable scanner that folded out like a silver insect. Neither of them spoke for the first few minutes except box numbers.

"Fifty-eight."

"Not it."

"Sixty-one. Tax sales."

"Wrong decade."

When Lena pulled Box 62 and lifted the lid, her hands shook enough to rattle paper clips. Inside sat hanging folders labeled in faded black marker: `Bell, Isaiah`; `Bell Heirs - Misc`; `Coroner Addenda`; `Probate Adj.` A fourth slot stood empty where a folder had been torn out hard enough to leave half the tab behind.

"Missing one," she said.

Mara was beside her in two steps. "Which one?"

Lena turned the broken tab toward the light. `Tran...` and then ripped pulp. It might have been Transfer, or Transcript, or Transaction ledger, and now it was gone.

"Shit," Mara whispered.

Lena opened `Coroner Addenda` first and got a thin stack of mimeographed forms held with a rusted brad. She expected one page about Isaiah Bell. She got seven packets across three years.

The first, dated August 22, 1963, named Isaiah Bell, male, nineteen, cause of death initially typed as "suspected drowning." On the supplemental page an earlier line had been crossed through hard enough to emboss the paper below: `ligature furrow present at neck, posterior contusions, right hyoid fracture.` Someone had retyped beneath it in cleaner ink: `postmortem artifact possible due to transport and decomposition.`

Lena swallowed bile. She had read enough modern autopsies to know what a broken hyoid usually meant. She heard Rao's voice in her head, dry and precise: neck compression, manual or ligature, probable homicide until ruled otherwise.

The second packet carried the name Ellis Bell, age forty-one, six weeks after Isaiah. Declared combine accident. Photo stapled to the back showed a hand laid on a morgue slab, palm up, fingers packed with Delta mud and blood under every nail, and bruises around the wrist in a clean loop like somebody had grabbed hard and held.

The third packet was a woman Lena had never heard: Cora Jean Bell, thirty-three, listed as smoke inhalation in a kitchen fire. The attached scene note described no soot in the trachea.

She looked up at Mara, who had gone very still. Mara did not fill silence when she was thinking. Her thumb hovered over the scanner trigger.

"This is not random," Mara said finally. "It's succession cleaning."

Lena kept turning pages. Each death date sat within weeks of a filing in `Bell Heirs - Misc.` Each filing moved a fraction of Gideon Tract into temporary administration because heirs were dead, missing, or legally unlocatable.

"They killed people in order," Lena said. "Not for rage. For paper."

"For paper that turns into dirt that turns into money," Mara said.

The heir file was worse. Thick packet, chancery heading, `In re: Gideon Agricultural Tract Parcel Group 7 and Associated Holdings.` Entire columns were blacked out with old marker that had bled through four sheets. A later clerk had copied those pages for preservation, so each redaction sat doubled, ghosted, impossible to reverse with light. But not every line was gone.
