Names survived in margins where the marker missed: fragments like `-aiah Bell Jr.` and `M. Bell (minor)`. Beside them, stamped entries: `Service attempted / returned unserved.` `Address unknown.` `No issue surviving.`

At the bottom of three pages, next to a line for service verification, the same signature appeared in tight slanted script.

`Deputy Waller`

No first name. No badge number. Just the title and the surname she had spent thirty-eight years carrying.

Lena stared until the letters blurred. Her father had taught her to print before cursive. He had sat at the kitchen table on a Sunday with a yellow No. 2 pencil and corrected her grip with two fingers, gentle, patient, almost shy. He had signed her permission slips with those same narrow loops. She knew the tilt of his W the way she knew the shape of her son's ear.

Mara touched her arm once, light. "Lena. We scan now."

Lena jerked back into motion and wiped the sweat off her palms onto her jeans. "Scan every page. Front and back. Include folder tabs and box labels for provenance."

"Already doing it."

Mara worked fast and methodical, setting pages on the scanner glass, photographing each stack in place, then reassembling in original order. Lena photographed shelf marker C-11, aisle placard, humidity monitor timestamp, and the transfer cart parked at the aisle end with tomorrow's date handwritten on masking tape: `09/19 - Jackson Hold.`

"They're moving this by morning," Mara said, following her lens. "Your source was right."

"Your source," Lena said.

"Yeah."

She pulled the next folder and found a transfer manifest with line items half-whited out. Box 62 listed as `partial contents.` Box 63 marked `removed per judicial directive.` Signature on that page came from a deputy clerk Lena did not know, but the directive number referenced Judge Pike's chamber docket.

"Missing folders weren't lost," Mara said. "They were curated."

"Say that on record and they'll call it editorializing."

"Then I'll quote the damn manifest." 

Their voices had risen. Lena forced hers down. "No quotes yet. If this drops before we pin chain continuity, Clay says contamination and gets an injunction."

Mara capped the scanner and looked straight at her. "If this sits in your trunk while you decide whether your father deserves soft handling, Bell families get robbed again."

The sentence hit like a slap because it carried the thing Lena feared other people already thought. She stepped closer until their shoes almost touched.

"Don't confuse grief with corruption," Lena said, low. "I'm not asking for soft handling. I'm asking for one clean line from vault to counsel so nobody can disappear this as gossip."

Mara held the stare another beat, jaw working, then let her shoulders drop. "Fine. We move now. We copy in duplicate. One goes to counsel before midnight. One stays offline with you."

"And if either of us gets grabbed?"

"Drive A goes to Deirdre. Drive B auto-uploads if I don't clear a timer." Mara tapped her phone. "I already armed it."

Lena blew out a breath she had been holding too long. "You're exhausting."

"You're slow when it hurts."

Both things were true. They packed pages into chronological order, replaced every folder exactly, and slid Box 62 back into the shelf with gloves on. The missing tab still stared like a pulled tooth.

They crossed to a side office where an old high-volume copier sat under plastic sheeting. Mara fed pages while Lena watched the corridor and counted seconds between the dehumidifier burps. Every noise sounded bigger at night: rollers turning, paper slapping output tray, the metal clink when someone in another wing dropped what sounded like a mop bucket.

"How many?" Lena whispered.

"Eighty-three scanned. Thirty-six hard copies so far."

"Don't jam on me now," Lena muttered at the machine.

"You praying at a Xerox?"

"I don't trust anything with this many moving parts."

At copy forty-two, footsteps sounded in the hallway. Not hurried. Not sneaking. The flat tired pace of someone who had worked this building too long to rush for any emergency smaller than fire.

Lena killed the copier switch, but it was too late. A man turned into the doorway and stopped with his hand still on a ring of keys.

He wore county maintenance navy, sleeves rolled to the elbow, and bright orange reading glasses on a beaded cord that bounced against his chest when he breathed. Gray in his beard. Sneakers with one lace replaced by twine. He stared at them, then at the stack of old chancery pages in the tray.

"Well," he said. "You picked the loudest machine in the county." 

Mara moved half a step in front of her bag. Lena put her hands where he could see them.

"Calvin," Lena said, recognizing him from daytime records windows. "It's late for everybody."

"Late does not cover this." He squinted at her face and then let out a short sound. "Detective Waller. Suspended Detective Waller, if county Facebook is telling the truth for once." 

He set his keys on the desk and rubbed the bridge of his nose with thumb and forefinger like a migraine had moved in behind his eyes. On his left wrist a hospital-style silicone band read `LILY STRONG` in fading glitter letters.

"My kid made me wear that since chemo started," he said, noticing Lena looking. "So if you're about to lie, save me the effort. I've got no patience tonight." 

The room tightened around Lena's ribs. She knew the rules in one clean flash: he could call security, call Clay, or call nobody and still hold leverage for years.

Mara spoke first, voice clipped and clear. "Mr. Pruitt, C-11 gets transferred by morning. Those files tie to an active homicide and heir suppression. We're making preservation copies before they walk."

"And you're doing that without a warrant, after hours, with a suspended officer and a reporter carrying a scanner." Calvin gave a humorless smile. "I can still do arithmetic." 

Lena stepped in. "You can do arithmetic. You can do history too. You work these shelves. You know which names vanish on purpose." 

Calvin looked at her for a long time. His right hand drummed a rhythm on the desk edge: three taps, pause, three taps again.

"I know this building eats paper when powerful people are hungry," he said. "I know my supervisor told me to clear aisle C by six a.m. because a truck from Jackson wants sealed boxes before doors open. I also know I've got a seventeen-year-old who has to be at oncology in Greenville at eight-thirty and insurance that acts blind every month." He glanced at the pages again. "So tell me exactly what you want from me."

No one moved. The fluorescent light buzzed and popped.

"Ten minutes," Mara said. "No calls till we're gone."

Calvin laughed once. "Ten minutes costs more than y'all think." 

Mara opened a zipper pocket and took out a cash envelope folded into quarters. She did not perform reluctance. She counted six hundred-dollar bills onto the desk and held his gaze while she did it.

Calvin did not touch the money yet. "If I take that, I'm dirty with you." 

"You're already in it with us," Mara said.

"That's not the same."

Lena spoke carefully. "No, it isn't. This is chain-of-custody triage. If those boxes leave clean before sunup, Bell heirs never see the full record. You know that." 

Calvin rubbed the silicone bracelet again. His fingers shook just a little. "You two think this county can be shamed into honesty with paper?"

"Not by itself," Mara said. "Paper won't do it alone. Paper and witnesses. Tonight that's you." 

He stared at the money another beat, then swept it into his lunch bag with the same motion he might have used on crumbs. He looked ashamed and angry and practical all at once.

"Nine minutes," he said. "Not ten. And hear me: if my name lands in your story, I say you forced me. I say you threatened my kid. I say whatever keeps me employed."

"Understood," Mara said.

"I mean it. I like being alive and insured."

"We heard you," Lena said.

Calvin picked up his keys and pointed one toward the hallway. "Freight elevator is down tonight, so transfer crew uses east stair. That means they hit C-aisle first. Those boxes move by seven-fifteen. If you need anything else, take it now or bury this whole night." He hesitated. "And Detective?"

"Yeah."

"Your daddy used to smoke clove cigarettes behind this wing where the camera can't see. He said they kept mosquitos off. They smelled like Christmas and bad choices." Calvin shook his head once. "People can be kind at supper and cruel on paper before breakfast. Do what you came for."

He left without looking back.

For two seconds Lena could not move. The clove detail hit harder than the signature had, maybe because it came from somebody else's memory, not hers. Her father did smell like cloves some nights when he kissed Ida's cheek and told Lena to keep studying. She had never asked where they came from.

Mara touched her elbow. "Lena. Clock."

They moved fast. They copied, collated, clipped, and bagged. Mara encrypted the scans on two drives with labels made from ripped painter's tape: `A` and `B`. Lena photographed each packet with a timestamp app, then shot the copier counter and the room clock in the same frame. The word provenance hammered through her head again and again. Her temples throbbed with it.

When they stepped into the corridor, the annex had gone quieter, as if the building itself held breath. Their shoes squeaked on waxed tile. Somewhere outside, thunder rolled low and long over the levee.

At the stairwell door Mara stopped.

"I'm sending Drive A now," she said. "Deirdre and Bell counsel. Not publication. Transfer-only link, expiration six hours."

Lena nodded. "Do it."

Mara thumbed her phone, waited for the progress bar, then held the screen out so Lena could see the recipient list. There were no games and no omissions.

"Sent," Mara said.

They descended one flight when a security alarm chirped once in the distance, then fell silent. Lena froze on the landing, heart climbing into her throat again.

"That us?" she whispered.

Mara listened, then checked her phone when it vibrated against the rail. Her face tightened.

"It's us. Badge 4477 just tripped an unauthorized-entry packet." She turned the screen so Lena could read: `INCIDENT 24-0917 / CHANCERY C-WING / POSSIBLE RECORDS TAMPERING.` "Source says it auto-pushed to county security and sheriff command at 12:57."

"How much time does that buy them?"

"Enough to come looking before dawn. Keep moving."

"You sound very calm for someone holding two felonies in a backpack."

"I'm not calm. I'm compartmentalized."

"That's reporter for terrified?"

"That's reporter for useful."

They reached the back door, swiped out, and stepped into warm wet air just as rain started, fat drops at first and then a full sheet that flattened the alley dust into mud in seconds. Mara pulled her jacket over the backpack. Lena tucked the folder tube under her shirt and felt paper edges scrape her ribs.

Neither spoke until they reached Lena's car three blocks away. Rain hammered the roof hard enough to drown the world.

Mara sat in the passenger seat dripping onto vinyl and passed one drive across the console.

"Drive B. Offline copy. Keep it dry, keep it hidden."

Lena took it and slid it into the zipper seam of her boot.

Mara's phone lit again before Lena could shift into gear. She shoved another screenshot between them. A grainy hallway still showed two women bent over a cart and a box lid open like a mouth. Below it, a line read: `Forwarded: MCSO Duty Captain`.

"This was supposed to batch at shift change," Mara said. "Somebody shoved it through early."

Lena gripped the steering wheel and felt the leather slip under wet palms. "Then split-home is dead."

"Call Deirdre now."

Lena hit speaker. Deirdre answered on the second ring, voice clipped over road noise.

"Tell me you are not at your residence," Deirdre said.

"In transit," Lena said. "We have scanned packets and hard copies. Incident twenty-four-zero-nine-one-seven is live."

"I saw it. Sheriff's office already asked a duty judge for an emergency seizure order tied to that incident number. Do not take originals into your house. If deputies lift those pages from your kitchen, they will argue theft and contamination in one breath."

Mara leaned toward the phone. "What's the move?"

"Change plan now. First Trinity south lot, two-oh-five. Bring one witness and a duplicate set. I can defend ugly facts. I cannot defend a solo stash."

"Understood," Lena said.

The line clicked dead.

Rain thinned enough for the wipers to catch up. Lena pulled from the curb and cut away from her neighborhood.

"And your source?" she asked.

"Already burned," Mara said. "They sent one more text: if security asks, they never issued the badge tonight."

Lena started to answer and stopped. The packet on her lap had shifted open to the signature again.

`Deputy Waller`

Ink feathered into old paper fibers. No tremor in the hand that wrote it. No apology. Just administrative certainty, the kind that moved land from one family to another while pretending it was weather.

She heard Ida in church voice reading from Amos about selling the righteous for silver and the needy for a pair of shoes. She heard Jalen asking if police lied. She heard her own answer.

"Say it," Mara said quietly.

"Say what."

"What does it do to your case map?"

Lena closed the packet. "It gives us mechanism: death certificates laundered into probate actions, probate actions laundered into tract control. My father signed the service step that made redaction look lawful." She rubbed her thumb over the folder edge until the paper bent. "And it gives Clay motive to bury every inch and call us criminals while he does it."

"Then we've got motive to make it unburyable."

"Yeah."

Mara leaned back and shut her eyes for a moment. "I need one more term."

Lena almost laughed. "Jesus, you don't stop negotiating."

"No, I don't. If this gets violent and you've got to pick between protecting me and protecting the documents, protect the documents."

"Not happening."

"Lena."

"No. I can replace paper. I can't replace you."

Mara opened her eyes and studied her, skeptical and tired and maybe moved despite herself.

"That's noble and stupid," Mara said.

"It's logistics," Lena said. "You're the only person outside this office who can tell the story in full sentences without flinching."

"Fine," Mara said. "Revised term: neither of us plays martyr. We leave together when we can, and when we can't, one verified copy stays in circulation."

"Done."

First Trinity Baptist sat dark behind live oaks and a chain-link fence that hummed with rainwater. The south lot held one church van and a dented Buick with a handicap tag. A porch light snapped on as they parked.

Deacon Loretta James came out in a raincoat over a nightgown, hair wrapped in a pink scarf, arthritic fingers clamped around a legal pad and a battery lantern. She had run the church legal clinic for years and did not waste words.

"Ms. Stokes said this was evidence preservation," Loretta said. "Table is in the fellowship hall. Camera stays on."

Inside, they laid documents across a folding table that smelled of lemon cleaner and old coffee. Mara read file identifiers and page counts into her phone camera while Lena held each sheet flat under the lantern, timestamp app visible beside the signatures. Loretta logged times in block letters and signed as witness at 2:17 a.m., then again at 2:23 when Mara sealed the duplicate set in a tamper envelope.

Mara's phone buzzed with Deirdre's next instruction: `Courier delayed. Keep one set moving. Do not park all copies in one place.`

So they changed again. Loretta locked the duplicate envelope in the church office fire safe and kept the key on a chain under her blouse. Lena kept originals in the plastic case under her seat. Mara carried encrypted scans and Drive A.

When they stepped back into the rain, Lena's hands were shaking hard enough that she missed the car door handle once.

"You think he knew?" Mara asked as they stood in the lot, water running off the church gutters in heavy ropes.

Lena kept her eyes on the dark windshield. "Knew what."

"That he was helping steal land from people who'd already lost blood."

The question had waited all night between them and finally sat in the open like a knife on the table.

"I think he knew he was making something official," Lena said after a while. "I don't know if he called it theft in his own head. Men like him were trained to call it duty." She swallowed. "Duty can be another word for cowardice when the wrong people sign your paycheck."

Mara lifted two fingers like she was tagging a quote in the air. "That's a line I could print."

"Don't."

"I won't. Not yet."

They split at the church gate: Mara east toward the interstate, Lena south toward home with the file case pressing against the side of her calf every time she hit the brake.

At 2:46 a.m., a patrol cruiser sat two houses down from Lena's driveway with its lights off and engine running. She drove past, circled the block once, and came back only after it rolled to the corner and idled there instead.

Inside, before she took off her shoes, her phone lit with an unknown number.

`Security asked about C-wing and six hundred dollars. I said I saw nothing. Do not make me do that twice.`

No signature. No need.

She set the file case on the table beside her father's notebook and opened to the page with `Deputy Waller` while rain ticked against the kitchen window. Outside, tires hissed past the house again, slow enough to count.

She did not sleep. She sat at the table until the clock crawled toward dawn, waiting for the next unknown number.


# Chapter 8

At 5:18 a.m., the next unknown number finally came. Beau said, "Get down to Legal Aid. Don't wait for coffee."

She was halfway into yesterday's jeans when he added, "It's Ava."

The line went dead before she could ask anything else.

The Mercer Public Defense Annex sat two blocks off the square in a one-story brick building that used to be a feed office. By the time Lena turned onto Willow, the street was already clogged with cruisers, church vans, and people who had come in house shoes because a cousin had texted them one sentence and they had not stopped to get dressed. Yellow tape stretched from an oak tree to a parking meter, then bowed inward where shoulders kept hitting it.

A woman in a denim robe screamed, "She got my boy out when nobody else answered the phone," and slammed both palms against a deputy's chest. He staggered, caught himself, and looked twenty years old.

Lena parked three blocks out because she had no county placard anymore. Suspension meant she had no lane, no clipped nod from uniforms, no right to cross tape. It also meant nobody had the authority to stop her from walking through the middle of people who knew her name and had reasons to hate what it represented.

"Detective Waller." Sergeant Nia Tolliver stepped in front of her with both hands up, then rubbed her thumb against a strip of pink athletic tape she kept wrapped around her index finger. Lena remembered the finger from softball season years back; Nia had dislocated it twice and never took the tape off entirely. "You're still on paper. I can't let you in unless command clears it."

"Then call command while I keep your people from getting run over," Lena said.

Nia worried the pink tape with her thumb until it rolled at the edge. "You ain't wrong. Stay on my shoulder."

They pushed together through the tape gap while a man behind the tape threw a plastic water bottle that hit the annex wall hard enough to burst. Warm water ran down brick like sweat.

Inside, the building smelled like bleach and copier heat. Fluorescent lights buzzed. The reception desk had been knocked sideways, and one of the waiting-room chairs had a shoe print on the seat, heel dug deep into cheap vinyl. Ava's office door stood open with a strip of paper evidence seal hanging off one hinge like wet skin.

Beau waited at the threshold in a wrinkled dress shirt and nitrile gloves. He had shoved one glove halfway off and forgotten it there. "Glad you came," he said. "Rao's in back."

He did not say he was sorry. He did not have to.

Ava lay on her side between desk and file cabinet, one arm trapped under her body. The overhead fan kept turning, slow and crooked, and made the loose pages on her desk tick against wood. Her throat had been cut from left to right in one deep pass. The blood had gone black at the edges and sticky in the center where humidity refused to let anything dry clean.

There was no second body beside her, no Isaiah Bell laid out like accusation and warning. There was only Ava, alone on the floor she had walked every morning in square-toed black pumps with a worn spot on the right heel where she dragged when she was tired.

On the desk, centered on a legal pad, sat a clear mason jar packed with human teeth and cloudy fluid.

Each tooth had a string tied to the root and a tiny manila tag threaded on the line. The tags were handwritten in all caps, thick black marker that bled into the paper fibers: GT-14C. GT-9A. GT-22B.

They were Gideon Tract parcel codes.

Lena felt her stomach pull tight so fast it hurt her back. She had copied those same letter-number patterns out of her father's notebook two nights ago in the chancery archive basement, her flashlight balanced between shoulder and cheek.

Dr. Naveen Rao looked up from the jar with his glasses sliding down his nose. He had a coffee stain on his cuff and an old habit of tapping his pen against his lower lip when he was sorting thoughts. Tap, tap, stop. "Do not touch the table," he said, then exhaled and corrected himself. "Don't touch anything near the table. The jar was staged for visibility. Whoever left it wanted first glance impact."

"One mouth?" Lena asked.

"No." Rao angled his light. "Mixed dentition. Different wear patterns, different restorations. At least one amalgam old enough to predate modern composite work, and at least three very recent extractions. This isn't a trophy from one victim. It's assembled."

"Assembled by whoever had access to oral evidence or a dental waste stream," Beau said. "Jesus."

Rao nodded once. "And confidence about handling biologic material. There are typed labels on two strings under the handwriting. Medical supply stock. Not grocery twine."

From the doorway, Mara Quinn raised her phone and then lowered it without taking a photo. She wore the same green windbreaker from storm season, cuffs frayed, and had a reporter's notebook tucked flat against her forearm like she slept with it there. "I'm not publishing scene photos," she said, voice clipped with anger. "But people outside already know about the jar. They're saying y'all planted it to spin out riot control."

"You heard that where?" Beau asked.

"On three group threads and one live stream from the parking lot. You lost the perimeter fifteen minutes ago."

Lena looked at Ava again, at the legal pad under the jar. The top page had an outline in Ava's handwriting, block letters and hard underlines: MOTION TO COMPEL BENEFICIARY ACCOUNTING. A coffee ring had warped one corner. In the margin Ava had written call T. Boyd re exhibits in blue ink and circled it twice.

The blue ink had feathered where blood mist reached it. Lena saw Ava in a courthouse hallway last winter, tie shoes kicked off under a bench at midnight, laughing through a cold while she rewrote a motion by hand because the clerk's portal crashed again. The newest ink on Ava's desk was still about work, and Ava's body was cooling three feet away from it.

A deputy from Records appeared behind Mara, breathing hard. "Detective Broussard, we got people trying to push in through the back loading door. They said if we don't let family see her, they'll break glass."

"Which family?"

"All of them," he said, and that was not an exaggeration.

Lena's suspension letter sat folded in her back pocket. She could feel the paper edge against her skin when she bent. She was not supposed to direct anybody, not supposed to touch chain of custody, not supposed to do anything except stay home and wait for Internal Affairs to schedule a hearing that kept moving every week.

Her throat tasted like pennies. If she kept quiet, people in that hall would bleed next. If she sounded like command, Clay would write her up before sundown.

She said, "Beau, tell Nia to pull three more units on the rear lane and get one clergy liaison in front. Nobody gets through this door, but Ava's sister gets visual through the hallway window once the body is covered. If we shut them out cold, they'll take it anyway."

Beau glanced at her, then at the deputy. "Do it. And get me body cam numbers for every uniform on this block."

The deputy ran.

Mara watched Lena with narrowed eyes. "You just gave an order while suspended."

"I gave a survival suggestion," Lena said.

"Same thing in this county."

Outside, the first plate-glass window cracked with a sound like a gunshot. A woman screamed. Boots thudded on concrete. Then came that low crowd roar Lena had started hearing in her sleep: grief and fury braided so tight they sounded like one living machine.

She went to the front anyway.

The porch had turned into a pressure point. Two deputies held a line with their forearms while bodies shoved from the sidewalk side. Reverend Ellis Cain stood on a bench near the azaleas, suit coat buttoned wrong, trying to project over everybody with the cough-ragged preacher voice that had carried three decades of funerals. He kept a silver inhaler in his left hand and hit it between sentences without stepping down.

"Back up and let the medics work," he shouted. "Back up so we don't hand them another body today."

Teresa Boyd stood directly under him in Ava's cardigan, sleeves too long over her knuckles, mascara run down both cheeks. "Don't tell me to back up," she yelled at the deputies. "My sister paid this county's bills in blood and continuances and now she's on that floor and y'all got your guns out on us?"

A young patrolman had already unclipped his pepper spray. His hand shook.

Lena stepped between him and Teresa and put her own empty palms up. "Officer, holster that now."

"I've got authority to deploy if-"

"You deploy in this crowd, we lose the whole block. Put it away." Her voice came out in the careful procedural cadence she used in court testimony, every word measured. "You'll keep your baton low and your body camera active."

He hesitated one beat too long, then shoved the spray back into his belt.

Teresa turned on Lena. "You were suspended. Ava said you were trying to help and they benched you anyway. So what are you now, huh? A cop or a mourner?"

Lena swallowed heat and copper. "I'm here to keep them from hurting you next."

"Then say her name where all these people can hear it."

Lena climbed onto the bench beside Reverend Cain, shoes slipping on wet paint. The crowd noise shook through the wood into her knees.

"Ava Boyd was murdered in her office this morning," she said. Her voice carried because everybody wanted to hear what lie came next. "She was not found with a second body. That's new. What was left at this scene is different from every other murder scene in this case, and we're preserving it exactly as we found it."

A voice near the doorway shouted, "Preserving for who? For Clay?"

"For court," Lena shot back. "If we lose chain now, whoever did this walks. You know that."

A man near the curb yelled, "Court never held shit for us."

Reverend Cain leaned into the mic clipped to his tie, not county issue, church issue, battery pack taped with red electrical tape because he refused to buy new equipment while the roof fund was short. "She's telling you where the trap is," he said. "Don't jump in it for them."

The shove at the front line eased by inches, not trust but a pause.

Lena got down from the bench and felt her phone vibrate in her pocket. Unknown number. She answered and heard Clay Reddick's smooth campaign voice over background office noise.

"Detective Waller," he said. "Stay where you are. We're convening a community session at New Hope in forty-five minutes. I need your cooperation."

"You need my face," Lena said.

A tiny pause. "I need order. Bring whatever facts you can verify."

He hung up before she could answer.

Back inside, Rao had started sealing the jar transport box. Tape screamed across cardboard as he pulled each strip tight, and the sound made Lena's shoulders jump before she could mask it. He glanced up as she entered. "I logged the tag strings separately," he said. "You were right about the parcel format."

"Tell me something I can say out loud in forty-five minutes," she said.

Rao slid a paper toward her with three lines at the top. "You can say this is an altered signature. You can say no complete historical remains were present. And you can say the tags map to land identifiers, not random numbers. Anything past that would be overclaiming."

Outside, Ava's name hit the windows again, a chant and a plea at once. Lena folded the paper once and put it in her pocket next to the suspension letter.

On Ava's desk, under the blood-dark edge of the legal pad, Lena saw the corner of a banker box inventory sheet. The first line read: Boyd v. Gideon Tract Trust et al.

She read it twice, then reached for the handles and stopped an inch short. "I can't touch that while I'm suspended."

Beau nodded once and stepped to the door. "Deputy Kincaid. In here with chain clipboard. You're on-duty evidence custodian now."

The Records deputy came in with fresh gloves and a body camera light blinking red. Sergeant Tolliver followed and braced in the doorway, also recording. Kincaid read the file label aloud, then read it again for clarity while Beau signed the temporary transfer line and Nia called timestamp and witness names into both cameras.

Only when the log line was complete did Kincaid seal the box handles with tamper tape and lift it level off Ava's desk. Lena walked beside him empty-handed toward Evidence while the crowd outside chanted Ava's name like they were daring the county to choke on it.

***

New Hope Baptist's fellowship hall had old linoleum that clicked under dress shoes and folding tables that never sat level. A deacon had set out coffee in church urns, and nobody touched it. The room smelled like rain-damp denim, bleach mop water, and the fried catfish a grieving auntie had tried to bring because in Mercer County grief still got fed even when nobody could swallow. The grease smell turned Lena's stomach anyway.

Clay did not come in uniform. He wore a navy sport coat and a flag pin and stood at the front beside a portable podium like this was donor outreach. To his left sat County Attorney Felicia Dunn with three binders and an expression that said she had not slept. To his right sat an empty chair with a paper tent card: DET. L. WALLER. The card looked less like welcome than bait.

Mara slid into the back row and set her phone rig on a tripod made from taped chopsticks and stubbornness. She caught Lena's eye and gave one small nod: on record.

Teresa Boyd was already at the front, arms crossed over Ava's cardigan, mouth pressed into a hard line. Next to her sat Deshawn Lee, paint still under his thumbnail from protest signs, heel bouncing fast enough to shake his chair. Reverend Cain took the aisle seat with his inhaler in his lap and a legal pad full of names of people who wanted to speak. Seeing that cardigan in Teresa's folded arms made Lena's chest tighten; Ava wore it to winter arraignments and always complained the pockets were too small for files.

Clay tapped the microphone. "We're here to reduce immediate harm while the investigation proceeds."

Boos rolled through the hall.

Teresa stood without waiting to be recognized. "You mean reduce harm now that she's dead," she said. "Ava asked this office for protective detail after she filed notice on Gideon, and y'all told her staffing was thin. Don't say immediate like this started today."

Dunn leaned toward her mic. "Ms. Boyd, there are pending privilege questions on your sister's files, and-"

"Don't lawyer me with her blood still wet," Teresa snapped.

The room surged. Chairs scraped. One deputy at the back moved his hand toward his belt, saw thirty phones pointed at him, and dropped it.

The scrape and shift hit Lena like a body memory of the porch crush outside. One bad move, one burst of spray or baton, and this room would go from mourning to triage in ten seconds.

Clay lifted both palms in a performance of calm. "Detective Waller has asked to present verified facts from the scene. Then we can discuss release parameters."

He stressed can like permission flowed one way.

Lena took the chair with her name and did not sit. She stood behind it, hands on plastic, and looked at faces she knew from court corridors, school pickup lines, church revivals, bond hearings at two in the morning. They looked back like jurors deciding whether she still belonged among them.

"Ava was killed in her office before dawn," Lena said. "There was no second full body with her. That pattern changed."

A man in the middle row said, "Changed by who?"

Clay leaned toward his mic. "Let's keep this to verified process and avoid speculation."

Lena did not look at him. "I'm doing exactly that. We don't know yet."

A few people nodded at the bluntness. More waited.

"A jar of human teeth was left on her desk," Lena said. Murmurs broke across the room like wind in tin roofing. "Each tooth had a tag with a Gideon Tract parcel code. Lab has confirmed mixed teeth from multiple sources. That means this was assembled, not random."

Deshawn leaned forward. "So whoever did this is harvesting evidence now?"

"Whoever staged it had access," Lena said. "Could be evidence, could be dental waste, could be both. I'm not feeding guesses into this room."

Mara called from the back before Clay could reclaim the mic. "Then give clocks. Not 'as available.' Tonight, what's public? Tags? Photos? Who signs off?"

"Questions will be queued," Clay said. "Detective Waller will finish her initial statement."

Mara stayed standing. "That's not a question. That's the whole fight."

Lena felt the old instinct to hedge, to leave room for command to step in and revise later. That instinct had cost too many people already.

"Milestone one," she said. "When lab logs final intake of the jar and tag strings, we release tag numbers and recovery photos to family counsel and the public defender board. Deadline: tonight, 8:00 p.m."

At recovery photos, Teresa made a small choking sound and pressed Ava's sleeve to her mouth.

Clay cut in, voice smooth. "Release still runs through legal privilege and victim-family safeguards."

Teresa barked a laugh with no humor. "You hear that? That's how they stall."

"Milestone two," Lena said, louder. "When privilege review clears what can be disclosed from Ava's case files, we release the index of entities and individuals named in her pending Gideon litigation. Deadline: tomorrow, 6:00 p.m."

Sweat rolled down Lena's spine under her shirt despite the hall AC rattling overhead. She could feel every eye in the room counting whether she'd flinch.

Dunn whispered, "Lena-"

Lena kept going.

"Milestone three. When the clerk certifies copies for court use, we publish the transaction map connecting the murdered parties to Gideon Tract trust payouts and land transfers. Deadline: seventy-two hours from now."

By the time she said seventy-two hours, her palms were numb from gripping the chair back.

The hall went very quiet for one full second, then exploded.

"Put it in writing!"

"No edits from Clay!"

"Name names now!"

Reverend Cain's voice cut over the shouting before feedback squealed. "I've heard this room in three decades of funerals and one courthouse riot. Rage talks fast, but paper lasts longer. Make them write what she promised."

Teresa stood again, breathing hard. "You already saw her files, didn't you? Say what she died over."

Lena looked at Clay. He smiled without showing teeth, an expression he used in campaign photos with people he planned to cut loose after November.

So she answered Teresa instead.

"Ava had active filings against Gideon Tract Trust," Lena said. "In those files, Hank Voss appears as paid operations manager on tract parcels now under dispute. Lyle Fenn appears as contracted survey witness on boundary revisions. Ava was moving to compel full beneficiary accounting."

Mara said, "So the first two murder victims weren't just old history. They were tied to ongoing land profit."

"Yes," Lena said. "And Ava's homicide now sits on that same line."

A woman in the second row began to cry without sound, just shoulders shaking. Her son had been one of Ava's clients on a parole violation last year. Deshawn reached over and held her wrist while she cried.

Reverend Cain stood and cleared his throat into the mic. "I want everybody hearing this to hear one more thing. If we tear this room apart right now, they'll call that proof we can't be trusted with truth. That lie has fed this county longer than I've been alive."

He took a pull from his inhaler and continued, voice thinner but steady. "You hold her to the deadlines she just spoke. You hold me too. We post a watcher list and a clock on the church board tonight."

Deshawn nodded, heel still hammering the tile. "We'll hold tonight. One night. If they stall by tomorrow, we march to the square and we don't ask permission."

Clay leaned into his mic before Lena could answer. "No one is threatening anyone in this hall."

Deshawn looked straight at him. "You call it threat when poor people make plans in public."

Lena stepped between microphones. "Deshawn, I heard you. Clay, you heard him. The timeline stands."

She turned to Teresa. "I need one thing from you now. Who had keys to Ava's off-site file cabinet?"

Teresa wiped her face with Ava's sleeve. "Three people. Ava. Me. Her paralegal, June Haskins." She swallowed. "June keeps peppermint schnapps in a cough-syrup bottle in her desk because she says courthouse air is poison. If she's gone, she did not run. She panics on the bridge over Bayou Road and never drives it alone."

Lena wrote that down fast, because details like that were how you found a person before rumor found them. "We find June first."

By the time the meeting broke, three church volunteers had taped butcher paper to the fellowship hall wall and written DEADLINE WATCH in black marker. Under it: 8 p.m. / 6 p.m. / 72 hours. Names filled in below, people volunteering to witness each handoff so nobody could claim confusion later.

Mara walked with Lena to the side exit under a bank of flickering lights.

"You just boxed yourself in on camera," Mara said.

"I know."

"If Clay blocks release, your credibility burns with his."

"I know that too."

Mara tightened the strap on her phone rig. "Good. I prefer collaborators who can count."

Outside, thunder rolled over the levee and the first cold rain in weeks started tapping the metal awning hard enough to make everyone raise their voices.

***

Clay's office had a new camera in the upper corner by the air vent, black dome half-hidden behind a county seal decal. Lena saw it before she sat down.

He noticed her noticing. "Security upgrades," he said. "Given current volatility."

Beau stood by the filing cabinet with his arms crossed and looked like he wanted to be anywhere else. Internal Affairs Lieutenant Mark Penrod sat at the conference end of the room, reading glasses low on his nose, a handkerchief folded under his wrist so his palm sweat did not smear the forms.

Clay slid a packet across the table. "Reinstatement order effective immediately," he said. "Restricted duty classification, subject to active oversight."

Lena did not touch the paper yet. "How restricted?"

Clay folded his hands. "You regain evidence access on the Bell-related homicides and the Boyd file review. In exchange, you operate under command monitoring."

"Define monitoring."

Penrod answered without looking up. "Continuous body cam during duty hours, GPS telemetry on your assigned unit, mirrored email archive, and supervised upload of field notes at end of shift. Any unscheduled media contact triggers review."

"You forgot call logs," Lena said.

Penrod looked up, surprised. "Those too."

Clay gave a practiced sigh that sounded paternal if you ignored the math inside it. "Lena, this is not punitive. We need stable process after today's unrest."

She laughed once, no humor in it. "You suspended me for refusing your script and now you need my face to sell your timeline. Call it by its name."

His voice softened, which meant he was angry. "Here's what this is, Detective: an opportunity to return to service instead of staying outside with activists and reporters guessing at evidence."

Lena leaned forward. "Then let's be precise. I accept monitoring if I get full chain access to the tagged-teeth evidence and unredacted review access to Ava's Gideon files. Not summaries. Originals."

Clay's smile thinned. "Original privileged material is not your lane."

"It's my lane now if you want me to keep that church clock from turning into a street fight," she said. "You heard the room. They're one delay away from believing this office killed Ava and staged the rest."

Beau spoke for the first time. "She's right. That room was one bad order from a stampede."

Clay did not look at him. "And this office does not make decisions from crowd pressure."

Beau started again. "With respect, you weren't in there when that line buckled."

Clay cut him off. "You're here as witness, Detective Broussard, not policy counsel."

Beau stared at the rain-blurred window, nostrils flaring once, and shut up.

Lena tapped the packet. "Add one line. Any directive that limits release milestones must be issued to me in writing with signature and timestamp. If you change the plan, you own the change on paper."

Penrod blinked. "That's unusual."

"So is all of this," Lena said.

Clay stood and walked to the window that faced the parking lot. Rain had started to sheet across the glass, turning patrol cars into blurred lights. He stayed there long enough to make the room feel the wait.

When he turned back, campaign calm had returned.

"Fine," he said. "Written directives required for timeline modifications. But you don't freelance statements. All press goes through Public Information."

"I already made public commitments," Lena said.

"And you will honor them through approved channels."

She heard the trap and chose it anyway.

Penrod revised the form by hand, block letters, then pushed it to her with a county pen that had no cap. Lena read every line.

REINSTATEMENT STATUS: ACTIVE, RESTRICTED.

OVERSIGHT PROTOCOL: LEVEL TWO.

FIELD REPORTING: REAL-TIME DIGITAL MIRROR.

COMMUNICATIONS: SUBJECT TO COMMAND REVIEW.

SPECIAL CONDITION: ANY MODIFICATION TO PUBLIC EVIDENCE-RELEASE TIMELINE SHALL BE ISSUED IN WRITING TO DETECTIVE WALLER WITH SHERIFF SIGNATURE AND TIMESTAMP.

She signed.

Clay signed.

Penrod stamped the top page twice because the first stamp came out half-inked.

Clay sat back down and slid her old badge across the table in a plastic evidence sleeve, as if even that had become contraband. "Welcome back," he said.

The words landed like a dare.

Lena clipped the badge to her belt and felt how light it was compared to the cost of touching it again.

In the hallway outside Clay's office, Mara waited on a metal bench under the flickering light panel, notebook open, one boot heel worrying a crack in the tile. She looked up when Lena stepped out.

"Well?"

Lena held up the reinstatement packet. "I'm back in. He put me on a leash."

"How short?"

"Short enough that if I sneeze off schedule, Penrod files a memo."

Mara made a face that might have been sympathy if she trusted the category. "Then we work around it."

"No," Lena said. "We work through it where we can and around it where we must. You don't send me anything that can burn your source trail if this office mirrors my phone."

Mara snapped the notebook shut. "Already moved sensitive docs to dead drop. You're late to your own paranoia."

A tired laugh escaped Lena before she could stop it.

In Evidence, Rao had already logged the jar under new homicide sequence numbers. The chain screen on the wall showed incoming items from Ava's office: one legal pad, seven banker boxes, one encrypted drive, two courthouse key cards, and three binders marked GIDEON TRACT ACCOUNTING. The scanner gave a sharp electronic chirp on each entry, regular as a monitor alarm.

Lena stood in front of the monitor with rain still drying on her shoulders and read the names on the first binder index as the clerk scanned pages: Voss, Henry. Fenn, Lyle. Pike Foundation Disbursement Committee. Emergency Motion Draft.

Ava Boyd had built the line in ink before a killer cut her throat for it. Lena remembered Ava at two in the morning outside Courtroom Two, shoe in one hand and file in the other, saying, "If they can hide the paper, they can steal a family in daylight." Lena had nodded then and gone home to sleep. Ava had stayed and kept filing.

Now the line was evidence, and evidence had a clock taped to a church wall across town with half the county watching.

Lena checked the time. 7:42 p.m.

Eighteen minutes to the first deadline, and a full night after that to turn Ava's binders into an affidavit a judge couldn't ignore.


# Chapter 9

By eight-thirty the next morning the courthouse air had already gone wet and sour, like a shirt left too long in a gym bag. Lena stood at the metal table in the records annex with Ava Boyd's marked-up probate printouts spread in a fan under fluorescent hum. The lights flickered every few seconds, enough to make black type blur at the edges, enough to make her think of a pulse skipping.

Rule 41 demanded three things in front of a judge: probable cause, a sworn connection between crime and place, and a description tight enough that nobody could call it a fishing expedition. The trust code demanded something else entirely. Trustees got to hide behind confidentiality unless somebody proved breach with specifics first. The law was a lock that asked for evidence before it would let her reach the evidence.

She had written the affidavit twice overnight. The first draft was anger. The second was dates, signatures, body-transfer windows, and the dead names Ava had circled in red before somebody put a knife in her throat. Lena kept reading one paragraph where she tied the trust's "security consulting" payouts to transport nights from the morgue corridor. The words were spare and careful. They still felt like begging.

Beau came in carrying two coffees in paper cups, one hand balancing the narrow case file they were gambling the month on. His tie sat loose, and his shirt stuck dark at the spine. He set a cup by Lena's elbow.

"Tastes like radiator water," he said. "Best this place has."

She did not touch it. "You talked to the clerk?"

"We're at ten-fifteen in chambers. Pike moved us up from afternoon."

"Why?"

He shrugged. "Why's anybody moving anything in this county? Somebody with more money than us got twitchy."

Lena slid him the draft warrant package. "I kept full scope. Trust disbursements, beneficiary identities, side-letter agreements, plus crematory manifests and county transport keys."

He read in silence, mouth working the inside of his cheek. "This asks for six years."

"That's when Gideon litigation restarted."

"And this asks for trustee emails and personal devices."

"Ava is dead. Two men were killed before her. Isaiah keeps showing up in places he can't walk into. I'm done pretending this is a tidy paperwork lapse."

He let out one dry laugh that had no humor in it. "You're not wrong. You're early."

"Early got Ava killed?"

"No. Sloppy gets the whole thing tossed."

She looked at him then, really looked. He had shaved too fast and left a pale nick under his jaw, one drop of dried blood beside his collar. He always talked practical when he was scared, like the right sequence could keep chaos on a leash.

"What do you want to cut," she asked, "so nobody important gets embarrassed?"

"Don't do that." He kept his voice low because two records clerks were sorting deed books on the far wall. "I want date-banded disbursements and manifests we can defend. We prove one shell company first. Then we come back bigger."

"By then they'll have burned the rest."

"Maybe. But if Pike calls this overbroad, we get nothing and we wear it."

Lena folded the corner of the affidavit until it creased white, then smoothed it flat. Her knee scar burned from too many stairs and too little sleep. She said, "I'm not asking for permission to be liked. I'm asking for enough paper to stop another body."

He looked at her for a long beat. "Then ask it in a way that survives appeal."

They walked upstairs without speaking. The courthouse smelled like old paper and bleach with coffee rotting underneath. Somebody had mopped too early and left gray streaks in the tile. In the hall outside chambers, a donor plaque wall gleamed with brass names Lena had seen on campaign signs and foundation galas. The same names from Ava's files. The same names on land parcels that used to hold Black families and now held hunting leases and tax shelters.

Judge Eleanor Pike's bailiff opened the door at ten-twelve and motioned them inside. He was a narrow man with a tremor in his left hand and a tiny stitched hummingbird on his cuff where a cufflink had torn out. He rode the latch with his thumb until the click came out small and embarrassed.

Pike sat at the end of her conference table in a cream suit despite the heat, posture straight enough to seem painful. A porcelain mug sat by her legal pad, untouched tea gone cold. She kept pressing two fingers to the skin above her right eyebrow, then dropping her hand like she had caught herself doing it. Her migraines were public gossip in the courthouse; today the overhead lights were off and one shaded lamp did all the work.

County Attorney Neal Graves stood to the side with a binder and a damp handkerchief. He nodded to Lena and Beau without warmth.

"Detective Waller," Pike said. "Detective Broussard. Please sit."

Lena sat. Beau stayed pitched forward in his chair, knees set to stand, coat still unbuttoned.

Pike lifted the affidavit packet. "You are requesting trust records, private account identifiers, communications between trustees and counsel, county transport rosters, and digital devices connected to named entities."

"Yes, Your Honor," Lena said. "The victims share a documented trust-litigation history. Payment cycles overlap with custody breaches and movement windows. We need complete records to identify who financed access."

Pike's voice stayed even. "The court is not a net. Particularity is not optional because a case is politically charged."

Lena felt heat climb up her throat. She heard Ava, not in words but in pressure: do not let them define clean as harmless.

"This is particular," Lena said. "It names one trust family, one transport corridor, and dates tied to homicides."

Graves cleared his throat. "Your Honor, the request for counsel communications is facially privileged. The request for all beneficiary identity data is disconnected from probable cause as currently sworn."

Lena turned to him. "One beneficiary class controlled payment authorization during each breach window. We can't test that without identities."

Pike touched her temple again, then picked up a fountain pen. "Cannot is not the standard. The standard is whether you have shown probable cause for each category sought. You have shown enough for a partial grant."

Beau spoke before Lena could. "Judge, if the court is willing, we can accept a narrowed date range and outgoing disbursement ledgers above five thousand. We can tie from there."

Lena cut her eyes toward him. He did not look back.

Pike wrote for a full minute. The scratch of nib on paper sounded louder than it should have.

"Order," she said. "The court authorizes seizure of outgoing disbursement ledgers, vendor payment schedules, and transfer manifests from Mercer Morgue and county crematory operations for the period beginning eighteen months before the first homicide and ending today."

She lifted her head.

"The court denies, without prejudice, seizure of beneficiary identity files, donor rosters, privileged communications, personal devices, and trust internal strategy memoranda. If subsequent evidence establishes a tighter nexus, you may renew."

Lena kept her hands on the table so nobody saw them shake. "Your Honor, if entities are layered, payment ledgers without ownership records can be decorative."

"Then bring me ownership nexus from what I have granted," Pike said. "This order stands."

The bailiff stepped forward for signatures. Pike signed first in careful strokes, then held the page out. A faint smell of peppermint oil came off her wrist when Lena reached for the pen.

Outside chambers, the hallway felt hotter than the stairwell to hell.

Lena did not wait for the door to close before she said, "You undercut me in there. I laid out probable cause, breach windows, and payment overlap, and you handed her a narrower ask before I finished."

Beau kept walking. "First warrant I ever carried alone, I asked for everything with a zip code. Judge called it a barn net and sent me home with nothing."

He glanced back at her. "I'm not doing that again. I gave Pike something she'd sign."

She caught his sleeve and stopped him by the donor plaque wall. The brass names shone over his shoulder.

"Procedure-wise, once we concede beneficiary files and counsel channels in the same hearing, the renewal clock starts from our own concession," she said. "That's not strategy. That's surrender."

His jaw tightened. "You think I don't know that? I do. But if we swagger in with theory and no ownership tree, she shuts us out and we're standing here with speeches instead of evidence."

"Talk radio isn't moving bodies. People with keys are."

"Exactly. And if you burn the warrant on pride, those people walk."

"This isn't pride. It's timeline."

"No," he said, and now his voice rose. "That's you deciding losing admissibility's an acceptable casualty so you can say you swung hard."

Two clerks looked over from the copier alcove. Lena dropped her hand from his sleeve.

"You want one shell at a time: pull vendor file, subpoena registry, map beneficial owner, then ask Pike again," she said. "I get the sequence. Bodies won't wait for it."

"You want the whole architecture right now," he said. "The law doesn't move on your clock, and if we pretend it does, we'll lose before we file."

"Bodies do."

He stared at her, tired and angry and suddenly older than forty-six. "Then we run the paper we got and stop bleeding each other in the hallway, or we're gonna be back here tomorrow with nothing but noise."

She let him go. Her pulse kept banging in her neck long after he disappeared down the stairs.

By one-thirty, rain had started in hard slanted sheets that rattled the courthouse windows and turned the parking lot into one broad puddle. The warrant team split by design and by resentment. Beau took two deputies to Mercer Trust Administration on Main. Lena took the second car to the service records annex behind the crematory, where manifests were supposed to be boxed by quarter and forgotten.

The annex door stuck in swollen wood. Inside, cold air fought humidity and lost. Cardboard boxes lined three walls, each marked by month with black marker. A woman in a denim skirt and bright yellow reading glasses waited at the desk, one ankle bouncing under her chair.

"I'm Mildred Cline," she said before Lena introduced herself. "Records compliance. I know what your paper says. I also have to leave at four for my husband's dialysis, so I need this to move."

"We'll move," Lena said, showing badge and warrant copy.

Mildred glanced at the badge, then at Lena's face. "You look like you haven't slept in a week. That's not a judgment. That's just eyes."

She stood and led Lena to stacked transfer binders. While she walked she rubbed the edge of a nicotine patch under her blouse sleeve, a small practiced motion she probably did without noticing.

"Chain sheets are in those blue folders," Mildred said. "Vehicle assignment tags are separate because accounting asked for easier billing. Whoever thought that up has never looked for anything in weather like this."

Lena pulled on gloves and began with dates around the Voss and Fenn murders. The manifests were a mess of carbon copies and corrections, but the law of paper held: people lied in stories and told on themselves in billing fields. She found two after-hours transport runs signed by initials only, then a third with the whole surname squeezed into the bottom margin: H. Reddick.

Her phone buzzed with a text from Beau.

`Trust office dragging. Counsel claims software outage.`

Then a second text.

`Get what you can. Keep it clean.`

She ignored the second one and kept turning pages. Vehicle 12 showed out-of-route mileage on the night between first-scene evidence lock and second-scene body appearance. The authorization line was half-legible. The invoice cross-reference pointed to an outside vendor: Delta Meridian Security Services, invoice batch DMS-44.

Lena took photos, then printed certified copies with Mildred watching each page like it might catch fire.

"Can I ask a thing?" Mildred said while the copier chugged. "My husband says I should stop asking questions at work. But I keep asking anyway."

"Ask."

"If this is about that old man they keep finding, why does money keep showing up in every room before y'all do?"

Lena met her eyes. "That's exactly what we're trying to answer."

Mildred nodded once, eyes narrowing on Lena's face before she looked back at the copier.

When Lena reached Mercer Trust Administration, thunder had moved east but the air stayed electric. Beau was in a conference room under recessed lights, tie off now, shirt sleeves rolled. He sat across from two trust employees and outside counsel, files stacked in exact squares. Through the glass Lena could see tension in the line of his shoulders. He was playing patient because that was his move.

Mara stood in the lobby pretending to read a tourism brochure, phone face-down on her knee. She wore a rain-dark jacket and had pen marks up the side of her hand.

"You're not supposed to be in here," Lena said quietly.

Mara did not look up. "I'm in a public lobby waiting for a quote. Also your face says something broke."

"Not now."

"Then give me one noun."

Lena hesitated, then said, "Disbursements."

Mara's eyes flicked up, sharp and quick. "You think they're paying movement crews."

"I think the payment calendar and body calendar kiss too often."

"That's a sentence I can use when you're ready to put your name on it."

Lena walked past before she could answer.

Inside the conference room, outside counsel introduced himself as Grant Moberly and complained about confidentiality with theatrical sorrow. He had a silk pocket square and mud on his loafers from the parking lot, which made Lena dislike him on principle.

"Detective Waller," he said, "we have complied fully within the four corners of your warrant."

"Then hand it over," she said.

Beau gave her a warning look, slight but clear. He was trying to keep temperature low.

The trust comptroller, a thin woman named Deirdre Shaw with ink-smudged fingers and a rosary bracelet she kept rotating around her wrist, slid three binders over. "Outgoing vendor ledger, scheduled disbursement calendar, and high-value transfer authorizations. Eighteen months. Exactly what the order says."

Lena sat and opened the first binder. Rows of payments marched down the page under bland names that sounded like office parks and weather systems: Cypress Strategic Holdings, Delta Meridian Security, Bayline Transport Solutions, Starlit Custodial Partners. Every one of them billed in amounts that sat just under additional reporting flags: $9,800, $9,750, $9,900.

"Who approves these?" Lena asked.

Deirdre looked toward Moberly before answering. "Dual sign-off. Foundation operations and external security liaison."

"Name."

Moberly stepped in. "Counsel's position is that personnel identities outside the warrant scope remain confidential."

Lena flipped to the disbursement calendar and felt her heartbeat change. Scheduled ACH batches landed on the first and fifteenth of each month, with special releases three days before board events and litigation deadlines. A note field on repeated entries read `site stabilization retainer - HR`. The next scheduled release sat five days away.

"HR," Lena said. "That stands for what?"

Deirdre's mouth opened, then shut.

Moberly answered for her. "Internal coding."

"Internal to who?"

"Internal to trust operations."

Beau leaned forward, voice flat. "Mr. Moberly, obstruction isn't confidentiality. The warrant covers outgoing disbursement ledgers and payment schedules. Coding legend is part of that record."

Moberly folded his hands. "The legend contains protected references."

"Protected from whom?" Lena said. "The dead?"

Beau shot her a look that said do not blow this up. She bit down on the next sentence until she tasted copper.

The door opened before anybody could respond. Deputy Hollis Reddick walked in without knocking, rainwater beading on his forearms, patrol polo stretched across gym-cut shoulders. A faded military tattoo ran from his wrist toward his elbow, something like crossed anchors and a date hidden in shading. He carried a half-empty bag of spicy peanuts and smiled like this was his break room.

"Y'all still doing taxes in here?" he said. "Main desk said I could grab signatures for transport keys."

Nobody spoke for a second. Hollis looked at Lena and tipped his chin.

"Detective Waller," he said. "Long day?"

"For some of us," she said.

He popped a peanut in his mouth and leaned over Deirdre's chair. "Need that release form on Bayline. If it misses four o'clock, dispatch throws a fit and I get yelled at by people with softer hands than mine."

Lena watched Deirdre slide a single-page authorization toward him. The header carried the same trust logo as her binder. The signatory line read `External Security Liaison: H. Reddick.`

He signed with a fast slash, then whistled under his breath while he looked at Lena again.

"You all right?" he asked. "You look like somebody sold your weekend."

"Get out," Beau said, not loud.

Hollis laughed once. "Relax. I'm leaving."

He tapped the folder with his knuckle, collected the signed page, and walked out still smiling.

The room stayed quiet after the door clicked shut. Rain ticked at the windows. Somewhere in the hall an old copier started and stopped, started and stopped.

Lena turned the authorization form toward Moberly. "Internal coding. Sure."

Beau did not look at her. "We need certified copies of every authorization carrying this liaison field, plus the full coding legend and batch transmission logs."

Moberly lifted his chin. "Your warrant does not include transmission logs."

"Then we'll note your refusal," Beau said.

"You can note whatever you like."

Lena spoke before Beau could smooth it over. "No, we'll note exactly what you're protecting and exactly when you protected it."

Deirdre flinched. Her rosary bracelet clicked against the table.

"I can print the legend," she said quietly. "If that's in scope."

Moberly turned to her. "Deirdre."

"It's in the disbursement binder appendix," she said, eyes down. "Appendix C."

Beau held Moberly's stare until the attorney looked away.

"Print it," Beau said.

Appendix C was three pages of abbreviations and euphemisms. `HR` expanded to `Hollis Reddick / External Security Liaison.` `SSR` meant `site stabilization retainer.` `CRT` meant `courier routing transfer.` There was nothing subtle about it once the key existed.

Moberly leaned across the table. "That appendix is internal work product. Stop copying it."

Deirdre's hand froze over the copier controls. Beau slid the warrant copy between them and tapped the seizure paragraph with two fingers.

"It's attached to the disbursement binder you produced," he said. "Copy every page."

Lena laid Appendix C beside the crematory manifests from Mildred's annex. The dates aligned like teeth in a zipper. Retainer release on the first. After-hours transfer on the third. Body appeared on the fourth. Again in the next cycle. Again before Ava.

Her stomach clenched so hard she had to brace her forearm against the table. Sweat slid under her shirt between her shoulder blades. She bit the inside of her cheek until copper filled her mouth and kept reading.

Beau read the two stacks in silence. His finger stopped on Hollis's initials, then moved to the calendar entry for next week.

"Five days," he said.

"Until the next release," Lena answered.

"Could be routine security work."

"Could be payroll for evidence movement and scene staging. Retainer on the first, transfer on the third, body on the fourth. Three cycles."

"Could be both," he said.

There it was again, the split dressed as caution.

Lena gathered the copied pages into evidence sleeves. "I'm filing for an emergency expansion and a freeze by six."

"Not yet."

She looked up. "Excuse me?"

"Not yet," Beau repeated. "We've got linkage, not a full ownership tree. If we file now and Pike swats it again, we show our hand and lose the next swing."

"The next disbursement hits in five days. ACH clears, shells drain, inboxes get scrubbed. We don't have five days."

"I chased an arson case off one invoice and a hunch years ago. Filed too soon, defense gutted the warrant, and the owner walked. I'm not handing Hollis that gift."

"Tomorrow gives them a full night to scrub accounts."

He rubbed both hands over his face and lowered them slowly. "Lena, I know what this looks like. I know who his uncle is. I know exactly how bad this is. But if you accuse the sheriff's nephew on partial paper and a coding appendix, they'll bury you and call it reckless, and then we're..."

"I'm already buried," she said. "I'm digging with my fingernails."

"Then don't snap them off on the first hard rock."

She almost laughed. It came out as air. "You want me to wait because waiting feels professional."

"I want you to hit with enough force to break something that can't be glued back together by six men in suits."

Outside, Mara stood up in the lobby when they came through the conference door. Her eyes tracked the evidence sleeves in Lena's hands.

"You got a name," Mara said.

Lena said nothing.

Beau answered before she could. "No comment."

Mara looked from him to Lena and back. "You two aren't on the same page."

"We're on the same case," Beau said.

"That's not what I said."

Lena stopped at the lobby window where rainwater ran in crooked lines over the glass. In the parking lot, Hollis leaned against his cruiser under the awning, laughing with a patrol sergeant, peanut bag rolled in one fist. He looked loose, even happy. He looked like a man who had never had to imagine his own name on a warrant return.

She could still feel the paper in her hands, the dry rasp of copied toner, the little coded initials that had turned into a person with a badge and family protection.

Mara stepped beside her. "If this is what I think, you can't sit on it long."

Beau came up on Lena's other side. "If this is what you think, a leak kills admissibility before we get the expansion."

Mara did not blink. "A body every few weeks kills people faster than admissibility memos."

"And a blown case kills the only shot at conviction," Beau said.

Lena stood between them, rain noise filling the gap where trust used to fit. Her knee ached. Her jaw hurt from clenching. She thought of Ava's red-circled names and of Pike's pen scratching partial permission onto a page like mercy.

"We do this my way for twelve hours," she said finally, eyes on Hollis through the wet glass. "No story tonight. No leak. We file emergency expansion with what we can certify before dark."

Mara folded her arms. "Twelve. Then I decide what I owe the public if this stalls."

"Fair," Lena said.

Beau exhaled through his nose, not relief, not agreement, only truce. "I'll work Secretary of State filings and UCC shells. You build the caption and exhibits. We regroup at eight."

In the parking lot, Hollis flicked his peanut bag into a trash can and climbed into his cruiser. The engine turned over. He backed out slow, one hand on the wheel, the other tapping time on the door, letting two open gaps pass before he eased into the lane.

Twenty-three minutes later Lena pushed through the courthouse side entrance with rainwater still on her sleeves and Appendix C clipped to the front of an emergency packet. At the duty window, the clerk scanned each exhibit, checked the case caption aloud, and brought the red stamp down hard: `RECEIVED 5:58 P.M.`

"Emergency expansion and temporary freeze request logged," the clerk said, tearing off the receipt strip. "E-service to Mercer Trust counsel and registered agent went at 5:59. Confirmation ID 26-EM-1047."

Beau took the receipt from Lena, read the service line, and let out a low breath. "That's done. Moberly can't bury a notice he already got."

Mara looked from the receipt to Lena. "Twelve hours started when that stamp hit, not when we shook on it."

Lena slid the stamped receipt into the evidence sleeve and felt the paper edge bite her palm. Hollis was already out on county roads, but now his name sat in a live docket that would not unring. At eight she'd leave her marked unit in the courthouse lot, climb into Beau's mud-spattered Tahoe, and work the levee crowd where Hollis liked to be seen.


# Chapter 10

At eight, by the time Lena parked below the levee, the catfish oil had been hot for three hours and everybody at the long tables smelled like cornmeal and river wind. The volunteer fire tent snapped and sighed in the gusts coming off the dark water. Cicadas ran a high electric line through the trees. Somebody's speaker kept losing signal, coughing in and out between old soul and church announcements.

She had left her unit in the courthouse lot and borrowed Beau's mud-spattered Tahoe, because people in Mercer talked different when they did not see county decals. It was the same strategy she hated when Clay used it, charm where force would leave bruises, but this was not Clay's room. This was folding tables and paper trays and women arguing over tartar sauce recipes while they filled to-go orders for men already halfway drunk.

Beau killed the engine and patted his shirt pocket twice, checking for the roll of antacid he carried now that he had crossed forty and would still eat anything dropped in hot grease. He wore clean jeans for once and a short-sleeve button-up with tiny blue shrimp stitched into the cloth, the kind of shirt that pretended to be funny so people looked at that instead of his badge belt tan line.

"You sure he's gonna stay?" Lena asked.

"Otis stays till the raffle, always," Beau said. "He thinks every raffle's rigged and keeps showing up to prove it."

Otis Mayfield sat near the far edge of the tent where the light reached him in stripes. He had his guitar case upright between his boots like it was another person at the table. The man was all elbows and knuckles, the skin on his hands polished thin with age and strings. His plaid shirt was pressed hard enough to cut paper, and he still wore the black armband from his sister's funeral six months ago, though he had unpinned the flower and replaced it with a brass safety pin shaped like a fishhook. He salted his tea before he took a sip, a habit he swore kept his voice from cracking, and nobody around him bothered to argue.

Lena carried two plates over and set one down in front of him. "Mr. Mayfield. You said if we came where the fish was fresh, you'd talk."

Otis looked at the plate, then at her. "I said I'd listen while y'all chewed. That ain't the same thing as talk." His gaze cut to Beau. "Boy, this slaw's too sweet. Somebody got scared of vinegar."

"That'd be Sister Almeta," Beau said. "You tell her that to her face, she'll beat you with the serving spoon."

Otis snorted. "That woman already did, in '92, and I deserved worse. Sit down. You standing over me like intake at county lockup."

Lena sat. The bench bowed under all three of them. Grease popped in the fryers. Somewhere behind the tent a kid practiced trumpet scales with no mercy for the right notes.

"I need to ask you about the old icehouse," she said.

Otis pinched a hush puppy in half and looked at the steam like he could read it. "There you go. You couldn't let a fish supper be a fish supper."

"I can eat and ask." Lena broke her own fillet with her fingers. It burned. She welcomed that. "You told Reverend Holt there used to be night hearings down there. Off the books. Before men got dragged to the trees."

He held the hush puppy over his plate until the steam thinned, and the little humor in his face flattened. "Night hearings," he repeated. "That's what y'all calling it in paperwork? Makes it sound like folks got a chance to appeal."

"What do you call it?"

"I call it men got tuned up till they signed whatever white folks needed signed. Then a day or two later everybody acted surprised there was a rope in play." He took one careful bite and chewed like his jaw hurt. "Language matters, Detective. You're too smart to pretend it doesn't."

Beau wiped his fingers and leaned back. "She ain't pretending. She needs what you remember, that's all."

"All?" Otis laughed once, sharp. "You asking me for fifty years of ghosts and you saying that's all."

Lena let that sit. The wind pressed the tent wall inward, then released. She could feel her old instinct to move fast, stack questions, box uncertainty before it spread. That was what command liked. That was what got witnesses shut.

"Start smaller," she said. "How many nights did you see people brought there?"

Otis rubbed his thumb over the edge of his cup. "Seen? Two, maybe three. Heard about plenty more. Most nights you did not look direct. Looking direct got noticed."

"Who brought them?"

"Sheriff trucks. Deputy trucks. Sometimes private cars with county men riding shotgun." He glanced toward the raffle table, where numbers were being shouted into a microphone too loud for the speaker. "Headlights off once they hit levee road. One lantern at the loading door. You could see that from the cutbank if you were dumb enough to be out there."

"What year?"

"You think I kept a diary?" He pointed at his temple with two fingers. "Memory don't run neat. It comes like rain in spots. I know one night was before they paved 17, because I still had red clay stuck in my truck tires. I know one night was after my first baby was born, because I remember hearing her cry when I got home and feeling thankful for the sound." He looked down at his plate. "She died at twenty-eight. Blood clot. I still hear that newborn cry easier than yesterday morning."

Lena nodded once. "I'm sorry."

"Don't do polite on me." He reached for a piece of fish from her plate without asking. "If you want useful, ask useful."

She slid her plate closer. "What did the place smell like?"

Otis blinked, surprised, then gave her the first unguarded half-smile she'd seen. "Now that's a question from somebody who's actually been in bad rooms." He tapped the table twice. "Ammonia. River mud. Old blood that got hosed but not gone. Burlap rot. Diesel off idling engines. You'd get a little mint too, from somebody chewing to keep from puking."

Beau's jaw tightened. "You ever go inside?"

"Once." Otis said it flat and did not lift his eyes. "A deputy told me to play for a supper there, said they'd pay cash. I was nineteen and stupid and needed gas money. I walked in with my guitar and saw a boy tied to a chair with baling wire around his wrists. No shirt. Back striped up so raw the skin had started shining." He touched the black armband at his sleeve as if checking it was still there. "A man at a table was writing names in a ledger and not looking up. I put my guitar back in the case and walked out before anybody decided I was useful. They docked me half pay for leaving early."

No one at their table spoke for a few seconds. The tent noise carried on around them, indifferent and bright.

"You can put that in a statement," Lena said quietly.

Otis barked a laugh that made two nearby women turn their heads. "No, ma'am. You can put that in your own head. Statement means my name on county paper, and county paper leaks faster than a jon boat with dry seams."

"We can seal-"

"Don't sell me courthouse weather reports." He pointed a greasy finger at her. "You can't promise what that building won't do."

She could not. Not honestly. Clay treated confidentiality like campaign yard signs, useful until the wind changed.

"Then give me place," she said. "No names. No oath. Just place."

Otis chewed the inside of his cheek and looked away toward the levee crown. Kids ran up and down the slope with glow sticks, shrieking every time one of them wiped out in the grass. The sound made him smile despite himself.

"You remember Junior Tate?" he asked Beau.

"The one with three thumbs if you count that knot?"

"That's him. Dumbest smart man I ever knew." Otis chuckled. "Junior said if you couldn't find trouble in Mercer, look for where the road forgot its own name. Icehouse was on one of those. Past the fertilizer scales. Past a lightning-split persimmon tree that leaned like it was praying wrong. Back then, there was a culvert with one corner busted and a rusted buoy somebody dragged up from the river hanging on a post."

Lena repeated it under her breath until it stuck. Fertilizer scales. Persimmon. Broken culvert. Buoy.

"That helps," she said.

"Helps ain't testimony." Otis wiped his fingers clean with a napkin, then folded the napkin into a neat square and set it under his cup like a coaster. "Don't get greedy because I ate your fish."

The raffle winner got called, and half the tent erupted when it turned out to be Sister Almeta herself taking home a pressure washer she'd donated. Otis laughed so hard he wheezed and pounded the table. Beau laughed too, and Lena felt her own shoulders drop for the first time all day. It was stupid and local and not for leverage. For two minutes she let it be exactly that.

When the noise settled, Otis's face hardened again.

"One more thing," he said. "Night court wasn't just beatings. They did paperwork there. Heard charges read like church liturgy, only every line ended with somebody else's land changing hands. Men signed blank sheets when their hands were shaking too bad to hold a pen."

Lena felt that sentence in her chest like a fist. The ledgers from last week were columns and shell names. Here was the bridge between ink and blood.

"Who read charges?" she asked.

Otis shook his head. "Voice changed. Sometimes old man, sometimes young. Sometimes white, sometimes not. Anybody who wanted to keep living learned not to turn toward the voice."

He stood, joints cracking, and hooked two fingers through the handle of his guitar case.

"Interview's over," he said. "I got to play two songs so these people can pretend they're dancing in rhythm."

He walked toward the makeshift stage beside the raffle table, moving slow but steady. Lena watched him go and understood exactly what she had and what she did not. Human context, no sworn testimony. A handful of landmarks and a memory that bled at the edges.

Beau leaned close. "We can work with that."

"Maybe," she said. "Maybe we just heard enough to know how little a courtroom will take."
