"Courtroom ain't tomorrow." He popped two antacids, chewed, and grimaced. "Search is."

By the time Otis finished his second song, the sky had gone from purple to a flat iron gray and the first heat lightning was crawling behind the trees. Volunteers started breaking down tables. Oil drums got capped. Somebody brought out lidded buckets for leftovers and argued over whose freezer had room.

Lena and Beau waited by the back of the Tahoe while she spread photocopies across the tailgate. Parcel maps, floodplain overlays, trust transfer records from the warrant fight, and a county roads sheet so old it still marked a ferry crossing that had washed out before Lena was born. Wind kept lifting corners. She pinned them with her phone, Beau's flashlight, and a plastic tub of tartar sauce packets somebody had abandoned. Every corner that popped loose tightened something in her chest: if she could not keep paper in place, she could not keep this case in place either.

Otis came offstage toweling sweat from his neck with a Saints hand towel gone almost white from washing. He set the guitar case down and looked at the papers without touching them.

"You still here," he said.

"Needed daylight while we've got it," Lena said. "Can you point where those landmarks sit now?"

"Now?" Otis's voice went flint-hard. "Now half that land belongs to folks who bought history cheap and call it development." He tapped one parcel with his nail. "This one used to be a bait shed. That one used to be Bell family row crop. That one used to be where my cousin got his jaw wired shut for arguing taxes." Lena felt her teeth set. The parcels stopped being neutral shapes and turned into injuries with fence lines.

Beau angled the flashlight lower. "We're not asking you to testify tonight. Just orient us."

"And tomorrow?" Otis asked.

Lena answered before Beau could. Her knee gave a damp-weather throb, old scar waking, and she caught herself reaching for process language before she reached for truth.

"Tomorrow I file for a search. If command signs it, we move before dawn. Your name doesn't go in the request unless you choose it."

"If command signs it," Otis repeated, tasting the words. "Meaning Sheriff Reddick and whoever he owes this week."

She did not deny it. She could already hear Clay turning this into talking points before sunrise.

"Then hear me plain." He stood with one hand on the guitar case handle, thumb worrying the old brass latch. "I am not giving deposition. I am not sitting in county conference room with a recorder blinking at me. I am not walking into a courthouse where men still smile like 1963 never ended."

He had gone formal on purpose, every clause set like fence posts. Fear did that too. Lena saw it and felt her own pulse speed.

"Okay," she said, too quick. "No deposition. No formal interview. We can classify source material as confidential pending corroboration and-"

Otis pinched the brass latch on his guitar case and clicked it twice, then stopped speaking altogether.

She heard herself and stopped. Procedure first, exactly the thing he distrusted. She put both palms on the tailgate and chose plain words.

"I can't protect you from all of it," she said. "I can keep your name off my first report. I can keep this between me, Beau, and whoever has to stand at that site with us. That is all I can promise and still tell the truth."

Otis studied her for a long time. Rain started as two fat drops on the tailgate map, then paused again.

"You talk like your daddy when you're scared," he said.

The sentence hit harder than it should have. Lena kept her face still.

"Did you know him?" she asked.

"Knew of him. Knew him enough to nod at feed store. Knew he carried himself like a man trying to balance two buckets on one yoke." Otis shrugged. "Sometimes he spilled from one side. Sometimes from both."

She thought about the signature she'd found in the sealed chancery files, her father's name in ink under documents that moved Black land into safe white custody. Balance was one word for it. She had darker ones.

"I'm not him," she said.

"No." Otis's mouth twitched. "You are meaner when cornered. Might help you. Might kill you."

Beau let out a breath that was almost a laugh. "Can we get back to roads before lightning picks this tailgate as a target?"

Otis crouched by the guitar case. Instead of opening it for the instrument, he reached under the neck rest and slid out a long envelope spotted with old grease. He sat back on his heels and held it in both hands for a moment like he was deciding whether the paper weighed more than wood.

"I drew this after my nephew asked where not to park when he goes noodling," he said. "Told him none of this and still sent him another way." He offered the envelope to Lena but did not release it yet. "You don't get to call this evidence in open court unless you can prove what you find without me."

"I understand." Lena wrapped her fingers around the envelope. It was soft at the folds and smelled like cedar oil and old paper. Otis let go.

Inside was a hand-drawn map on the back of a church revival program from 2019, lines done in blue ballpoint and retraced in pencil where his hand had shaken. The river bend was there, the levee spur, the drainage ditch, and an X boxed three times over a rectangle labeled ICE. He had marked the broken culvert with a tiny square and written busted corner. He had drawn a persimmon tree as a crooked fork and added, in cramped letters, struck by lightning but still living.

He had also written three names in the margin and crossed two out so hard the paper almost tore. The third name remained: M. Kearney. No title, no date.

"Who's that?" Lena asked.

Otis's jaw tightened. "Dead two years now. Used to keep scales at the fertilizer yard. He saw trucks. That's all you need." He pointed at the map. "Road in from the east washes out after hard rain. If you're going before dawn, come from levee side and kill lights after the second cattle guard. There is still chain-link on the north wall with a cut big enough for a body."

Beau whistled low. "You been back there recently."

Otis looked offended. "I fish. I check traps. I keep my own weather. You think history sleeps because y'all stop asking it questions?"

Thunder rolled, close enough to shake the tent poles. People cursed and ran for trucks with covered pans clutched to their chests.

Lena folded the map along its old crease, then changed her mind and flattened it again so she could photograph both sides. She sent the images to her secure case folder and watched the upload crawl in weak signal. Fragile lead, fragile chain.

"You need anything from us tonight?" she asked.

Otis barked a humorless breath. "Yeah. Leave before somebody sees us talking too long and decides to make it useful." He picked up his guitar case and rose with a grunt. "And Detective?"

"Yeah?"

"When you write whatever you write, don't make me brave. I ain't brave. I'm old, and I'm tired of carrying a county's teeth marks in my sleep." He jerked his chin toward the map in her hand. "That paper's what tired looks like."

He walked into the rain before she could answer, head down, case swinging against his leg. Halfway to the parking lot he turned to shout back at Beau.

"You still owe me twelve dollars from that Saints bet, liar."

Beau shouted after him, "You changed the spread after kickoff!"

Otis raised one hand without turning around.

By the time they got into the Tahoe, the storm had opened all the way. Wipers knocked hard from side to side. Water sheeted off the windshield and turned the levee lights into smears.

Lena set the envelope on her lap and kept one hand over it as if it might slide away. The map was not testimony. It was not chain-clean. It could be attacked six different ways before breakfast. It was also the first object in this case that told her exactly where to stand.

Beau pulled onto the road slow, headlights cutting through rain and gnats and low fog rising off ditch water.

"You thinking pre-dawn?" he asked.

"We have to," Lena said. "If anybody else hears about this, we lose the site."

"Clay'll want to be first through the door for cameras."

"Then we move before cameras wake up."

She looked down at Otis's cramped lettering, at the note by the tree struck by lightning but still living, and felt the old split inside her tighten again: procedure and memory, paper and blood, what could be proven and what had always been known. The rain drummed louder on the roof. Somewhere behind them, under a collapsing tent and a generator hum, a man with tired hands packed up his guitar and walked home through the weather he trusted more than any courthouse in Mercer County.

Before she slept, she slid Otis's map and envelope into a gallon freezer bag from the fish-supper cleanup, the plastic still carrying a faint fried catfish and lemon pepper smell, and set her alarm for before dawn.


# Chapter 11

Lena turned off the levee road lights two bends before the icehouse and drove the rest by memory and Otis Mayfield's map, the paper sealed inside a gallon freezer bag that still smelled faintly like fried catfish and lemon pepper. The map's pencil line for the back approach wavered where Otis's hand had shook or laughed or both. It brought her exactly where he said it would: a rutted service track behind a dead loading dock, floodwater standing black in the low places, the old brick shell of the icehouse leaning toward the river like it had given up holding its own weight.

The storm had moved east before dawn, but the heat had not broken. It sat on her skin like wet cloth. Cicadas kept a high metallic whine in the trees behind the building, and levee wind moved through rusted sheet metal with a throatier sound, as if someone was slowly tearing canvas.

Beau killed the engine and tapped the dash twice before he got out, the superstition he had never admitted was one. He had quit cigarettes again this month, and he cracked sunflower seeds into an empty ibuprofen bottle while he drove; the bottle sat in the cup holder now, half full of shells and bitter spit. He thumbed his duty light on, checked the extra batteries in his shirt pocket, then looked at Lena.

"Tell me we're not about to find one old cot and six dead raccoons," he said.

"I'd love six dead raccoons," Lena said. "Dead raccoons don't lawyer up."

He snorted once. "You get sentimental this early, I'm calling Dr. Rao and reporting neurological symptoms."

Crime-scene tech Trina Cade climbed out of the rear unit with two camera cases and an evidence roll cart. She had a cartoon dinosaur Band-Aid wrapped around her thumb where she had pinched it fixing her son's bike chain before shift, and the edges had gone gray with fingerprint powder. "Floodwater's about three inches at the door," she said. "I brought the tall boot covers, not the cheap ones. You're welcome."

Lena gave her a nod and checked her watch: 4:41. They had a county warrant signed at 11:18 the night before, narrow but valid, and a four-hour window before traffic and politics woke up all the way. She had counted on the clock, on darkness, on the possibility that whoever had hidden this place still believed nobody local had the map.

She had not counted on mercy. She had counted on speed.

The side entrance lock was a swollen disk of rust. Beau set the bolt cutter jaws and leaned in with his shoulder. Nothing. He cursed softly, reset, leaned harder. The cutter slipped, snapping his knuckles against the steel hasp. He hissed, shook his hand, set again.

"Don't bleed on my first chain log," Trina said without looking up from her camera setup.

"Don't run commentary till coffee," Beau said.

The third bite sheared halfway through the shackle. Lena finished it with a pry bar, the metal giving all at once with a sound like a gunshot under a blanket. They froze and listened. The only sounds were cicadas and wind. There were no engines.

Lena read warrant language into Trina's body cam for entry record, gave time and location, then pushed the door.

Cold, wet air came out carrying mold, old ammonia, river mud, and something sweet-rotten underneath, the scent you got when cardboard sat damp for years and rats nested between documents. Their lights cut through suspended dust and gnats. The first room held a conveyor frame with no belt, broken pallets, an ancient walk-in unit hanging open with no compressor attached. Floodwater sloshed over concrete up to Lena's ankles, lukewarm and slick with algae.

"Foot placement," she said. "No quick turns."

"Yes, ma'am," Beau said, mock-formal, then pointed with his light. "Office door. Back left."

The office had once been cinder block with a wire-mesh window. Now the mesh had a square cut through it and bent inward. Somebody had made entry here before and not long ago; the cut edges were bright where rust had been disturbed. Lena touched the metal. It was not dry. It was not old.

Inside, shelves lined both walls. Banker boxes, three deep in places, labels in two kinds of handwriting and three kinds of marker. Ledger spines stood upright in milk crates, leather darkened by humidity. Trina swore under her breath.

"Holy hell," she said. "This isn't a stash. This is a library."

Lena felt the room change shape around her. It was not because the office was small; it was because all the air now had weight.

She went shelf by shelf, calling labels while Trina photographed.

"`MERCER CHANCERY - SPECIAL HEARINGS - OCT 62 TO FEB 64.`"

"`DISBURSEMENT AUTHORIZATIONS - GIDEON TRACT - CLOSED.`"

"`COUNTY TRANSFER RECEIPTS - COURTESY COPIES.`"

"`NIGHT COURT DOCKET B.`"

Beau lifted one ledger from the second shelf, both hands under it like it might split. "This one got numbers on the edge. Could be indexing."

"Don't open yet," Lena said. "Photos first."

Her voice came out flatter than she intended, the public voice she used in hearings and on reports. Under it, her pulse kicked hard enough that she could feel it in her gums.

Trina moved fast, circling, flash muted with a taped diffuser so they did not white out the ink. Lena wrote a rough item sequence in her notebook, water spotting the page from her sleeve. Box 1 through Box 9 from east wall. Three loose ledgers from floor crate. One steel dispatch case under desk. She knelt to read the case tag and got mud on both knees.

The tag read: `MC-SH/ARCH TEMP / 04-17`.

She stood and looked at Beau. He had read it too.

"Sheriff archive temporary," he said. "That's our house code."

"Was our house code," Lena said.

He did not answer.

From the third shelf, a half-open ledger showed onion-skin carbon sheets curled at the corners. Lena should have waited for full photo sequence. She did not. She touched the edge with gloved fingertips and lifted one page enough to read three lines.

Case Number 43-B.

Property hearing conducted without claimant present.

Transfer approved pending sheriff certification.

At the bottom of the page sat initials she knew from old report signatures and from the notebook she had slept beside for weeks: D.W.

Her father's hand had always made the W too tall, like it was trying to stand up from the line.

She lowered the page and stepped back. She became aware again of water around her boots, of the low hum of Trina's camera, of Beau breathing through his nose in quick shallow pulls when he was trying not to say something stupid for comfort.

"We've got enough for probable-cause expansion," Lena said. "We bag priority ledgers and the steel case first."

Beau nodded. "I can carry two at a time if the bottoms hold."

Trina zipped open evidence bags and said, "If these are damp all the way through, we need breathable containers in the truck. Plastic's going to cook them by noon."

"We transfer to paper once logged," Lena said. "Get me wide shots of all four walls before we touch anything else."

Trina pivoted and fired off the sequence. Through the window cutout, the sky had gone from black to bruised navy. Lena checked the time again: 5:03.

She heard engines before she saw light. Two heavy vehicles, one lighter. Not county units; the idle sounded smoother, less rattled. The noise came in fast, then cut at once.

Beau's head snapped toward the door. "Company."

Lena killed her flashlight and moved to the office threshold. Headlights washed across the main room in white bars. Doors opened. Boots on waterlogged concrete outside. Somebody called, "Federal task force! Announce and hold your position!"

Lena swore once, quietly, and clicked her body cam to live stream.

When she stepped into the first room, five people in tactical vests were already inside the side entry she had forced. Two more came through the loading bay with hard cases. A white box truck with government plates idled beyond them, rear door rolled up.

The woman in front held up an ID wallet with practiced impatience. "Special Agent Celia Kearns, Civil Rights Division task force." She was maybe forty, maybe less, with neon-orange running shoes under federal-issue cargo pants and a nicotine patch just visible above her collarbone. She pressed two fingers against that patch every few seconds as if checking it had not slipped. "This site is under federal seizure authority as of zero four fifty-six. You're ordered to cease search activity and surrender all material already handled."

Lena did not move. "Show me your signed order."

Kearns handed over a printed notice clipped to a chain-of-custody cover sheet. Federal letterhead. Statute citations. No itemized inventory. A magistrate signature block marked `telephonic authorization`, name redacted in the copy.

"This is not an item transfer," Lena said. "This is a hold notice."

"It supersedes your local warrant under civil-rights seizure authority," Kearns said. "You can challenge it in court. You do not challenge it on this floor."

Behind her, one of the agents said, "East office shelves, B-seventeen through B-twenty-four, then the floor crate by desk." He said it like he had walked the room yesterday.

Lena looked at him, then at Kearns. "You already had an internal map."

Kearns's mouth tightened. "Detective, you're obstructing a federal seizure. Step back now."

"Not without transfer log." Lena held up her notebook. "Anything that leaves this building gets itemized against local sequence, with signatures from both agencies. That's custody law, not ego."

Kearns leaned closer, voice low enough that only Lena and Beau could hear. "You're treating this like a county HR dispute. It's a federal civil-rights seizure."

"Then act like it and log your seizure properly."

Beau moved to Lena's left shoulder. "Ma'am, we can do this clean in five minutes. Nobody here wants a fight."

He sounded almost friendly, but his stance had gone workboat-steady, feet set wide for slippery deck, knees loose. He had worked docks before patrol and he trusted weight distribution more than rank.

A second county SUV rolled up outside. Sheriff Clay Reddick came through the door adjusting the cuff of a blue campaign polo that still showed stitch marks where a donor logo had been picked off. He smelled like mint and expensive soap, as if he had changed in a hurry and still remembered to shave.

"Everybody take a breath," Clay said, hands out. His tone was all press conference. "We're in joint territory now, and our federal partners have the lead. Let's keep this clean."

Lena turned on him. "You knew."

"I got a call before five."

"You knew before we entered." 

Clay gave her the false-courteous smile he used when he wanted to appear patient in front of cameras. "Detective Waller, this is not the place for assumptions."

"They called box numbers before they crossed the threshold."

"We're keeping order," Clay said. "Stand down."

There it was: order, the noun he used when he meant control.

Lena looked at Beau. He gave the smallest shake of his head, a warning and a question in one movement. She understood both. If she pushed too hard, Clay would make the push the story.

She looked back to Kearns. "You'll sign for each item."

Kearns gave a curt nod to her team, not to Lena. "Move."

Two agents went past Lena into the office. One carried preprinted barcode strips. Another held a tablet already loaded with placeholder entries instead of blank fields. They were not documenting discovery; they were executing a list.

Trina stood by the shelves with her camera against her chest. "You can't bag over active local capture," she said.

An agent took hold of her elbow. "You're done."

"Hands off my tech," Lena said.

The agent released Trina, but not before leaving four wet fingerprints on her sleeve.

In the office doorway, Beau planted himself between the first outgoing crate and the hall. "Need your signature," he said, holding out Lena's notebook and a pen. "Item by item."

The agent with the crate kept moving. "Out of the way."

"Sign and go."

"Move."

The crate tilted as they both adjusted for footing. Water sloshed. Beau reached to steady it, one hand on the box edge, one on the agent's forearm. A second federal agent came from the side and tried to peel his grip. Beau jerked backward, boot sliding on algae-slick concrete. The first agent yanked the crate up. Beau's right hand caught between plastic lip and steel doorframe.

There was a wet pop, not loud, more like snapping a green bean close to a microphone.

Beau made a noise Lena had never heard from him, raw and high and instantly cut off when he clamped his teeth down. The crate dropped. Ledger corners hit water. Beau went to one knee, then both, right wrist bent wrong under him.

"Stop!" Lena shouted. "Everybody stop moving!"

Nobody stopped at first. One federal agent was still trying to keep hold of the crate handle. Trina was cursing now, full voice. Clay said, "Hold it," too late and too calm.

Lena shoved between bodies and got to Beau. His face had gone gray under the stubble. Sweat ran off his nose in a steady line. He cradled his right forearm against his chest with his left hand, trying not to look at the wrist.

"Don't move it," Lena said.

"I wasn't planning to dance," he said, then sucked air through his teeth so hard his words broke. "Fuck. Fuck."

The distal forearm had already started swelling. The wrist angled inward in a way wrists did not angle. Lena had seen enough fractures to know this one was not a clean line.
The angle punched heat up the back of her throat. She had told him to hold that doorway, and now his shooting hand was folded wrong because she had chosen speed over backup.

Kearns crouched two feet away. "We'll call EMS."

"Do it," Lena said. "And your people step back now."

Kearns signaled and they backed, but only two steps. The crate with the dropped ledgers sat half in water. The pages had fanned at one corner.

Lena looked up at Clay. "This is on your scene. Write it like it happened."

Clay's jaw worked once. "Let's keep the temperature down."

"His wrist is broken in half and you're talking temperature."

Clay did not look at Beau. He looked at Kearns. "Can your medic assess while we finish transfer?"

Lena almost laughed from the cruelty of how practical it was. She did not laugh.
Guilt came metallic at the back of her tongue. She wanted to blame algae, federal elbows, and Clay's timing; the order to move before dawn had still been hers.

"Nobody touches those ledgers until they're photographed where they fell," she said.

Kearns nodded to one of her agents, who took two quick pictures with a body cam still rolling. "Documented," she said.

"For you," Lena said. "Not for us."

Sirens rose from the road, close. While they waited, federal agents kept moving evidence in a line to the truck. The first load past Lena was the steel dispatch case tagged `MC-SH/ARCH TEMP / 04-17`, still wet where she had touched it.

"That case gets logged against my local sequence," Lena said, shoving her notebook and pen toward the bearded agent carrying it. His vest patch read `MERRITT`.

Merritt looked to Kearns instead of to her. Kearns answered for him. "No local co-signs. Federal intake only."

Lena kept the pen out. "Then write your barcode and your name in my book."

Merritt walked past her shoulder and loaded the steel case into the truck without touching the pen.

The next container was a gray humidity bin holding the half-open ledger from shelf three, page edge still stamped `43-B`, her father's tall `W` visible through the wet plastic sleeve. An agent slapped on a temporary strip that began `BX-1179-` before his glove covered the rest and snapped the lid shut.

"Say that serial again," Lena said.

"Stay clear," the agent said, and kept moving.

A paramedic crew came in with a splint bag and pain meds. The lead medic, a woman with silver cat-eye frames fogged from humidity, cut Beau's sleeve and palpated gently. He cursed her, then apologized, then cursed again when she stabilized the wrist.

"Probable distal radius with displacement," she said. "Could be ulna involvement. He needs X-ray now."

"He rides with me," Lena said.

Clay stepped in before the medic could answer. "He rides with EMS. You follow."

"And scene custody?"

"Federal lead."

"Local witness log?"

"You'll get what they release."

Her gut tightened at the practiced script: they would release what served them and bury the rest under process.

As the stretcher wheels rattled across broken concrete, Beau grabbed Lena's sleeve with his left hand. His grip was weak and shaking.

"Don't let them rewrite this," he said.

"I won't."

"I know you won't," he said, eyes squeezed shut. "I said don't let them." 

His blood had soaked through her cuff at the icehouse and dried there, stiff against her wrist. The stain felt more binding than any warrant signature she had carried in.

Then the medic pushed and he was gone through the door into gray dawn.

By the time Lena finished giving initial injury statement to county dispatch, the white truck was nearly full. Federal seals went across the rear door in bright red tape. Kearns signed a federal transfer sheet listing `MC-SH/ARCH TEMP / 04-17`, one humidity bin coded `BX-1179-44?A` on the smeared duplicate, and seven banker boxes from the east wall. She marked the county signature line `not required` and pushed Lena's notebook back unopened.

"You'll receive itemization through official channel," Kearns said. "Do not contact my team directly."

"You're not even pretending this is cooperative," Lena said.

Kearns held her gaze for one long second. "Cooperation requires trust in both directions."

"You came with a shopping list and no inventory."

"I came because somebody in your county kept burying bodies inside evidence systems." Kearns looked toward the office where water still pooled around empty shelf outlines. "You're not the only person who can see what this is."

Then she turned and walked to the truck, pressing the nicotine patch at her collarbone again, twice, hard.

Lena stood in the doorway with wet socks, mud to her calves, and nothing in her hands but a warrant copy and a notebook full of sequence numbers with no receiving signatures. She had visual confirmation. She had chain collapse. She had no admissible possession of what mattered.

When she finally got in her unit to follow the ambulance, the dashboard clock read 6:12.

She sat for three breaths before turning the key.

On the fourth breath she called Dr. Rao.

He picked up on the second ring, voice grainy with no sleep. "Tell me this is not what it sounds like in dispatch chatter."

"It's worse," Lena said. "Meet us at Mercer General if you can."

"I'm already on my way there for another consult. Send me what you wrote down before you clean it up."

She looked at her wet notebook, at the smeared ink where B-19 bled into the margin. "I don't have enough."

"Send what you have," Rao said. "Enough is a moving target."

Mercer General's emergency corridor smelled like bleach, stale coffee, and the sour edge of floor wax not fully dry. Fluorescent tubes buzzed overhead in uneven pulses. Lena sat on a molded plastic chair outside imaging while rain started against the windows in light, hard taps, not enough to cool anything.

Beau came back from X-ray with a temporary sugar-tong splint and a face like he had been scraped hollow. He held his right arm across his lap, elevated on a pillow wrapped in a sheet. His knuckles were already turning violet under freckles.

"Comminuted distal radius," the ER physician said. "Maybe intra-articular. Ortho will set and cast once swelling allows. He's out of field duty for now."

For him, "for now" meant weeks, maybe months. For a cop with his dominant wrist broken, it meant paperwork he hated and a sidearm qualification he could not pass until healed. It meant Lena losing the one partner who could read her half-sentences in a bad room.

When the physician left, Beau stared at the closed curtain and said, "You were right about the place."

Lena leaned against the wall, arms folded tight to stop her own shaking. "I'd rather be wrong and bored."

He laughed once and winced. "Yeah."

Silence settled for a beat, then shifted.

"You moved too fast," he said.

His words landed soft, almost tired, and that softness cut deeper than a shout.

Lena kept her voice level. "We had a valid warrant window and a source who'd already delayed us twenty years by being scared to talk."

"You moved before we had state backup."

"State backup would've put Clay in the loop earlier."

"Clay was already in the loop, Lena." Beau looked at her then, eyes bloodshot and bright with pain meds and anger both. "That's my point. You ran a sprint in a race somebody else started yesterday."

She opened her mouth, then closed it. The fluorescent light flickered above his bed and came back.

"If we'd waited," she said finally, "the shelves would've been empty when we arrived."

The sentence left a grit taste in her mouth. She still could not tell whether she was defending the warrant window or defending herself.

"Maybe," Beau said. "Or maybe my wrist still works and we lose the same boxes anyway."

The image of his hand pinned in the steel frame flashed bright and exact, skin gone white before the pop.

The truth of that sat between them with no place to set it down.

Sheriff Clay appeared in the corridor outside the curtain, speaking to a deputy before stepping in. He had swapped the campaign polo for a pressed white shirt, sleeves rolled to exact mid-forearm, but the mint smell was the same. He carried a legal pad with nothing written above the fold, the blank top strip looking like a redaction waiting to happen.

"How's our patient?" he asked.

"In pain," Beau said. "And listening."

Clay gave a short nod that could pass for concern from a distance. "Workers' comp paperwork's already moving. We'll code this as line-of-duty under joint federal action and keep him covered."

There was no apology in it, only coverage codes. Lena felt heat climb her neck.

"Classify it as what happened," Lena said. "He was injured in a custody dispute your office concealed."

Clay's eyebrows lifted just enough to signal offense without committing to it. "Let's avoid loaded framing in a hospital corridor."

Behind Clay's shoulder, Beau shifted and the splint crackled against the sheet. The sound pulled the icehouse doorway back into Lena's throat.

"You had pre-notice and withheld it."

"I got preliminary notice of possible federal interest. That's not the same as operational certainty."

"They knew exact shelf numbers before entry."

"Could've come through a half-dozen channels."

"Name one that doesn't run through you."

He gave her that patient smile again, the one that erased content and preserved tone. "Detective, your job right now is to document your observations and quit improvising jurisdictional fights you can't win."

"My job is homicide evidence integrity."

"Your job is what I assign while you wear this badge." He tapped the legal pad once with his pen. "There'll be a joint statement at nine thirty. You won't freelance with press. You won't contact federal personnel directly. You route every request through my office."

He tore off a second sheet and held it out until she took it. The header read `Administrative Communication Restriction - Case Cluster 43-B`. The second paragraph read: `Any unauthorized direct contact with federal personnel or media triggers immediate relief from duty pending internal review.` A deputy in the hall photographed Lena taking the page for receipt.

Beau shifted on the bed, face tightening. "Clay, with respect, that's a choke chain."

Clay looked at him with rehearsed sympathy. "It's structure."

"It's control," Lena said.

He ignored her and addressed Beau. "Take the week, maybe two. Heal up. We'll put you on desk review after cast."

Beau's laugh was dry as paper. "Desk review with one hand. Living the dream."

Clay wrote something on the pad at last, tore off the top sheet, and handed it to Lena. It was a single line with a case contact number for federal liaison, no names.

"Use that if you need status updates," he said. "And for your own sake, let this settle before you decide what it means."

"It already means something," Lena said.
Lena did not crumple Clay's order. She photographed it beside Beau's intake wristband and sent the image with her raw scene timeline to county risk-management and her own case archive before Clay reached the elevator. Then, before the pain meds blurred him out, she had Beau dictate exactly who touched the crate, where his hand was pinned, and which item tag he saw before he went down. She wrote every word, including the curses.

Clay tucked the pad under his arm. "Meaning is not evidence." He looked at Beau. "I want you back healthy. That's not rhetoric."

Then he left, shoes quiet on the waxed floor.

Beau watched the curtain sway behind him. "He lies like a pastor," he said.

"He lies like a man who's practiced in mirrors," Lena said.

A nurse came in to hang fluids, humming old R&B under her breath and tapping one chipped red fingernail against the IV pole while she counted drops. She checked Beau's splint, adjusted his pillow, and said, "If either of you rips that cast once ortho sets it, I will haunt your grandchildren." Then she smiled without waiting for a response and moved on.

At 8:07, Dr. Naveen Rao stepped into the corridor carrying two paper cups and a manila envelope tucked under his arm. His scrub cap was gone; his hair stood up in the back where he had run his hands through it all morning. A tiny brass chess knight dangled from his ID reel, clicking against his badge when he walked. He set one cup beside Lena without asking and sat in the chair opposite her.

"Cardamom tea from the volunteer desk," he said. "Tastes like hot regret, but it has caffeine."

Lena wrapped both hands around the cup for the heat. "You saw dispatch?"

"I saw enough to miss breakfast and step on one administrator's ego." He glanced toward Beau's bay. "How bad?"

"Wrist fracture. Dominant hand."

Rao closed his eyes for a beat and exhaled through his nose. "I'm sorry."

He pulled the manila envelope into his lap but did not open it yet. He looked once down the corridor, making sure Clay was not in sight, then lowered his voice.

"Before you ask, no, I wasn't invited into the federal circle," he said. "I arrived at the icehouse at six twenty with a field contamination kit because your voicemail said wet paper and possible biological carryover. They blocked me at perimeter." He tapped the envelope. "But somebody dropped this while loading."

He slid out a strip of thermal printer paper, the kind used for quick chain labels. On it was a barcode and typed text:

`US-CRT-MSR-EV / BX-1179-443A / DOC-MIX / TEMP HOLD`

Lena felt her pulse in her throat again.

"You got this where?"

"Under the ramp lip by the truck," Rao said. "It had river mud on one side and fresh adhesive on the other. I photographed location before picking it up. Time-stamped."

"You picked up federal property."

"I picked up litter that would have washed into the river in ten minutes." He held her eyes. "I also memorized the string before I touched it. Then I wrote it down on my glove in case the strip fell apart. I'm tired, Lena, not reckless."

She looked at the code again, forcing herself to read each character twice.

BX-1179-443A.

One concrete number in a morning designed to erase specifics.

"If that tag is real," she said, "it maps to their intake ledger."

"If they keep one worth subpoenaing," Rao said. "Which is an if."

Beau called from behind the curtain, voice rough. "Subpoena all the ifs anyway."

Rao stood and stepped into the bay. "Good morning to you too," he said.

Beau lifted his splinted arm half an inch and dropped it back with a grimace. "Morning's generous."

Rao checked the cast wrap and distal circulation with two quick fingers. "Pain level?"

"Eight if I'm honest, six if I want to leave today."

"Honesty first," Rao said. "Masculinity can wait in the parking lot."

Beau gave a cracked grin despite himself. "You always this sweet with cops?"

"Only the ones who try to wrestle evidence crates with their shooting hand." Rao adjusted the sling and looked directly at him. "You need this healing straight. If the radius sets wrong, you'll lose grip strength for months. That means no weapon retention and no emergency braking with that hand. You understand me?"

"Yeah," Beau said quietly. "I understand."

Rao nodded and stepped back out into the corridor with Lena.

"I can run the serial through pathology shipping contacts," he said, "not officially. Somebody in Jackson owes me a favor and hates federal grandstanding on principle. It may return nothing, or it may return destination facility."

"Do it," Lena said.

"I will, but hear me first." Rao's voice tightened; detail density climbed the way it did when he was trying not to overstate. "This morning was not random agency overlap. Their team had prebuilt barcode schema, preassigned quantity placeholders, and transport humidity bins staged in the truck. That means planning horizon of at least several hours, likely more. They did not pivot because of your entry. They timed around it."

Lena leaned her head back against cinder block and closed her eyes for one second. Inside the lids she could still see shelf outlines and her father's initials on wet paper.

"Interagency concealment," she said.

"At minimum," Rao said. "Could still have lawful core. Could still be dirty as hell. Both can coexist."

She opened her eyes. "I hate when you're careful."

"You hate when I'm right in slow motion." He held up one hand before she could answer. "I'm not saying give up. I'm saying stop chasing a full retrieval fantasy. Work with fragments before fragments disappear."

She looked at the serial strip again and folded it into her notebook behind her sequence list. "One fragment," she said.

"One is more than zero," Rao said. "And one can become chain if you anchor it fast."

At 8:31, rain thickened outside and then thinned. The corridor lights buzzed, flickered once, steadied. A housekeeping cart squeaked past, then doubled back because someone had spilled coffee near triage.

Lena stood there with bleach smell in her nose, mud drying on her pant cuffs, Beau's blood from the icehouse still dark on one sleeve where she had caught his arm. She had entered the morning with a warrant and a plan to carry proof out in banker boxes. She had left with an injured partner, no custody, and a single serial number written on thermal paper.

It was not enough to prosecute.

It was enough to keep the lie from closing clean through the nine-thirty cameras, until she could get back to Beau after dark.


# Chapter 12

By the time Lena found Beau's room again, the hospital had folded into its night smell: bleach riding over fryer grease from the cafeteria, old coffee burnt to tar in a pot nobody remembered to empty, hand sanitizer sharp enough to sting the back of her throat. Fluorescent tubes hummed above her with that thin insect whine county buildings always had, one bulb near the nurses' station flickering in a slow pulse that made everybody's skin look bad. Her knees felt hollow. She had slept ninety minutes in a plastic chair and woke with the cuff of her jacket imprinted across her cheek.
The thermal strip Rao had pressed into her hand in the corridor was still folded behind her sequence list, the numbers already smudging at one edge.

Beau sat propped in bed with his right forearm wrapped hard white from knuckles to elbow, the cast already smudged where he'd bumped it against a rail. Someone had taped his call button to the blanket because his pain meds made him fumble. The TV was on mute with closed captions sprinting under footage of somebody else's fire. He looked at Lena, looked away, and used his good hand to lift a paper cup of melting ice chips. He missed his mouth the first time and cursed under his breath like he was talking to a stubborn bolt.

"You took your shot," he said. "You got us there before dawn and got us exactly what? A federal boot in our throat and my wrist cracked like kindling."

Lena stayed by the door, hand still on the metal push bar as if she might need to leave fast. "I pushed the entry because we had a clean weather window and no guarantee the ledgers would survive another day. If we'd waited for--"

"Don't hand me the memo version." He turned his head then, eyes red not from crying, from no sleep and morphine and rage held too long. "I was there, Len. I heard that lock give. I smelled wet paper and rat shit and motor oil and I thought, for five seconds, we finally had 'em. Then they rolled in with a seizure order stamped pretty as a wedding invitation."

She crossed to the sink and set down the coffee she'd brought him, black with two sugars because she remembered and because remembering felt like proof she had not broken everything yet. Her voice came out in the flat on-record register she used when she was trying not to shake. "I know what happened. I know what it cost you."

"No." He shifted, winced, and pressed his shoulder into the bed frame until the pain passed. "You know the report version. You don't know what that pop sounded like in my arm. You don't know the second after, when I thought, stupidly, not my hand, not my right hand, not now."

That landed clean. It punched through the procedural script she had been hiding inside since dawn. Beau wrote everything by hand before he typed it. He carried a carpenter pencil behind his ear half the shift because his father had done the same on shrimp boats, and he never trusted pens when his palms got slick. A broken wrist for him was not just pain. It was competence gone unreliable.

Lena pulled the visitor chair closer and sat. The plastic squealed on the tile. "You're right," she said. "I don't know that sound. I know the sound of choosing wrong in front of people I need alive. That's what I've got."

He watched her for a long moment, jaw grinding through invisible gristle. The monitor beside him ticked out his pulse in patient, indifferent beeps. "I almost asked Clay to move me off this case," he said.

She felt the blood leave her face before she could stop it. "You should, if that's what keeps you standing."

"I said I almost did." His mouth twitched, not quite a smile, more a tired surrender to the fact that he still cared. "I didn't because I kept seeing one thing I should've said sooner."

The door eased open before he could continue. Ida Waller came in carrying a foam clamshell and a church bulletin folded into a fan. She wore a navy dress with a thin white collar and sensible shoes polished enough to catch the hallway light. Her fingers, knuckles enlarged by arthritis, worked the bulletin without looking at it, a steady back-and-forth that made a soft papery breeze.

"You two done biting chunks out of each other?" she asked. She set the clamshell on Beau's tray and tapped it twice. "Catfish. No pepper flakes. Last time one red flake had you at the altar."

Beau snorted despite himself. "Ms. Ida, I didn't cry."

"Baby, if your eyes water and your nose runs at the same time, that's crying with extra steps." She turned to Lena, gave her one quick scan that took in the wrinkled shirt, the stale coffee smell, the tremor in her left hand. "Eat something that didn't come from a vending coil. I'm going downstairs for ice."

She left before Lena could argue. Ida's practicality under stress had always passed for ordinary errands until you noticed every errand kept somebody from falling apart.

When the door shut, Beau exhaled through his teeth and nodded toward the hallway. "Your mama's the only person in this building who scares me more than federal paper."

"She's got range," Lena said.

That earned the smallest actual smile. Then it was gone. Beau leaned forward a fraction. "Night before the icehouse, I was at the morgue loading bay. It wasn't official. I went to grab the spare evidence camera 'cause ours was eating batteries. Around one-thirty, Hollis came through in a county refrigerated van with lights off. He wasn't parked at intake. He backed into the side dock where dietary gets produce."

Lena straightened. Fatigue dragged at her, waterlogged and stubborn, but this cut through. "Who was with him?"

"One driver I didn't know. Ball cap pulled low. Could've been anybody, could've been nobody. Hollis had a yellow pallet jack and one of those gray archival totes with the red latch. I remember because he clipped the dock plate and cursed the same way he does at traffic stops, with the old assumption the world owed him smoother pavement."

"You logged it?"

"I logged the van plate in my field notebook and told myself I'd run it after shift." He looked at his cast. "Then all hell kept scheduling itself."

Lena had seen Hollis in enough hallways to know his swagger voice and his friendly-deputy smile. She had not pictured him sweating behind a dark dock with a pallet jack. That image changed the geometry of the case: this was not abstract corruption but bodies and boxes moved by men with cousins in the right offices.

The door opened again, quick this time. Dr. Naveen Rao stepped in with a clipboard under one arm and a pair of reading glasses tucked into the collar of his scrub top. His hair had a flattened streak on one side from where he'd slept against a wall, and he was peeling the label off a water bottle in thin spirals while he talked, an unconscious habit when his brain outran his mouth.

"Broussard, your discharge is tomorrow if you stop trying to prove machismo through noncompliance." He glanced at Lena. "Detective. I was looking for you anyway."

He slid a folded glove wrapper from his pocket and opened it on the tray table. Inside, written in marker, was a string of letters and numbers: TR-44-1187-P.

"Serial from the federal crate tag," Rao said. "I gave it to you in the corridor, but you stared through me for a second, so here. I've checked twice against what I saw before they boxed everything."

Lena read it once, then again, letting each character settle. "You remember this clean?"

"I remember what I can defend under oath and what I can only defend to myself." He met her eyes. "This one is the second category, so handle accordingly."

"Thank you," she said.

Rao tucked the peeled plastic strip into his pocket instead of dropping it on the floor, nodded to Beau, and left with the efficient irritation of a man who trusted very few institutions and still showed up to work inside one.

Beau tapped the glove wrapper with one blunt fingertip. "That's your breadcrumb."

"One breadcrumb and no admissible loaf," Lena said.

"Then we bake ugly." He settled back, grimacing as the cast thudded the bed rail. "You still got Mara's photo dump from probate? The ones from that moldy room where the fan kept rattling toward death?"

She still had it: hundreds of rushed images from chapter-seven trespass, including file spines, shelf labels, half-legible docket tabs, and accidental corners of rooms they had not meant to capture. They were probably useless by courtroom standards, but useless was not the same as empty.

Lena stood. Her legs carried the bite of an all-night march on levee gravel. "Sleep," she told him.

"Can't with this arm." He raised the cast a little. "But I can think. Call me before you move on any live lead, not after, and don't do that thing where you decide to carry all the weight by yourself and call it strategy. If you freelance another entry, I'm off this case the minute this cast comes off."

There was no clean apology large enough for what she had broken, but there was this: he was still in it with her on terms she did not control. Hurt did not erase allegiance. It changed its texture and set hard edges around it.

"I'll call before I move on anything," she said.

Ida returned as Lena reached the door, balancing a cup of chipped ice and two contraband packets of hot sauce in her palm. "Take one," Ida said, holding out a packet. "For your purse. Emergency medicine."

Lena took it, because refusing gifts from her mother at moments like this counted as a kind of blasphemy.

Outside, the hallway lights buzzed. Somewhere down the corridor a child cried in short, furious bursts and a nurse murmured a song Lena recognized from Sunday service. She pressed the glove wrapper flat against her thigh and walked faster.

***

By midnight, the courthouse annex had gone quiet except for the old HVAC knocking in the vents and a storm beginning to drag its fingernails across the windows. Lena used her reinstated badge to get into Records Room C, then wedged the door with a busted law reporter because the lock liked to seize when humidity climbed. The room smelled like mildew, toner, and old glue. Two metal fans pushed warm air in opposite directions and did not cool anything.

At 11:38 p.m., Mara had pushed an encrypted folder to Lena's burner drive and sent one text in all caps: NOT COMING IN. CLAY'S PEOPLE ARE PARKED OUTSIDE MY BUILDING. I'M ON AUDIO.

Now Lena propped her phone against a stack of probate digests and opened a three-way call. Beau's voice came in from his apartment, a little slurred from pain meds but steady. Mara joined through a wired headset from somewhere with an ice machine rattling every few minutes.

"You look like a bar fight with paperwork," Mara said through the speaker.

"You sound judgmental for somebody hiding behind motel coffee."

"Fair. I sorted the probate photos by timestamp and focal length so we can separate intentional shots from accidental background capture. If the serial appears, odds are it's in a blur."

"I made a list of every place Hollis could've staged that tote between dock and freezer," Beau said. "You want it now or after y'all stop insulting each other's nutrition choices?"

"Now," Lena and Mara said at the same time.

They worked in layers. Lena started with the obvious photos, including shelf signs, binder labels, and banker boxes with probate stamps. Then she worked the throwaways, including blurred corners, floor tiles, door hinges, and fluorescent reflections in glass. She keyed TR-44-1187-P into the annex inventory terminal and got nothing but a dead-end error from a database that treated older records like rumors. She moved to manual index books and turned pages until paper dust coated her fingertips gray.

At 12:47 a.m., her vision doubled for three breaths. She blinked hard, pinched the bridge of her nose, and almost marked the wrong line: TR-44-1178-F, juvenile tax photos, irrelevant. Exhaustion did what exhaustion always did, sanding precision into guesswork.

She snapped a photo of the index page and dropped it in the call thread. Mara answered in four seconds. "Wrong suffix. You skipped a row."

"I saw it," Lena lied.

"No, you hoped it," Mara said. Her tone stayed dry, not cruel. "Drink water before you invent evidence."

Lena obeyed. The tap in Records Room C ran hot before it ran cold, and the water tasted faintly metallic. She drank anyway.

At 1:12 a.m., Beau spoke up from the speaker. "Quit digging in evidence logs. Pull probate procurement instead. Those gray totes are clerk furniture, not sheriff gear. Different bucket of money, different paper trail."

That was the workman mind she trusted: start with the object, ask who paid for it, and follow the hand that signed. Mara pulled county purchasing PDFs she had scraped months ago for an unrelated story about mold remediation, then walked Lena through the file path over the call. Lena opened a 2019 invoice bundle for thirty archival transport crates with gray polymer, red latches, and internal foam rails. The delivery location was Probate Photo Archive, sub-basement east wing, and the asset tag prefix was TR-44.

"There," Mara said. "Invoice language says trust records, not transport. That's a naming choice, not a typo."

Lena felt a small sharp click in her chest, that mechanical certainty when pieces finally seated. She pulled up the chapter-seven photo batch from the night archive break-in and filtered for red color dominance. Image 7-143 opened with a blurry shelf, an out-of-focus stool, and the corner of a gray tote. She zoomed until the pixels broke and the world turned blocky. The tag came into focus one character at a time: TR-44-1187-P.

The number matched, and the tote matched. Lena had seen it months before federal agents ever laid a glove on the icehouse boxes.

"Cross-match holds," Mara said softly, as if loud speech might spook the fact back into uncertainty. "Timestamp, tag prefix, unit number. Same crate."

"Don't give me newsroom poetry," Beau said. "Say it so a tired deputy can't miss it."

Lena answered. "The crate the feds seized started life in Probate Photo Archive inventory on the trust records line, not sheriff evidence issue. Somebody moved trust-archive material through morgue logistics."

A beat of silence held across all three lines. Rain thickened against the annex windows. Somewhere down the hall, a pipe knocked twice.

"Which somebody?" Beau asked.

Lena opened another folder and spread printed copies across the table, including partial warrant returns from chapter nine, disbursement schedules with half the names redacted, bank routing slips tied to shell entities, and one ugly photocopy of a trust governance amendment where the toner had blown out the margins. She smoothed the page flat with both palms.

"Read the signature block," Mara said.

Lena had read it before and filed it under later, after they recovered cleaner originals. Later had burned down with the seizure. She read it again now, slower:

Acting Disbursement Authority - Thomas Pike.

Judge Eleanor Pike retained oversight, but routine release authority for quarterly trust transfers sat with her son. Thomas approved payout timing. Thomas approved recipient bundles. Thomas touched the valve.

In this case, people who touched valves ended up dead, framed, or useful.

Her phone buzzed. Ida's name lit the screen. Lena answered on the first ring.

"You still in that paper cave?" Ida asked.

"Yeah."

"Then listen while I drive. Thomas Pike called your father every year before harvest gala season because he couldn't read people and your father could. He wanted to know who was angry enough to embarrass him in public." Ida's turn signal clicked in the background, steady as a metronome. "Men like that don't fear bullets first. They fear witnesses."

"How do you know it was Thomas on those calls?"

"Because he always introduced himself full name like a bank lawyer, even to people who knew his diaper size." A breath, then softer: "Baby, when rich boys start calling old deputies for crowd advice, something ugly is already in motion."

The line clicked off before Lena could ask more. Ida had never liked lingering at the edge of confession.

"Your mom just gave us motive behavior and event timing," Mara said.

"She gave us a pattern," Lena said. "Don't print it."

"I know."

Lena did not treat Ida's call as proof. She opened the decoded pages from Deputy Waller's notebook and found three entries from prior gala weeks that used the same shorthand: TP CALL / CROWD READ / 0199. She ran 0199 through the county contact directory and got the Pike Foundation executive office line assigned to Thomas's desk.

Then she opened the gala operations packet from the chapter-nine warrant return. The run-of-show put Thomas onstage for the redevelopment speech at 9:32 p.m., then moving through Service Corridor B to a private board room for disbursement signatures with two witnesses and one family lawyer. The security annex showed one private guard on that corridor, no metal wand, and a blind camera angle by the breaker closet where catering carts stacked before dessert service.

The next scheduled trust release sat forty-nine hours out, attached to gala disbursements and donor pledges. Three supports sat in front of her now: trust authority paperwork, historical call-pattern logs, and a predictable movement corridor with weak screening.

She wrote on a yellow pad in block letters:

THOMAS PIKE = CURRENT TRUST VALVE

NEXT EXPOSURE WINDOW: 49 HOURS

LOCATION STACK: HOUSE / COURTHOUSE / FOUNDATION HALL

Beau whistled low over speaker. "Hell. That's not a suspect tree; that's a deer stand aimed at one man's ribs."

"Yeah," Lena said. Her stomach cramped with hunger and adrenaline and too much coffee gone acidic. "If we're right, Thomas Pike is the body at risk. The attack lane is Service Corridor B between the ballroom and board room when he steps offstage to sign. One person can jam a catering cart, shove him into that blind spot, and put a blade under his ribs before anybody in formalwear hears him hit tile. If we miss that window, he dies in that hallway, command locks the building for optics, and whoever set it up gets clean minutes to strip signature packets or stage the next lie."

"Then put a source on the next move," Mara asked over the speaker. "Do we warn Pike tonight, or are we documenting why we waited?"

Lena considered Clay, his polished concern voice, and the way he could turn any warning into a press asset by dawn. She considered Eleanor Pike's legal armor and the county's appetite for spectacle. She considered Beau in a cast because she had wanted one more hour on a flooded floor.

"We'll notify command per protocol," she said. "Timestamp it clean. Then we build our own prevention map before they slow-walk this into a press line."

"You're assuming bad faith as a baseline," Mara said. "What are you hanging that on besides pattern memory?"

"Their pattern," Lena said. "They call it sensitive, schedule a meeting, and run the clock out."

Beau cut in, rough with fatigue. "Len, this isn't a permit pull. Give notice so they can't say you hid intel, then put bodies where bodies need to be."

There it was again: not obstacle but ally, practical and wounded and still pulling toward the work.

Lena gathered the documents into three piles, including what could survive court, what could only guide strategy, and what could get them all indicted if it left the room. She labeled each with black marker and the date. Outside, thunder rolled over the levee and rattled the annex glass in its frame.

At 2:03 a.m., she called the sheriff's duty line and logged a threat assessment tied to Thomas Pike's trust authority and forthcoming disbursement schedule. She kept her voice measured, gave no flourish, and asked for an immediate protective planning conference at first light. The deputy taking the call sounded nineteen and terrified of spelling anything wrong.

When she hung up, she took the hot-sauce packet Ida had pressed into her hand and tore it with her teeth, squeezing a stripe onto a stale pecan roll.

"That is revolting," Mara said through the speaker.

"It's calories," Lena said, chewing.

"It's a hate crime against pastry."

"Put it in your notes."

"Already did," Mara said.

When the call finally dropped, Lena squared the three labeled piles at the table edge for the first-light conference and kept working until the annex windows went dishwater gray.

Lena taped the yellow pad page to the wall above the printed sheets Mara had sent, drew a box around 49 HOURS, and underlined it until the pen tore paper.

The case they could prove in court was still broken open on the floor somewhere between county pride and federal jurisdiction. The case they could act on was suddenly plain: a man with a signature, a schedule everyone knew, and a county that kept pretending violence arrived like weather instead of paperwork.

She looked once at the serial number on the glove wrapper before sliding it into an evidence sleeve marked OFF-RECORD LEAD. Then she set a new legal pad on top of the old one and started building the protection grid for Thomas Pike's house, courthouse route, and gala ingress, line by line, while the storm leaned harder against Mercer County and morning crept in anyway.


# Chapter 13

The rain had quit twenty minutes before sunset, but the lot behind the sheriff's annex still held heat like a cast-iron pan. Water steamed off the cracked asphalt and lifted the smell of diesel and wet clay into the room through the propped back door. Lena stood at the whiteboard with a dry-erase marker that had gone mushy at the tip and drew the Pike Foundation Hall from memory, the same route she'd been tracing behind her eyes since before dawn: ballroom rectangle, kitchen spine, donor entrance under the awning, service lane that ran behind everything like a vein nobody wanted to admit was open.

The first-light conference she had forced onto the duty log had burned through the day and returned the same answer in cleaner language: not enough bodies, not enough authority, not enough time.

She wrote numbers in each corner, then crossed half of them out.

"That is what we have," she said. "Not what we asked for. What we have."

Beau sat sideways in a metal chair, his taped right wrist resting in his lap like he had borrowed it from somebody else. He kept rolling a peppermint lozenge against his left cheek until it clicked his molars. The cast had been cut down to a brace, but the skin at his knuckles was still yellow-green where blood had pooled weeks earlier. Each click took Lena back to the icehouse crack and left a sour film on her tongue.

"Four sworn on foot," he said. "One on parking-lot sweep and one in a cruiser at the donor gate. If transport doesn't run late, maybe we steal Nguyen for the first hour."

"Transport will run late," Lena said.

"It always does." He shrugged. "I'm trying to earn my optimism badge."

On the long table, the printed staffing grid curled at the edges from humidity. Two deputies were out with fever. One was on mandatory court watch. Overtime had to be approved through Clay's office now, and Clay had answered every request with the same line through his aide: preserve operational stability, avoid overreaction, maintain confidence.

Confidence had become a budget category.

Lena circled the service corridor on the map until the marker bled through the board's glossy skin. Her shoulder ached from redrawing the same weak point, and she heard her own cadence tightening into statute-speak. "This is the breach lane. We don't have legal authority for pre-event sweeps of private contractor closets or equipment trunks unless we can articulate probable cause tied to a specific offense. We can't do preventive detention because we're scared somebody is going to make us look stupid. We can run visible perimeter. We can run random bag checks at the public entrance. We can post one body at this door and pray they keep line of sight."

Beau tipped his head toward the corridor circle. "Prayer's not in county policy."

"Then call it forecast."

The door opened and closed with a soft hydraulic sigh. Eleanor Pike stepped in without scanning the room first, as if the room had already agreed to itself. She wore a navy suit too heavy for September in Mercer County, pearl studs, and the flat expression of somebody who had slept with one eye open and still missed the alarm. Her makeup was neat except at the temples, where fine powder had lifted in crescents around sweat.

Thomas Pike followed her, carrying a cardboard archive box against his chest. He set it down as if it weighed much more than paper. His tie knot was centered to the millimeter. He smoothed it twice anyway and glanced at Lena with a smile so quick it read as accidental.

"Detective Waller," Eleanor said. "Detective Broussard. Thank you for seeing us on short notice."

Beau stood halfway and thought better of shaking hands. "Judge. Mr. Pike."

Lena capped the marker, feeling the plastic barrel dig a groove into her palm. "This is the short notice. We're less than twenty-four hours out from your gala. We need a cancellation."

Eleanor folded her hands at her waist. "No."

The single syllable landed clean, with no anger in it. Just closure.

Lena pulled out a chair for Eleanor anyway. Eleanor remained standing. Thomas sat, then stood again, then sat once more. He slipped an antacid tablet from a foil packet and chewed it dry.

"Judge, listen to me plain," Lena said. "We've got a credible threat window around this event. The pattern is public, your household controls trust disbursement, and your husband is the gatekeeper on signatures. If he's in that room tomorrow night, he's a viable target."

Thomas lifted a hand. "I'm right here. You can talk to me."

"I'm talking to both of you." Lena kept her eyes on Eleanor. "Cancellation drops your exposure and compresses access points we can't currently cover."

Eleanor exhaled through her nose. "Cancellation invites panic, litigation, and political interpretation that this office has conceded criminal liability. The foundation has scholarship checks tied to tomorrow's pledge cycle. Contracts are signed. Vendors are paid. Donors are already in town." Each legal noun hit Lena like another dead bolt thrown in a hallway she needed open.

"Donors being inconvenienced is not my concern."

"It should be, if your objective is county stability." Eleanor glanced at Beau, then back to Lena. "You know how quickly rumor becomes a run on institutions here."

Lena felt the old urge to lecture statute and threat matrix until somebody surrendered. When pressure climbed, her voice always narrowed into training-room cadence, numbers and clauses stacked so high nobody could breathe under them. Ida used to tap her wrist and tell her to slow down before she talked herself out of being heard.

She let the manual go.

"I watched a public defender get killed on county asphalt," she said. "I watched my partner's wrist snap because every agency in this state wanted the same boxes for different reasons. Don't sell me stability like it's neutral."

Eleanor's jaw flexed. "Do not assume I am untouched by this." She revised herself mid-breath. "I mean, do not assume my family is insulated from risk merely because we host a fundraiser."

Beau scraped his chair back and leaned over the map. "Judge, we're not trying to win a philosophy contest. We're trying to keep your husband breathing through dessert. Help us lower variables. Move the event. Strip it down. Virtual speech, no crowd, no open bar, no backstage maze."

Thomas gave a brief laugh that sounded more like coughing. "If I announce from my kitchen, every reporter in the Delta will write that I'm hiding in my pantry."

"Better than hiding in a casket," Beau said.

Thomas dropped his gaze to his hands. He had clipped his nails to the quick, and one finger had a fresh crescent where he had bitten too far. "I hear you," he said quietly. "I do. But we've got families coming from Jackson, from Clarksdale. Alumni kids who already bought dresses they can't return. We're honoring faculty retirements. It's not only donors."

Lena had worked enough county events to know there were always two rooms: the room where people talked about children and civic duty, and the room where money moved under those words like river current under hyacinth. They were both real. One room just got used to excuse the other.

"Then we change format," she said. "Short program. No receiving line. One entrance, one exit. Metal detector wanding at all bags. We keep everyone out of service lanes."

Eleanor said, "Private security has jurisdiction over interior access under our contract. They have made their position clear."

"Their position can change."

"Not without legal cause, Detective. You know this." Eleanor's voice thinned, and she reached into her tote for a pill vial, dry-swallowed one tablet, and put the vial back with careful fingers. Migraine meds. "We are proceeding."

Silence held for three beats. Rainwater dripped somewhere in the hall behind them with metronome patience.

Lena met Beau's eyes. He gave one small nod, the kind that meant: We are not winning this room.

She flipped the staffing grid around and slid it toward Thomas. "If you insist on this event, I need signatures acknowledging operational limits. We can't guarantee interior line of sight for all corridors. We can't guarantee continuous camera access where your contractors deny police presence. We can't guarantee immediate lockout if power fails."

Thomas read the first line and swallowed hard. "This sounds like a liability waiver."

"It is documentation of reality."

Eleanor took the paper from him and scanned it fast, lips barely moving. "You are building a record to protect your office."

"I'm building a record so nobody can say we promised control we didn't have."

"You are asking me to certify vulnerability in writing twenty hours before a public event."

"Yes."

Eleanor held Lena's gaze a long moment. "Bring me a revised copy that removes speculative language and limits assertions to observable staffing constraints."

"No."

That surprised even Lena. It came out hard and simple, with no procedural packaging.

Eleanor blinked. Thomas stared at her like he was trying to decide whether to be relieved or offended.

Lena said, "This is the language. You can sign it or not sign it. Either way the weakness stays weak."

Eleanor put the paper down. "Very well. Leave it."

She turned toward the door. Thomas reached for the archive box, then paused and pushed it toward Lena instead.

"These are seating charts and vendor maps," he said. "You asked for full floor assignments." He pulled the limits sheet back, initialed beside the restriction block, and slid it to her. "And put this in your plan: no donor side-door entry for me. I come through public screening, I skip the receiving line, and I stay on one route to the stage."

Eleanor turned, surprise breaking through her courtroom calm. "Thomas."

"I'm not debating it," he said, not looking at her. "If I'm the target, I don't get shortcuts."

When they were gone, the room felt smaller, as if the walls had moved a foot inward while nobody watched.

Beau rubbed the bridge of his nose with his good hand. "You got your no."

"I got a documented no."

"That and twelve bucks gets you stale coffee." He nudged the archive box with his elbow. "You think she signs the limits sheet?"

Lena tracked from the closed door to the steaming lot outside. "She signs if she thinks unsigned paper can be subpoenaed anyway."

"You sound tired enough to bite through wire."

"I'm tired enough to taste copper in my sleep."

Beau stood. The chair legs screeched against concrete. "All right. Perimeter-first, then. We run the choke points, keep med access open, lock parking flow, and pretend we don't see the holes inside."

Lena nodded. The choice sat in her chest like wet sand. It had weight but no shape.

She waited until Beau stepped out to call Eleanor's cell. It rang three times.

"Judge Pike," Eleanor said.

"I need ten minutes off-record. Alone."

A pause. "At the foundation side entrance in thirty. No entourage."

The Pike Foundation Hall sat two miles from the old icehouse on roads that dipped where floodwater liked to collect. By the time Lena parked behind the venue, dusk had gone blue-gray and the levee wind had started up, carrying the metallic chatter of tent poles being unloaded.

A caterer smoked behind a stack of rental chairs, one heel balanced on the curb to keep her shoes out of the puddles. From inside came the clatter of flatware and a piano technician hitting the same middle key again and again, tuning by stubborn repetition.

Eleanor waited under the service awning with her arms crossed tight over her ribs. Without the courtroom lights and tailored posture, she seemed older than sixty-two, and smaller. She had changed into low black shoes and wrapped a silk scarf at her throat even though the air was still thick.

"You wanted off-record," she said.

"I want the part you don't say in that room." Lena stayed on the edge of the light. "Why keep this event when you know the risk isn't theater?"

Eleanor fixed her attention past Lena toward the loading dock where men in black polo shirts rolled flight cases over cracked concrete.

"Because cancellation does not erase exposure," she said. "It redistributes it. It tells every opportunist in this county that the Pike house is movable by threat."

"And because money moves tomorrow."

Eleanor gave a brittle half-smile. "Money always moves."

"Did you benefit from Gideon disbursements?"

The question sat between them. A forklift beeped in reverse. Somewhere nearby, a generator kicked on and coughed twice before settling into a steady thrum.

Eleanor did not answer for several seconds. When she did, her voice had lost some of its courtroom polish.

"Yes," she said. "I benefited."

Lena held still.

Eleanor went on, each sentence assembled as if she had to sign it before speaking. "Trust income funded this foundation's endowment growth. It funded my husband's cardiac rehab after his first event. It funded my campaign debt retirement. I did not draft the original instruments. I reviewed subsequent distributions and I allowed them. I told myself title questions were old injuries under active litigation. I told myself ongoing programs served people who had nothing to do with 1963."

Her mouth tightened. "Both statements were convenient."

Lena had expected denial, maybe anger, maybe the familiar patrician trick where a confession came packaged as civic burden. This sounded uglier and truer than that. It did not absolve anybody.

"Then cancel," Lena said. "Take the hit in daylight, before somebody else writes the night for you."

"No." Eleanor's eyes were wet but steady. "I will not hand the county to men who use murder as scheduling leverage."

"You think this is about dignity?"

"I think it is about precedent."

"Precedent got people buried without names."

Eleanor flinched as if Lena had touched her. Then she straightened again. "You asked for truth. That was truth. I will give you cooperation within law and contract. I will not give you cancellation."

Lena felt anger rise hot and then flatten into something colder. She thought of Ida's hands, knuckles swollen from arthritis, holding church ledgers where old names had survived only because a woman with bad joints wrote them down year after year. She thought of scholarship brochures printed on glossy stock with smiling faces over the same land stolen from their grandparents.

"Fine," Lena said. "Then walk the building with me. Now."

Eleanor hesitated. "I have a trustee call in ten minutes."

"Call from your phone while we walk. If your people refuse access while you're standing there, I put that in writing too."

The woman who had chaired a hundred hostile hearings briefly resembled a patient told there was one more scan before she could go home. "Very well," she said.

Inside, the hall smelled of citrus cleaner and damp drywall. Crew ladders leaned against cream-colored walls. Somebody had run extension cords across the corridor and taped them down with black gaffer strips that already curled at the edges. Lena could feel the vibration of bass tests through the soles of her shoes from the ballroom where a DJ checked levels.

Beau met them by the staff elevator, badge clipped to his belt and a cheap rain shell darkened at the shoulders. He held a yellow legal pad because his right wrist would not tolerate the tablet for long.

"Judge," he said. "You look thrilled."

"Detective Broussard," Eleanor replied. "I am adapting."

A broad man with close-cut gray hair stepped out from the corridor by the loading dock and intercepted them before Lena could speak. His black polo read DELTA EVENT RISK in white block letters. A gold wedding band hung from a chain at his neck instead of his finger; he touched it each time he paused, thumb rubbing the worn edge.

"Name's Reggie Kline," he said. "Site lead for interior security. We already sent your office the access memo. Law enforcement can stage at public ingress and exterior post points. Interior lane control belongs to my team."

Lena showed her badge. "I saw the memo. I'm here to amend it."

"You can request amendment through foundation counsel."

"I'm requesting it now."

Kline shook his head. "Without warrant or incident trigger, I can't give sworn officers free walk in restricted prep zones. My insurer will drop us by morning."

Beau said, "You worried about insurance while we're trying to keep bodies off your catering tables?"

"I'm worried about both, Detective." Kline's voice stayed level, but his left knee bounced once, fast. "Throughput and liability. I buried a cousin after a crowd crush in Baton Rouge because a cop panicked and sealed the wrong exit. I don't freelance crowd architecture at nineteen hundred because somebody feels jumpy."

That landed. He was not a cardboard gatekeeper; he was a man with his own dead.

Lena said, "Then walk me through your lane plan: cameras, choke points, staff loops. If I can name the holes, I can write conditions tonight."

Kline turned to Eleanor.

Eleanor said, "We are walking. Mr. Kline, show us the full interior route."

They moved as a loose line through the back of house. The service corridor was narrower than it had seemed on paper, concrete floor sweating under industrial coolers, one wall stacked with banquet chairs wrapped in plastic. Sound from the ballroom came muffled through fire doors: a laugh, a microphone squeal, a burst of horns from somebody's soundcheck playlist. Each cross-hall opening created a new blind angle. Lena counted them and felt the count rise like pulse.

"Camera one," Kline said, pointing up. "Dock door. Camera two at kitchen-pass junction. Camera three at the staff-elevator choke."

"No camera on the utility closet," Lena said.

"Dead by design. Electrical contractor flagged moisture on that run."

"No camera on the linen cage."

"Union privacy grievance last year. I lost that fight."

"No camera on north turn past floral staging."

"Historic wall there. Foundation counsel denied anchors and cable raceway."

Beau snorted. "Protect the wallpaper, risk the people."

Kline shot him a look but kept moving.

At the far end of the corridor, a steel push bar opened to a narrow exterior lane where dumpsters sat under sodium lights. The lane ran to the parking lot in twenty yards and to levee road in less than a minute if you knew which gate stuck.

Lena put her hand on the damp cinderblock and imagined someone using the darkness, the music, the confusion of staff traffic. A body could come in this way. A body could go out. She hated how easy the sentence felt.

"This is the breach route," she said quietly.

Beau wrote it on the pad in block letters, pressing hard enough to tear the paper. "Service lane, north turn, utility dead zone."

Eleanor stepped closer, scarf shifting in the draft from the door. "Can this lane be sealed during program hours?"

Kline shook his head. "Fire code says no hard seal. We can staff it."

"With who?"

"Two floaters rotating every twelve minutes."
